The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 86:1) quotes the Rambam's ruling, while the <i>Tur</i> and the Rama cite that of the Ramban.<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> also quotes Rashba who states that in the present age, we purchase eggs from gentiles without compunction, because non-kosher species are uncommon and the overwhelming proportion of eggs sold are from chickens or geese. This ruling is quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 86:2).</i> We do not suspect that they came from a fowl that was <i>trefe</i> or <i>nevelah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., that died without being ritually slaughtered; see Chapter 4, Halachah 1. We rule leniently, because it is very uncommon to have eggs from fowl in such a condition (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> And we do not purchase an [unshelled and] stirred egg from a gentile at all.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">For we fear that it came from an egg that was <i>trefe</i> (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i>
The distinguishing signs of fish eggs are the same as those for fowl. When both of the ends of an egg are rounded or both are pointed, it is non-kosher. If one end is pointed and the other rounded, he should inquire of the Jew who sells them.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">For the distinguishing signs themselves are not sufficient for the eggs to be considered kosher. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains that although <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 40a would appear to indicate that the distinguishing signs are sufficient, since <i>Chullin</i> 64a compares fish eggs to fowl eggs, we assume that all the laws that apply to one apply to the other.<br>In his <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 83:8), Rav Yosef Caro quotes the Rambam's ruling, but states that at present, it has become customary to buy any red fish eggs, even from gentiles. Black fish eggs, however, may not be purchased. In his <i>Beit Yosef</i> 81:12, he explains that the Rabbinic authorities of the earlier ages researched the matter and discovered that there are no common non-kosher fish that lay red eggs. See <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 83:27 who quotes other sources from which it is not clear whether or not this ruling was accepted in all communities.</i> If he tells him that he salted them<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., to preserve them, for it is common to bring fish eggs from distant places.</i> and removed them from a kosher species,<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Naming the species as in Halachah 18.</i> he may partake of them on the basis of his statements. If he tells him that they are kosher, he may not rely on him unless he is a person who has an established reputation for observance.
Similarly, we may not purchase cheese and pieces of fish that do not have distinguishing signs except from a Jew who has an established reputation for observance. In <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, at the time it was populated primarily by [observant] Jews,<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">As indicated by Chapter 11, Halachah 25, today, the same principles that apply in the Diaspora apply in Eretz Yisrael.</i> one could purchase these items from any Jew located there. And it is permitted to purchase milk from any Jew, anywhere.
When a person pickles non-kosher fish, the brine produced is forbidden. The brine produced by non-kosher locusts, by contrast, is permitted, because they do not possess any moisture.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">In the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 10:9), he states that they possess very little moisture. The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam's ruling and forbids brine from non-kosher locusts.</i> Accordingly, we do not purchase brine from gentiles unless there is a kosher fish floating in it.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">For then we assume that the brine came from this species of fish.</i> Even one fish is sufficient.
When a gentile brings a trough filled with open barrels of brine and there is a kosher fish in one of them, they are all permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">For we consider all the open barrels as a single entity and the one fish indicates that the entire quantity is acceptable. See <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>. This represents the Rambam's understanding of <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 39b-40a. The rulings of the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 83:6) quotes the Rambam's ruling, but also those of others who interpret that passage differently.</i> If they are closed, one opens one and finds a kosher fish and one opens a second and finds a kosher fish, they are all permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The intent is not that all the barrels are considered as a single entity, but that since two barrels are discovered to be kosher, we assume that the others are also kosher (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> [This applies] provided the head of the fish and its backbone are present so that it is recognizable that they are from a kosher species of fish.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., by looking at the head and the backbone, the person is able to recognize that the fish comes from a kosher species. One alone, i.e., either the head or the backbone, is not sufficient (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 40a).</i><br>For this reason, we do not purchased crushed, salted fish from gentiles which are called <i>terit terufah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 2:6), the Rambam explains that it was customary to crush and stir the fish until it produces a mixture like dough that was used as a dip.</i> If, however, the head and the backbone of a fish is recognizable, even though it is crushed, it is permitted to purchase it from a gentile.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if we discover the head and the backbone of one fish, we may purchase a larger quantity, because we do not expect that kosher fish and non-kosher fish were salted together (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i>
When a gentile brings a keg of pieces of evenly cut up fish and it is obvious that they are from one fish,<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 83:4 notes that the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 83:4) does not quote the Rambam's wording and explains that according to that source, it is not necessary for it to be obvious that they all come from one fish.<br>If it is not obvious that they come from one fish, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) rules that only the piece with scales is permitted.</i> they are all permitted if he finds scales on one of the pieces.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">For every fish that has scales will also have fins.</i>

Chapter 4

A person who partakes of an olive-sized portion of a domesticated animal, wild beast, or fowl which dies is liable for lashes, as [Deuteronomy 14:21] states: "Do not partake of any <i>nevelah</i>."<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 180) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 472) include this among the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> All animals that were not slaughtered in the appropriate manner are considered as if they died. In the Laws of<i> Shechitah</i>, we will explain which types of slaughter are appropriate and which are not.
Only animals from kosher species are forbidden as a <i>nevelah</i>, for they are the species that are fit to be ritually slaughtered and if they are slaughtered in a kosher manner, it is permitted to partake of them. [When,] by contrast, one partakes of [meat from] a non-kosher species, [since] ritual slaughter is of no consequence with regard to them, whether they are slaughtered in a kosher manner, whether they died in a natural manner, or whether one cut meat from a living animal and ate it, one does not receive lashes for partaking of a <i>nevelah</i> or partaking of <i>trefe</i> meat,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 10.</i> only because one ate the meat of a non-kosher animal.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">From <i>Chullin</i> 100b, it would appear that the rationale for this ruling is the general principle: "One prohibition does not fall upon another prohibition." Since the meat is already prohibited because it is from a non-kosher species, the prohibition of <i>nevela</i> does not apply to it. The Rambam's wording, however, is slightly different and may be alluding to a slightly different rationale.</i>
When a person eats an [entire] kosher fowl<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Which was not correctly slaughtered.</i> of any size, he is liable for lashes for partaking of a <i>nevelah</i>, even though he ate less than an olive-sized portion. [The rationale is that] he consumed it in its entirety.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This reflects a general concept with regard to the laws of kashrut. As stated in Chapter 2, Law 21, whenever "one eats an entire forbidden creature by itself, one is liable for lashes according to Scriptural Law even if it is smaller than a mustard seed."<br>The commentaries (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>, Rav Moshe HaCohen) question why the Rambam states that the person is liable for eating a <i>nevelah</i>. Seemingly, the prohibition he transgresses is eating a limb from a living animal (see Chapter 5). The <i>Meiri</i> explains the Rambam's position, stating that an entire creature cannot be considered as "a limb."</i> If he ate it after it died, it must be the size of an olive [for him to be liable].<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">For the conception of being liable for an entire creature even though it is not the size of an olive, applies only when that creature is inherently forbidden (<i>Meiri</i>). While alive, the bird is considered as an entire forbidden entity, like a non-kosher species. After its death, that distinction does not apply.</i> Even though it does not have an olive-sized portion of meat on it, since as a whole, it is the size of an olive, he is liable for [partaking of] a <i>nevelah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">For we include the bones and the sinews as well (<i>Chullin</i> 102b).</i>
When a person partakes of an olive-sized portion of a stillborn fetus<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The same laws apply if the fetus is born alive, but it is obvious that the birth is not viable. Even if the animal is slaughtered in the appropriate manner, we are forbidden to partake of it.</i> of a kosher animal, he is liable for lashes for partaking of a <i>nevelah</i>.<br>It is forbidden to partake of a newborn animal until the night of the eighth day [of its life].<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even if the animal is slaughtered properly, it is forbidden because it is possible that the birth is not viable. Although our Sages (<i>Chulin</i> 136a) spoke of the eighth day of an animal's life, their intent was the beginning of the eighth day (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> For whenever an animal has not lived for eight days, we consider it as stillborn, but lashes are not administered [for partaking] of it.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is not a definite matter, lashes are not administered (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> [Moreover,] if it is known that the animal was born after a full term period of gestation, i.e., nine months for a large domesticated animal and five months for a small domesticated animal, it is permitted on the day that it was born.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast, an animal may not be offered as a sacrifice until the eighth day of its life (Exodus 22:29; <i>Turei Zahav</i> 15:3).</i>
The placenta that is expelled together with the newborn is forbidden to be eaten. A person who eats it, however, is not liable,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he is not considered to have partaken of a <i>nevelah</i>.</i> because it is not [considered] meat.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">For as stated in Halachah 18, these are not considered meat, but rather comparable to an animal's wastes. For that reason, the Ra'avad (in his gloss to Chapter 5, Halachah 13) states that there is no prohibition against partaking of a placenta.</i>
When a person eats an olive-sized portion of a kosher<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., like the prohibition of <i>nevelah</i>, the prohibition of <i>trefe</i> does not apply with regard to non-kosher species.</i> domesticated animal, wild beast, or fowl that was mortally wounded is liable for lashes, as [Exodus 22:30] states: "Do not eat meat [from an animal that was] mortally wounded (<i>trefe</i>) in the field. Cast it to the dogs."<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even if it was slaughtered properly before it died, the meat is, nevertheless, forbidden, as stated in the following halachah. <i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 181) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 73) include this among the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i><br>The term <i>trefe</i> employed by the Torah refers to [an animal] mortally wounded by a wild beast, e.g., a lion, a tiger, or the like, or a fowl mortally wounded by a bird of prey, e.g., a hawk or the like.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">As the Rambam proceeds to explain in Halachah 8, the term <i>trefe</i> also applies in other situations. Nevertheless, there is an added dimension of severity to the laws applying to animals that are mortally wounded by beasts, as stated in <i>Hilchot Shechitah</i> 5:3 (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>; <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> We cannot say that the term <i>trefe</i> refers to an animal that was attacked and killed, for if it died, it is a <i>nevelah</i>. What difference does it make if it died naturally, was struck by a sword or died, or was battered by a lion and died? Thus [the term <i>trefe</i>] must refer to an instance when it was mortally wounded, but did not die.
If an animal that is mortally wounded is forbidden, shall we say that if a wolf or a lion comes and drags a kid by its foot, its tail, or its ear, and a man pursues [the beast] and saves [the kid], it will be forbidden, because it was attacked?<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The term <i>trefe</i> literally means "preyed upon." Our translation "mortally wounded" is the halachic definition as the Rambam proceeds to explain.</i> The Torah states: "Do not eat meat [from an animal that was] mortally wounded (<i>trefe</i>) in the field. Cast it to the dogs." [An animal is not considered <i>trefe</i>] unless it was brought to a state that its meat is fit [only] for the dogs. Thus we have learned that the term <i>trefe</i> employed by the Torah refers to [an animal] that was attacked by a wild beast and battered by it that has not died yet. Even if the person hurries and slaughters it before it dies, it is forbidden as<i> trefe</i>. For it is impossible that it will live after suffering such wounds.
Thus we have learned that the Torah forbade [an animal] that died, a<i> nevelah</i>, and it forbade one that was on the verge of death because of its wounds even though it has not died yet, i.e., a <i>trefe</i>.<br>Now we do not make a distinction with regard to an animal that has died regardless of whether it died naturally, it fell and died, it was strangled until it died, or it was attacked by a wild beast who killed it. Similarly, we do not make a distinction between an animal that is on the verge of death, regardless of whether it was attacked by an animal and battered, fell from the roof and broke the majority of its ribs,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following examples are specifically mentioned by the Rambam in <i>Hilchot Shechitah </i> 10:1, 9:8, 11:4, 6:1.</i> fell and crushed its limbs, it was shot with an arrow and its heart or lung pierced, it developed an illness that caused its heart or lung to be perforated, one broke the majority of its ribs, or the like. Since it is on the verge of death regardless of the cause, it is a <i>trefe</i>. [This applies] whether [its wound] was caused by flesh and blood or by God's hand.<br>If so, why does the Torah use the term <i>trefe</i>? For Scripture speaks with regard to prevalent situations.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">This is a general principle employed by our Sages with regard to the interpretation of the Torah's language. When it mentions a specific situation, it is not confining itself to the limited setting mentioned in the verse, but applies to other circumstances as well. Why is that situation mentioned? Because it is common.</i> [We are forced] to say this. If not, only an animal that was mortally wounded in the field would be forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">For only that is in the direct scope of the verse.</i> One that is mortally wounded in a courtyard would not be forbidden. Thus we learn that Scripture [is employing this example,] only because it speaks with regard to prevalent situations.
The intent of the verse is that [an animal] that is mortally wounded and will not live<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Longer than twelve months (<i>Hilchot Shechitah </i> 11:1).</i> because of these wounds is forbidden. On this basis, our Sages said:<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Chulin</i> 42a.</i> "This is the general principle: Whenever [an animal] in this condition will not live, it is <i>trefe</i>." In <i>Hilchot Shechitah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">From Chapter Five to Chapter Eleven. A concise list is found in Chapter 10, Halachah 9.</i> we will explain which conditions cause an animal to be deemed <i>trefe</i> and which do not cause it to be deemed <i>trefe</i>.
Similarly, when one cuts meat from a living kosher<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">For as mentioned above, the prohibition against partaking of a <i>trefe</i> applies only with regard to kosher animals.</i> animal, one receives lashes for partaking of a <i>trefe</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter Five, Halachot 2-3, where the Rambam distinguishes between this prohibition and the prohibition against partaking of a limb from a living animal. Note, however, <i>Hilchot Melachim</i> 9:10-11 where the Rambam includes eating the meat from an animal and eating a limb from an animal as a single prohibition for a gentile.</i> For this meat comes from an animal that has not been ritually slaughtered and has not died. [Hence it is comparable to a <i>trefe</i>.] What difference does it make to me if it was attacked by an animal or cut by a knife? And what difference does it make if [the animal] was [wounded] in its totality or only a portion of it was wounded?<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., just as we forbid the meat of an animal that was mortally wounded, we should forbid a portion of meat that was cut off with a knife.</i> For the verse states: "Do not eat meat [from an animal that was] <i>trefe</i> in the field." Since [a portion of] the animal was made meat in the field,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the meat was cut off from its natural place. See also Chapter 5, Halachah 9.</i> it is <i>trefe</i>.
When an animal is sick because it is weakened and is on the verge of death, it is permitted, because it did not suffer a wound in any one of the limbs and organs that will cause it to die. For the Torah forbade only those situations resembling an animal mortally wounded by a preying wild beast. In that situation, the animal wounded it with a blow that caused it to die.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">With this explanation, the Rambam is clarifying the distinction <i>Chulin</i> 37a makes between an animal which is <i>misukenet</i> (dangerously ill) and <i>trefe</i>. The <i>trefe</i> condition is a result of wound, while in the case of a <i>misukenet</i>, all of its limbs and organs are sound. Nevertheless, as stated in <i>Hilchot Trefot</i> 5:2, there are other physical conditions which render an animal <i>trefe</i> even if it has not been attacked by an animal. These conditions were communicated as halachot to Moses at Sinai.</i>
Although it is permitted, the great sages would not partake [of the meat] of an animal which people were hurrying to slaughter before it died.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> (based on <i>Chullin</i> 37b) interprets this as a gesture of pious conduct. Note, however, <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 17:8.</i> [This applies] even if it makes convulsive movements after being slaughtered.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Such a convulsive movement is a sign that it was alive at the time that it was slaughtered, as the Rambam continues to explain in the following halachah.</i> This is a matter that does not involve a prohibition. Nevertheless, whoever desires to accept this stringency upon himself is praiseworthy.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> quotes opinions that maintain that this leniency was granted only to alleviate the financial loss a Jewish owner of an animal would suffer. Therefore, meat from an animal belonging to a gentile which is in such a condition should not be eaten. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 116:7) quotes this ruling.</i>
When a person slaughters a domesticated animal, wild beast, or fowl and blood does not flow out from them, they are permitted. We do not say: perhaps they were dead already. Similarly, when one slaughters a healthy animal and it does not make convulsive movements, it is permitted.<br>Different [rules apply with regard to an animal that] is dangerously ill, i.e., one which cannot maintain itself when others cause it to stand it up.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">It must be able to stand up on its own when called or hit with a switch. If it is stood up by hand, it is still considered dangerously ill even if it is able to remain standing [Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 17:1); <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 17:1].</i> [It is placed in this category] even if it eats the food of healthy animals. If [such an animal] is slaughtered and does not make any convulsive movements at all, it is a <i>nevelah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., we assume that it died before the slaughter was completed (see <i>Chulin</i> 38a).</i> and one is liable for lashes [for partaking] of it. If it makes convulsive movements, it is permitted.<br>The convulsive movements must be made at the end of the slaughter. If they are made at the beginning, they are of no consequence.
What is meant by convulsive movements? For a small domesticated animal and for both a small and a large wild beast, the intent is that it extended its foreleg and returned it, extended its hind leg even though it did not return it, or merely bent its hind leg.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> and the <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 17:4 quote opinions that rule that if a small domesticated animal's foreleg was extended and it bent it, that is sufficient to render it acceptable.</i> This is considered a convulsive movement and [the animal] is permitted. If, however, it merely extended its foreleg and did not return it, it is forbidden. [This movement is] merely a result of the expiration of the soul.<br>With regard to a large domesticated animal, [more lenient laws apply]. If it either extended its foreleg or its hind leg without bending it or bent its foreleg or hind leg without extending it, it is considered as a convulsive movement and it is permitted. If, however, it neither extended or bent its foreleg or its hind leg at all, it is considered as a <i>nevelah</i>.<br>With regard to a fowl, even if it only blinked its eyelid<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Note the <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 17:5 which quotes different versions of <i>Chullin</i> 38b that state that a fowl must move its wing. Winking its eyes is not sufficient.</i> or swatted its tail, it is considered a convulsive movement.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> (and the <i>Turei Zahav</i> 17:4 and the <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 17:6) quote opinions that maintain that swishing its tail is also sufficient for an animal to be considered as having made a convulsive movement.</i>
When one slaughters an animal that is dangerously ill at night and does not know<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., because he cannot see. Needless to say, if the room is illuminated, this law does not apply.</i> whether or not it made convulsive movements, it is forbidden, because of the possibility that it is a <i>nevelah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., we follow the principle that when a doubt concerning a Scriptural prohibition is involved, we rule stringently.</i>
None of the substances prohibited by the Torah can be combined with each other [to reach the minimum measure for which one is liable for lashes] with the exception of the prohibitions that apply to a nazarite, as explained in that source.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Nazirut</i> 5:3, although there are separate prohibitions against eating raisins, grape peels, grape seeds, and partaking of wine, if one combined all these substances together, one is liable.</i> Therefore when a person takes a small amount of fat, a small amount of blood, a small amount of the meat of a non-kosher animal, a small amount of the meat of a<i> nevelah</i>, a small amount of the meat of a non-kosher fish, a small amount of the meat of a non-kosher fowl, or the like from other prohibited substances, although he collects an olive-sized portion from the entire mixture and partakes of it, he is not liable for lashes. He is bound by the laws that apply when one eats half the minimum measure [of a forbidden] substance.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">In which instance, the prohibition is Scriptural in origin, but lashes are not given (Chapter 3, Halachah 6).<br>The Rambam's statements in this and the following halachah touch on an issue of general significance. Rabbi Meir (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 66a) maintains that forbidden substances of different types can be combined together to make a person liable for lashes. The Sages differ and maintain that they cannot be combined, but instead are judged individually. If there is enough of the one substance to make one liable, he is liable. If not, he is exempt.<br>The principle stated in Halachot 18-19 is a correlory to these concepts. Since the forbidden substances are not combined with each other, but are instead considered as discrete entities, they help nullify each other, as explained there.</i>
All [types of] <i>nevelot</i> may be combined together. A<i> nevelah</i> may be combined with a <i>trefe</i>. All the non-kosher animals and wild beasts may be combined with each other. But the meat of a <i>nevelah</i> and the meat of a non-kosher animal may not be combined.<br>What is implied? When one takes [some meat] from a <i>nevelah</i> of an ox, some from the <i>nevelah</i> of a deer, some from the <i>nevelah</i> of a chicken and combined it so that he has an olive-sized portion of meat, he is liable for lashes if he eats it. Similarly, if he collected half of an olive-sized portion from the <i>nevelah</i> of a kosher animal and half of an olive-sized portion from a<i> trefe</i>, or half of an olive-sized portion from the meat of a <i>nevelah</i> and half from meat taken from a living kosher animal,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">For this is included in the prohibition against a <i>trefe</i>, as stated in Halachah 10.</i> he is liable if he eats it. Similarly, if he collects an olive-sized portion [by combining] the meat of a camel, a pig, and a hare,<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Although each one of these species is mentioned separately in the Torah, they are all included in the same prohibition.</i> he is liable if he eats it.<br>If, by contrast, he takes half of an olive-sized portion of a <i>nevelah</i> of an ox and half an olive-sized portion of a camel [an eats it], they are not combined.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad mentions that this point is the subject of a difference of opinion among the Sages of the Talmud, seemingly implying that the opinion which maintains that they should be combined should be followed. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> justifies the Rambam's position.</i> Similar principles apply in all analogous situations. Similarly, the meat of a non-kosher animal, fowl, or fish are not combined for they involve different prohibitions. For each one is forbidden by a separate negative commandment, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 2.</i> Nevertheless, all the forbidden species of fowl can be combined as may all the forbidden species of domesticated animals and wild beasts.<br>This is the general principle: Whenever substances are included in the same prohibition, they may be combined. [If they are included] in two [separate] prohibitions, they are not combined. The [only] exceptions are a <i>nevelah</i> and a <i>trefe</i>. [The rationale is that] a <i>trefe</i> is the beginning of [an animal] becoming a <i>nevelah</i>.
When a person eats the skin, the bones, the sinews,<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Note the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Chulin</i> 9:1) which interprets the Hebrew term <i>giddim</i> as also referring to veins, arteries, and nerves.</i> the horns, or the hoofs<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">This applies even if the portions eaten were soft and blood spurts from them.</i> of a <i>nevelah</i>, a <i>trefe</i>, or a non-kosher domesticated animal or wild beast, from the nails of a non-kosher fowl in the places where blood would spurt through when they are cut off, or from their placenta,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Halachah 5.</i> although this is forbidden, he is not liable. [The rationale is that] they are not fit to be eaten. They cannot be combined with meat [in the measure of] an olive-sized portion.
[Milk found in] the stomach of a <i>nevelah</i> and the stomach of a non-kosher animal<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam does not distinguish between milk that has coagulated and milk that is fluid. For even if it is fluid, it is already considered as a waste product. (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> is permitted, for it is like other waste products of the body. Therefore, it is permitted to use [milk found in] the stomach of an animal slaughtered by a gentile or the stomach of a non-kosher domesticated animal or wild beast to cause cheese to solidify.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., since the digestive juices from the animal's stomach have already mixed with this milk, it will be an effective catalyst to cause the large quantity of milk to curdle and harden into cheese. See also Chapter 3, Halachah 13, and Chapter 9, Halachah 15.<br>The Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 81:6) differs and quotes Rabbenu Tam's opinion states that liquid milk found in the stomach of a forbidden species is forbidden. Moreover, at the outset, one should not use even dried milk found in the stomach of a non-kosher animal as a catalyst because of the impression it will create. After the fact, it is permitted.</i> The skin of the stomach, by contrast, is like the other digestive organs and is forbidden.
The placenta of a donkey<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the glosses of Rashi and <i>Tosafot, Bechoros</i> 7b. The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam's ruling and maintains that this placenta is forbidden. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> justifies the Rambam's ruling.</i> is permitted to be eaten because it is like dung and urine which is permitted. There is skin which is considered like meat and one who partakes of an olive-sized portion is considered like one who eats an olive-sized portion of meat, provided one partakes of it when it is soft.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">And not processed so that it becomes hard like leather.</i>
The following [types of] skins are considered like meat: the skin of a human, the skin of a domesticated pig,<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">That of a wild boar, by contrast, is too tough to be eaten.</i> the skin of a camel's hump upon which a burden has never been loaded, [because] it has not reached the age [to serve as a beast] of burden, for then it is still soft, the skin of genital area, the skin that is below the tail, the skin of a fetus, the skin of the hedgehog, the chameleon, the lizard, the snail.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in the notes to Chapter 2, Halachah 7, the names of these species are a matter of debate.</i> When all of these skins are soft, they are considered like meat with regard to all matters, whether with regard to [liability<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">This addition is necessary, for there is a prohibition against partaking of any skin, as stated in Halachah 18.</i> for] the prohibition against partaking of them or with regard to the laws of ritual purity.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">For an olive-sized portion of the meat of a <i>nevelah</i> can convey ritual impurity, while a hide or a piece of leather that size does not.</i>
With regard to an ox condemned to be stoned,<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">For goring a human being. See <i>Hilchot Nizkei Mammon</i>, ch. 10, which explains the pertinent laws.</i> [Exodus 21:28] states: "Its meat shall not be eaten." Now, how could one think that it would be eaten after it was stoned to death, for it is a <i>nevelah</i>?<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">For it died without ritual slaughter.</i> Instead, the Torah is coming to teach you that once it has been sentenced to execution by stoning, it becomes forbidden; it becomes like a non-kosher animal. [Even] if one hurried and slaughtered it in an acceptable manner [before it was executed], it is forbidden to benefit from it.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 188) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 52) include this among the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.<br>Instead, the corpse of the executed animal should be buried (<i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 19:11).</i> If one eats an olive-sized portion of its meat, he is liable for lashes. And when it is executed by stoning, its [meat] should not be sold or given to the dogs or to a gentile, [as implied by the phrase]: "shall not be eaten."<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Chapter 8, Halachah 15, unless there is a teaching that states otherwise, this phrase implies both a prohibition against eating and a prohibition against deriving benefit.</i> It is permitted [to benefit from] the dung of an ox condemned to be stoned.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 5:2, the rationale is that the dung is considered of negligible importance with regard to the ox itself. As implied by Halachah 20, the dung of an animal is not considered as part of the animal itself, nor is it included in the prohibitions applying to it.</i><br>If it is discovered that [a condemned ox] is not liable to be stoned after it was sentenced, e.g., the witnesses who testified against it were disqualified, it may be sent out to pasture with the herd. If this was discovered after it was executed, it is permitted to benefit from [its meat].

Chapter 5

According to the Oral Tradition, we learnt<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Chullin</i> 102b.</i> that [the intent of] the Torah's statement "Do not partake of the soul together with the meat" [is to] forbid a limb cut off from a living animal.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 182) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 452) include this prohibition among the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> With regard to a limb cut off from a living animal, it was said to Noah [Genesis 9:4]: "But flesh, together with its soul, its blood, you may not eat."<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This prohibition is also one of the seven universal laws commanded to Noah and his descendants (<i>Hilchot Melachim</i> 8:10).</i><br>The prohibition against [partaking of] a limb from a living animal applies to kosher domesticated animals, wild beasts, and fowl, but not to non-kosher species.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Since the species is forbidden, no additional prohibitions apply.</i>
The term <i>ever</i> [translated as "limb"] applies both to a limb that has flesh, sinews, and bones, e.g., a hand or a foot, and to an organ that does not have a bone, e.g., the tongue, the testicles, the spleen, the kidneys, the heart, and the like.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Merkevat HaMishneh</i> notes that this represents a difference between this prohibition and the prohibition against ritual impurity stemming from a limb where the limb must possess a bone. He explains that there a bone is necessary, for the source of the impurity is that of a corpse, and a corpse possesses bones.</i> [There is, however, one difference.] When an organ does not possess a bone, the prohibition [against partaking of] a limb from a living animal applies whether one cut off the entire organ or only part of it.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam and does not accept this distinction. He maintains that partaking of part of an organ is also included in the prohibition against partaking of a <i>trefe</i>.</i> When, by contrast, a limb possesses a bone, a person is not liable [for violating the prohibition against] a limb from a living animal unless he separates it in its complete state, with its flesh, sinews, and bones. If, however, he only removes flesh from the living animal, he is liable for [the prohibition against partaking of] a <i>trefe</i> [animal] as explained,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 4, Halachah 10.</i> and not because of a limb from a living animal.
One is liable for lashes only for partaking of an olive-sized portion of a limb from a living animal. Even if one eats an entire limb or organ, if it is the size of an olive, one is liable;<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">We do not consider an organ or limb as a distinct creature and hold him liable, as he is liable for partaking of an ant, even if it is smaller than an olive. <i>Chulin</i> 102a explains that we require an olive-sized portion for the verse that states the prohibition speaks of "partak[ing] of the soul." The term partaking is appropriate only when one eats an olive-sized portion.</i> if not he is exempt.<br>If one cut off a olive-sized portion of flesh, sinews, and bones from the limb according to its natural form and ate it, one is liable, even if it possessed only the smallest amount of meat.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Since one did not alter its natural form, one is liable for the bones and sinews as well.</i> If, however, one separated a limb which he tore off from a living animal and detached the flesh from the sinews and the meat, he is not liable for lashes unless he eats an olive-sized portion of the meat alone. The bones and the sinews are not included in the olive-sized portion since he changed [the limb's] natural form.
When one divides this organ and eats it bit by bit, he is liable if there is an olive-sized portion of meat in what he ate.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Even though he did not detach the meat from the sinews and the bones, since he cut the limb and distorted its natural form, we consider only the meat and not the other elements of the limb.</i> If not, he is exempt. If he took an olive-sized portion of a limb with flesh, sinews, and bones according to its natural form and ate it, he is liable, even though it became divided inside his mouth before he swallows it.
When a person rips a limb from a living animal and causes it to become <i>trefe</i> when doing so, he is doubly liable for partaking of it: once for [partaking of] a limb from a living animal and once for [partaking of] a<i> trefe</i>. Both of these prohibitions take effect at the same time.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">This explains why the person is liable for the prohibition against partaking of a <i>trefe</i>, for seemingly, we should follow the principle "a prohibition does not fall on a substance which is already forbidden." This principle does not apply in this instance, for here, both prohibitions take effect at the same time. Hence, one does not take precedence over the other and the transgressor is liable for both. (See <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 17:8-10; Chapter 14, Halachah 18, for explanation regarding these principles.)</i> Similarly, if one rips fat from a living animal and partakes of it, he is doubly liable: for [partaking of] a limb from a living animal and for [partaking of] fat.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">He is liable for both prohibitions, because the prohibition against partaking of a limb from a living animal is of a wider scope (<i>issur mosif</i>; i.e., it is forbidden to gentiles as well as Jews) than the prohibition against partaking of forbidden fat (<i>Lechem Mishneh</i>).<br><i>Kin'at Eliyahu</i> asks why a limb taken from a forbidden species is not forbidden. Here also, the prohibition against taking a limb from a living animal is of a wider scope than that against partaking of a forbidden species.</i> If he rips fat from a<i> trefe</i> [animal], he is liable for [the violation of] three [negative commandments].<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The two mentioned in the previous clause and the prohibition against partaking of a <i>trefe</i>.<br>He is liable for all three prohibitions, because the prohibition against partaking of forbidden fat is of a wider scope than the prohibition against partaking of a <i>trefe</i> and the prohibition against partaking of limb of a living animal is of a wider scope than the other two (<i>ibid.</i>).</i>
[The following rules apply when] meat is disjoined from an animal and an organ is hanging from it. If it is impossible that this meat will again become a living part of the body, it is forbidden,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">From a comparison to the following halachah, it appears that this prohibition is of Scriptural origin.</i> but one is not liable for lashes for it. [This applies] even though it was not separated [from the animal] until after it was slaughtered. If the animal dies, we consider [the limb] as if it fell off while [the animal] was alive.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Chullin</i> 74a makes this distinction between an animal that dies naturally and one which is ritually slaughtered.</i> Therefore one receives lashes for [partaking] of it, because of the prohibition against [partaking of] a limb from a living animal. If, however, the limb could again become a living part of the body and the animal is ritually slaughtered, it is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">For it is considered as part of the animal.</i>
If one pulled an organ [from its natural position],<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Without detaching it.</i> crushed it, ground it, e.g., one crushed testicles or pulled them from their place [and then slaughtered the animal, the organ] is not forbidden according to Scriptural Law. [The rationale is] that it possesses a trace of life - as evidenced by the fact that it does not decay. Nevertheless, it is forbidden to partake of it as a result of a custom followed by the entire Jewish people from previous generations. For it resembles a limb separated from a living animal.
[The following laws apply when an animal's] bone was broken:<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Shechitah</i> 8:11-12, the Rambam mentions instances where a broken bone causes an animal to be designated as <i>trefe</i>. Here the Rambam is speaking of instances where the broken bone does not cause the animal to be <i>trefe</i> and the question involves merely the broken limb. May the meat from that limb be eaten or not?</i> If the flesh or the skin covers the majority of the thickness of the broken bone and the majority of the circumference of the fracture, it is permitted. If the bone emerged outside [the skin], the limb is forbidden. When the animal or the fowl is slaughtered, one should cut off [the limb] at the place where it is broken and discard it. The remainder of the limb is permitted.<br>We rule that [the limb] is forbidden until the flesh is healed [in all the following situations]: the bone broke, the flesh covers the bone, but that flesh was crushed or decayed like flesh which a doctor would remove, it is scattered in many different places,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., when one would calculate the entire amount of the flesh, it would be large enough to cover the majority of the bone. Nevertheless, it is not located in large sections, but is instead, made up of many small pieces.</i> there were many perforations within the flesh,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">None of these perforations, however, caused a decrease in the mass of the flesh.</i> the flesh was cracked or pierced like a ring, the flesh was rubbed off from above until only a [thin] peel remained, or the flesh decayed from below around the broken bone to the extent that the flesh surrounding the bone does not touch it.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Chullin</i> 76b mentions all these circumstances without reaching a final ruling regarding them. Hence we rule stringently.</i> If a person partook [of the limb] in any of these [circumstances], he is liable for stripes for rebellious conduct.
When a person inserts his hand into the inside of an animal, cuts off the spleen, one of the kidneys, or the like,<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., organs that do not cause an animal to be considered as a <i>trefe</i>.</i> but leaves [the severed organ] inside the animal, and then slaughters it, the pieces cut off are forbidden as organs from a living animal although they remained within the animal's womb. If, however, he cut away [a portion of] a fetus within the womb, but did not remove it, and then slaughtered [the mother], the pieces or limbs of the fetus are permitted because they did not emerge [outside the mother].<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">This distinction is made on the basis of <i>Chulin</i> 68a, 69a. The rationale is that the animal's organs are an integral part of it. On the basis of Deuteronomy 14:5, "You shall eat it," our Sages explain when it's whole, you may eat everything within it, but not when it is lacking. The fetus, however, is not considered an integral part of the animal. Hence, as long as its limbs have not emerged outside the womb, they are permitted with the slaughter of the mother.</i><br>When a fetus sticks its foreleg or hind leg out of the womb, that limb is forbidden forever, whether one cuts off [the limb] before he slaughters the mother or afterwards.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">From this halachah and Halachah 11, we see that there are two prohibitions involved: partaking of a limb considered <i>trefe</i> and partaking of a limb from a living animal. At times, one applies, and in other situations, the other applies.</i> Even if it returns the limb to the womb of the mother and afterwards, [the mother] was slaughtered or the fetus was born and lived for several years,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> and <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> interpret this as referring to a situation where the fetus was not born before the mother was ritually slaughtered (see also Halachah 12). Instead, the mother was slaughtered while the fetus was still in its womb. Afterwards, the fetus was taken out and it survived. If, however, the fetus is born before the mother is slaughtered, the leg which emerged is not forbidden. When the calf is slaughtered, all of its legs are permitted. There are authorities who differ whether this is the Rambam's intent. Most, however, agree that this ruling should be followed in practice [Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 14:2)].</i> that limb is forbidden as a <i>trefe</i>. [The rationale is that] all meat that emerged from its natural position is forbidden as flesh that was separated from a living animal.<br>[This is derived from the phrase (Exodus 22:30 :] "Meat [from an animal that was] mortally wounded (<i>trefe</i>) in the field." [Our Rabbis extrapolated:] When meat comes out to a place that is like a field for it,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it is not its natural place.</i> it becomes <i>trefe</i>, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 4, Halachah 10.</i>
[When the fetus] sticks out a portion of a limb and a portion remains within, even if it is only the minority of it, the portion which emerged is forbidden and that which remained within is permitted.<br>If he cuts off the portion of the limb that emerged after it was returned within the animal and the animal was slaughtered, only that portion is forbidden, the remainder of the limb is permitted. If he did not return it to the womb and it remained outside and he cut it off there, the place where he cut it off - i.e., the place on the limb open to the air after the limb was cut off - is forbidden. He must afterwards cut off this portion as well. [This applies] whether he [originally] cut off the portion of the limb before [the mother] was slaughtered or afterwards.
Whenever a limb emerges and is cut off before the animal is slaughtered while it is outside, it is considered as a limb from a living animal<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">As evident from a comparison to Halachah 9, the prohibition against partaking of a <i>trefe</i> applies only when the limb was cut off following the ritual slaughter of the mother.</i> and one is worthy of lashes for partaking of it. [This applies] even if the fetus dies before [the mother] is slaughtered.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">For the ruling is dependent on the mother's condition, not that of the fetus. Even if the fetus dies, it is permitted to partake of it after the slaughter of the mother (see <i>Tosafos, Chullin</i> 72a).</i> If it is cut off after ritual slaughter, one who partakes of it is not liable for lashes,<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he is not liable for lashes for transgressing the prohibition against partaking of a limb of a living animal. As reflected by Halachah 9, he violates the prohibition against partaking of a <i>trefe</i>. The Ra'avad maintains that he is liable for lashes for this violation. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> maintains that this situation is comparable to a maimed limb as described in Halachah 6 in which instance a Scriptural prohibition is involved, but one is not liable for lashes.</i> even if it dies. If [the mother] dies and then one cuts off this limb, one who partakes of it is liable for lashes for the prohibition against partaking of a limb from a living animal.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">And not for either the prohibition against partaking of a <i>trefe</i> or a <i>nevelah</i>. The death of the mother causes the limb to be considered as if it fell off during the animal's lifetime. See <i>Hilchot Sha'ar Avot HaTumah</i> 2:9 which explains the parallels that apply with regard to the laws of ritual impurity.</i>
[The following rule applies when] a fetus sticks out a limb and that limb becomes forbidden and then the fetus is born.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> states that this also refers to a situation where the fetus was born after the mother was slaughtered.</i> If it is female, we are forbidden to drink its milk because of an unresolved halachic question.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Chullin</i> 69a asks whether this milk can be compared to milk from a kosher animal or not. The distinction is that a kosher animal will become permitted if it is slaughtered in the ritual manner and this limb will never become permitted.</i> For the milk comes from all of the animal's limbs and it has a limb which is forbidden. Hence, it is comparable to milk from a <i>trefe</i> animal that becomes mixed with milk from a kosher animal.
When a person slaughters a kosher animal that is pregnant and discovers a fetus - whether live or dead - within it, the fetus is permitted to be eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">With regard to a live fetus, see the following halachah. With regard to a dead fetus, the Rambam is emphasizing that it is not considered as a separate entity (in which case it would be forbidden as a <i>nevelah</i>), but instead as one of the limbs of the mother.</i> Even the placenta is permitted to be eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 4, Halachah 5, states that a placenta that is expelled together with the newborn is forbidden to be eaten. In this instance, however, since the placenta has not been expelled, it is still considered part of the mother's body and permitted.</i><br>[The following rules apply if] a portion of the placenta emerged and then one slaughtered the mother. If the placenta was attached to the fetus, the portion which emerged is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Like the limb of a fetus that emerged before ritual slaughter (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).<br>The Ra'avad states that the placenta is comparable to an animal's waste products and therefore is not forbidden at all. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> justifies the Rambam's ruling.</i> and the remainder is permitted. If it is not attached to the fetus, it is forbidden in its entirety, for perhaps the fetus that was in this placenta disappeared<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Which was not permitted because of the slaughter of the mother.</i> and maybe the placenta of the fetus that is found in the womb disappeared. Needless to say, if a fetus is not found in the womb at all, the placenta is forbidden in its entirety.
If one finds a living fetus [in the womb of a slaughtered animal] - even though it has been carried for nine months,<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the period of gestation was full term.</i> and it is possible that it will live, it does not require ritual slaughter.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">And can be killed in any manner.</i> Instead, it is acceptable because of the slaughter of its mother. If it steps on the ground, it requires ritual slaughter.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Because of the impression that might be created (<i>Chullin</i> 75b).</i>
If a person ripped open an animal<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Without slaughtering it according to Torah law.</i> or slaughtered an animal that was <i>trefe</i> and found a live fetus that had been carried for nine months, [that fetus] must be ritually slaughtered to be permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 13:3) states that at present, we do not permit any fetus found in the womb of a <i>trefe</i> even if it was ritually slaughtered.</i> The slaughter of its mother is not effective.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Since the slaughter of the mother does not cause the mother to be permitted, it is not effective with regard to the fetus.</i><br>If the period [of gestation] was not completed, it is forbidden even though it is alive in the womb of the <i>trefe</i> animal. [The rationale is that] it is considered as one of the mother's limbs.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Since the period of gestation has not been completed, it is not considered as an independent entity. Even if it is taken from the womb and lives for a brief time, ritual slaughter does not cause it to be permitted.</i> Whenever an animal thrust its head [out of the womb] and then returned it and [only] afterwards its mother was slaughtered, the slaughter of its mother has no bearing on it, it is considered as if it was born and it must be ritual slaughtered [to be permitted].

Chapter 6

When a person partakes of an olive-sized<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Although blood is a liquid, the Rambam mentions an olive-sized portion, i.e., a measure of mass, rather than a fourth of a <i>lug</i>, a liquid measure. It is possible to explain that since the Torah uses the word "eat" while stating the prohibition, the intent is the same measure that applies with regard to other prohibitions involving "eating," an olive-sized portion.</i> portion of blood intentionally, he is liable for <i>karet</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The soul is cut off in this world (i.e., the person dies prematurely) and in the world to come (<i>Hilchot Teshuvah</i> 8:1). Whenever a person is liable for <i>karet</i>, he is punished by lashes if he was warned before committing the transgression. If lashed, he is absolved from <i>karet</i>.<br><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 184) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 148) include this prohibition among the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> If he does so inadvertently, he is liable to bring a fixed sin-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This term is used to differentiate this offering from a guilt offering whose value is adjusted according to the person's means.</i><br>It is explicitly stated in the Torah that he is liable for partaking of blood from all domesticated animals, wild beasts, and fowl alone. This applies whether they are from a non-kosher or kosher species,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the prohibition against partaking of <i>cheilev</i>, forbidden fat, which applies only with regard to kosher species of domesticated animals.</i> as [Leviticus 7:26] states: "You may not partake of any blood from a fowl or an animal in all your dwellings." A wild beast is considered as an animal as [Deuteronomy 14:4-5] states: "These are the animals that you may eat: an ox... a gazelle and a deer...."<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the verse uses the term <i>behemah</i> which more specifically refers to domesticated animals and mentions both domesticated animals and wild beasts.</i><br>One is not, [by contrast,] liable for transgressing of the prohibition against partaking of blood<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">One may, however, be liable for another prohibition as the Rambam continues to explain.</i> for partaking of the blood of fish, locusts, creeping animals, teeming animals, or humans. Therefore it is permitted to partake of the blood of kosher fish and locusts. Even if one collects it in a container and drinks it, it is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad and the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> note that <i>Keritot</i> 21a states that it is permitted to partake of fish blood that has been collected only when fish scales are placed in it. Otherwise, it is forbidden because of the impression that may be created. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 66:9) quotes this view.</i> The blood of non-kosher fish and locusts is forbidden because it comes from their bodies like the milk of a non-kosher animal.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">As apparent from Chapter 3, Halachot 1 and 6, although this prohibition is of Scriptural origin, it is not punishable by lashes. See also Chapter 3, Halachah 22.</i> The blood of creeping animals is comparable to their bodies, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 2, Halachot 9-10.</i>
The blood of a human is forbidden according to Rabbinic law if it departed [from the person's body]. One is liable for stripes for rebellious conduct for [partaking] of it. When, by contrast, one's teeth bleed, he may swallow it; he need not hold himself back. If one bit into bread and found blood upon it,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the same blood that he is permitted to swallow.</i> he must scrape away the blood before partaking of it, for the blood has departed [from the body].
One is liable for <i>karet</i> only for blood that flows out [from the animal] when it is slaughtered, killed, or decapitated as long as it is tinted red, blood that is collected within the heart,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">See the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Keritot</i> 5:1) where he states that this is the blood of fundamental importance. See also Halachot 5-6.</i> and blood that is let, i.e., blood that flows forcefully [from the body]. One is not, however, liable for blood that drips at the beginning of bloodletting before it begins to flow forcefully or blood that drips at the ending of bloodletting when the bleeding begins to cease. It is like "blood within the limbs." [The reason for the distinction is that] blood that flows forcefully is bleeding through which the soul may expire.
One is not liable for <i>karet</i> for concentrated blood<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., blood that flows slowly after the majority of the animal's blood has already been discharged. See the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>loc. cit.</i>).</i> or the blood within the limbs, i.e., the blood of the spleen, the kidneys, the testicles, the blood that collects in the heart at the time the animal is slaughtered, and the blood found in the liver.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">There is a difference of opinion among the Rabbis if the prohibition against the blood of the liver is Scriptural or Rabbinic in origin. See the gloss of Rav Moshe HaCohen.</i> A person who partakes of an olive-sized portion of it, however, is liable for lashes, as it is written: "You may not partake of any blood."<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The term "blood within the limbs" appears to refer to blood that is absorbed within the meat and organs of the animal. As indicated later in this chapter and as stated in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 67:1, this blood is forbidden only when it emerges from the meat or moves from place to place within the meat. See the notes to Halachah 12.</i> With regard to one's liability for <i>karet</i> [Leviticus 17:11] states: "For the soul of the flesh is in the blood." [Implied is that] one is liable for <i>karet</i> only for blood that causes the soul to expire.
When a fetus is found in an animal's womb, its blood is like the blood of an animal that has been born.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">There is a distinction between the blood of a fetus and its fat (which is permitted in certain circumstances, see Chapter 7, Halachah 3). The rationale is that the verse forbidding blood prohibits "any blood" (<i>Lechem Mishneh</i>).</i> Therefore one is liable for the blood that is collected in its heart.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Halachah 3.</i> The remainder of its blood, by contrast, is considered as the blood of the limbs.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">According to the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>, even if one slaughters the fetus after removing it from its mother's womb, he is not liable for partaking of its blood.</i>
Whether one [desires to] roast or cook a heart, one must cut it open, remove its blood, and then salt it.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Halachah 12, the Rambam maintains that even when one roasts meat, he must salt it first. As stated in the notes to that halachah, there are other Rabbis who differ with that point and require salting only when one cooks meat. See also the Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 72:1) which quotes certain authorities that forbid eating a cooked heart, even if it was cut open and salted.</i> If one cooks a heart without cutting it open, one may cut it open after it was cooked. It is then permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Pesachim</i> 74b states that the meat of the heart is smooth and hard and will not absorb the blood. Other substances that are cooked with it, however, are forbidden (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i> in the name of the Rashba). The Ra'avad and others differ with the Rambam and consider a heart cooked with its blood as forbidden. This is the view cited by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 72:2).</i> If one did not cut it open and partook of it, one is not liable for <i>karet</i>.<br>When does the above apply? With regard to the heart of a fowl, because it does not contain an olive-sized portion of blood.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The heart of a fowl is not large enough for there to be an olive-sized portion of blood absorbed within its meat before slaughter. And it is only that blood for which one is liable for <i>karet</i>. If an olive-sized portion of blood collects there when the animal is slaughtered, one is liable for lashes.</i> If, by contrast, one [partakes] of the heart of an animal, one is liable for <i>karet</i>. For there is an olive-sized portion of blood within the heart and therefore one is liable for <i>karet</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">This indicates that according to the Rambam, even when one cooks blood, one is liable for <i>kereit</i> for partaking of it (<i>Lechem Mishneh</i>). Other authorities differ and maintain that if blood has been cooked or salted, one is not liable according to Scriptural Law (<i>Siftei Cohen</i> 87:15).</i>
If one cuts open the liver<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 73:1) states: "The liver has an abundance of blood. Therefore at the outset, it is not a sufficient measure to prepare it for cooking by salting."<br>The liver must be cut open thoroughly so that the blood contained in the blood vessels inside of it will flow out. Afterwards, placing it in vinegar or hot water causes the blood to be sealed in its place and not to flow into other portions of the liver. It is only blood that flows from place to place within the meat itself that is forbidden (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> and casts it into vinegar or boiling water until it turns white, it is permitted to cook it afterwards.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Implied is that if one desires to roast it, there is no difficulty (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).<br>Many opinions maintain that we are not knowledgeable with regard to the process of casting a liver in vinegar or boiling water in the present age and should not rely on this practice. This view is quoted in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 73:2).</i> It has already become universal Jewish custom to singe it over a fire<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">See the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 10:11) where the Rambam mentions ordinary roasting and not singeing for the liver to be permitted.</i> and then cook it. [This applies] whether one cooks it alone or one cooks it with other meat.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 73:1) states that it must be roasted to the extent that it is fit to be eaten. The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 73:2 explains that this means that it must be roasted at least half the extent to which one would normally roast it.</i><br>Similarly, it is a common custom that the brains are not cooked nor roasted until they are singed over a fire.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> states that this custom is not as widespread as the custom of singeing the liver. Instead, he writes that it is customary to cut open the membrane surrounding it and then to salt it thoroughly. See the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 71:3).</i>
When a liver was cooked without being singed over a fire or cast into vinegar or hot water, the pot in which it was cooked is forbidden entirely: the liver and everything cooked with it.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 73:2) follows the opinion that the liver itself is permitted if it is cooked after being salted, though everything cooked with it is forbidden. The Rama, however, states that the Rambam's view should be followed.</i><br>It is permitted to roast a liver together with other meat on one spit, provided the liver is positioned below [the other meat].<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">For then the blood will not flow from the liver to the other meat. Hence, even at the outset, this is permitted. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 73:4) states that this ruling applies with regard to the ovens that existed in the Talmudic era. In the present era, however, it is common to turn the spit upside down. Hence, one should not roast the liver together with other meat.</i> If one transgressed and roasted it while it was positioned above the meat, [after the fact,] one may eat it.
It is permitted to cook a spleen<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">That has been salted [<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 74:1)].</i> together with meat, because it is not blood, but meat that resembles blood.<br>When one breaks the neck of an animal before its soul expires, the blood is absorbed into the limbs.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the animal suffers internal bleeding when its neck is broken. Since it is in the midst of expiring, it does not have the potential to expel this blood from its system. Instead, it becomes absorbed in its meat [<i>Tur</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 67)].</i> It is forbidden to eat raw meat from it even if one causes the blood to be sealed.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">By casting it into vinegar or boiling water.<br>To explain the difficulty the Rambam is addressing: There is a certain amount of blood absorbed in the meat of an animal. It is forbidden to partake of blood that has moved from place to place within an animal's body and cooking meat will certainly cause such movement. To avoid this difficulty, we salt meat, for this removes the blood. Casting meat in vinegar or boiling water does not remove blood, but instead causes it to be sealed in its place without moving even when the meat is cooked. Hence, according to the Rambam, this process is effective for ordinary meat. It is not, however, effective in this instance, for the internal bleeding that resulted from the breakage of the neck leaves blood that is not in its natural place (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, see also his gloss to Halachah 12).</i> What should be done? One should cut open<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation follows the gloss of the <i>Lechem Mishneh</i> who states that one should cut open the meat to allow its blood to flow out. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> states that it is unnecessary to cut open the meat and his view is accepted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 67:3). The Rama, however, states that at the outset, one should be stringent.</i> the piece and salt it thoroughly and afterwards, cook it or roast it.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">It is then permitted, because the salting removes all the blood.</i><br>We have already explained<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 4, Halachah 13. In that chapter, the emphasis was that the animal was not dead when slaughtered. Here the Rambam is restating the law to emphasize that we do not fear that the blood became absorbed within the meat and will not be released through salting.</i> that when a person slaughters a domesticated animal, wild beast, or fowl and no blood emerges, they are permitted.
Meat does not release [all] the blood it contains unless it is salted thoroughly and washed thoroughly. What should one do? One should wash the meat first<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Among the reasons given for this initial washing are:<br>a) it removes the blood on the external surface of the meat;<br>b) it softens the meat and enables the blood inside to flow out more easily;<br>c) it enables the salt to adhere well to the surface of the meat.</i> and afterwards, salt it thoroughly. One should leave it in the salt for the time it takes to walk a <i>mil</i><sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>mil</i> is a Talmudic measure equivalent to approximately a kilometer. According to many Rabbinic opinions, it takes 18 minutes to walk a <i>mil</i>. [This view is cited in the present context by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 69:6).] The Rambam, however, follows a more stringent view [see his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Pesachim</i> 3:2)] and requires 24 minutes.<br>The Rama states that these measures are acceptable only after the fact or when there is an urgent need to prepare meat quickly. Otherwise, meat should be allowed to soak in water for at least half an hour.</i> and then wash it thoroughly, [continuing] until clean water emerges.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">This washing removes the salt and blood from the meat. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 69:7) states that the meat should be washed twice and the Rama requires a third washing.</i> Immediately afterwards, one should cast it into boiling water - warm water is not [sufficient] - so that it will become white immediately and [no further] blood will be released.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">This follows the Rambam's view that we are knowledgeable in the process of casting meat into hot water to seal it in its place. (As mentioned above, there are many who maintain that we lack that knowledge.) Moreover, in this instance, once the meat has been salted, there is no need for this measure, because all its blood has been released. For this reason, the Ra'avad and the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> object to the Rambam's ruling. It is, however, mentioned by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 69:19).</i>
When we salt meat, we salt it only in a utensil that has holes.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">So that the blood will drain out and not be reabsorbed by the meat. If one salted meat in utensil that did not have holes, all the meat lying in the brine is forbidden. Moreover, the outer surface of the meat above the brine becomes forbidden [<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 69:18)]. The Rama maintains that the entire piece of meat becomes forbidden.</i><br>We salt it only with thick salt that resembles coarse sand. [The rationale is that] salt that is thin like flour will be absorbed by the meat and will not extract the blood.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 69:3) states that one should be careful not to use salt with overly large crystals for they will not adhere to the meat. The Rama adds that if one has only thin salt, it is permitted to salt meat using it.</i><br>One must shake the salt from the meat before washing it.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">For the water will stop the meat from releasing blood. Afterwards, the salt may cause the blood on the surface to be reabsorbed into the meat (<i>Siftei Cohen</i> 69:27).</i>
All of the above procedures apply with regard to meat that one must cook. For roasting, by contrast, one may salt the meat and roast it immediately.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">When meat is cooked, the blood will enter the pot in which it is being cooked and cause the meat and any other substances to become forbidden. When it is being roasted, the blood will flow down from the spit without being absorbed.<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> states that the Rambam's words imply that he maintains that one must salt meat before roasting it. There are other Rabbinic opinions that do not accept that approach. (They are favored by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 76:1).<br>The Rashba emphasizes that if one salts meat and does not roast it immediately, he should wash off the salt and the blood. Otherwise, the blood may become reabsorbed. The Rama rules that one should wash meat and salt it slightly before roasting it and should roast it directly after salting it.</i><br>When a person desires to eat raw meat, he should salt it thoroughly<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's statements have attracted the attention of the commentaries, for they appear to contradict his approach in Halachah 9 and in the latter clause of this halachah. To explain: From Halachah 9, it would appear that it is forbidden to eat raw meat that is not salted only when there is internal bleeding while the animal is being slaughtered. For although the meat contains blood, that blood is forbidden only when it moves from one part of the meat to another while cooking. If one eats the meat raw, such a transfer will not take place.<br>Similarly, the latter clause of this halachah permits meat when it is cast into vinegar because the blood becomes sealed in its place. Implied is that the blood itself is permitted.<br>This clause, by contrast, states that one must salt the meat to remove the blood even when one eats the meat without cooking it. Implied is that the blood is forbidden even though it has not moved from place to place within the meat. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> resolves the contradiction, explaining that since the blood is fit to move from place to place, it is forbidden. Hence, the latter clause which speaks about blood that is sealed in its place does not represent a contradiction.
