Similarly, this interpretation allows Halachah 9 to be understood in a manner that does not produce a contradiction.<br>It must be emphasized that the Ra'avad and many other authorities object to the Rambam's ruling and maintain that as long as the blood has not actually moved from place to place, it is not forbidden. Therefore it is permitted to partake of raw meat without salting it. It must, however, be washed thoroughly to remove all blood on its surface. This view is cited by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 67:2).</i> and wash it thoroughly. If he causes the blood to be sealed [by casting the meat into] vinegar, it is permitted to eat the meat while raw.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned previously, in the Ashkenazic community (and also among many Sefardim), the custom of sealing blood by casting meat into vinegar is no longer practice. See Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 67:6).</i> And it is permitted to drink the vinegar which sealed it, for vinegar does not extract blood.
Vinegar in which meat was sealed should not be used to seal meat a second time.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">For its power has been weakened (Rashi, <i>Chulin</i> 33a).</i> When a piece of meat turns red within vinegar,<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad objects to the Rambam's ruling, maintaining that it is based on an improper interpretation of <i>Chulin</i> 93b. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 67:4-5 appears to think that the interpretations are not mutually exclusive, for it accepts both of them.</i> it and the vinegar are forbidden. [It can be permitted by] salting it thoroughly and roasting it.<br>When meat turns red,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">An indication that it has absorbed additional blood.</i> similarly, the testicles of an animal or beast and their membranes,<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">For they also contain large quantities of blood. See <i>Chulin</i> 93a.</i> and similarly the neck which contains large blood vessels that are filled with blood, it is permitted to cook them if they are cut open and salted as required. If one did not cut them open and instead roasted them on a spit, they are permitted if he roasted the neck with its opening facing downward or he roasted all of them on the coals themselves.
[The following rules apply when one] roasts the head of an animal in an oven or a furnace. If one hangs the head with the opening to its neck<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Literally, the place where the animal was slaughtered.</i> hanging downward, it is permitted, for the blood will emerge and flow outward.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 68:1) states that, as an initial and preferred measure, it is customary to be stringent and not to roast the head while it is whole at all, even if the opening to the neck is positioned downward.</i> If the opening to the neck is positioned to the side, the brain is forbidden, because the blood collects in it.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, a hole is made in the skull and its membranes so that the blood can drain off, the brain is permitted [Rashba, as quoted by the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>; <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 68:1)].</i> The remainder of the meat on the external surface of the bones is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The brain must, however, be removed from the skull, before the skull is cooked (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i> in the name of the Ramban).</i><br>Should he [roast it] with its nose positioned downward, if he places a straw or a reed in its nose so that it will remain open and the blood can flow out through it, [the brain] is permitted. If not, it is forbidden.
One should not place a utensil beneath meat<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">That has not been salted and left to let the blood drain off.</i> that is being roasted to collect the juice [dripping from it] until no red color remains in the juice. What should be done?<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">For if one waits until there is no trace of the blood, the fat that one wishes to collect will also have drained off.</i> One places a small amount of salt<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">One should use only a small amount of salt. If one uses a large amount, the blood will become mixed with the fat (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> in the utensil and leaves the utensil there until the meat roasts. He then removes the fat resting on top. The liquid below the fat is forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Because it is mixed with blood. Since the fat does not mix with the other liquids but instead floats above them, it remains a distinct entity and is permitted.<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> writes that there are opinions that we are not familiar with the details of this process. Hence one should not rely on this leniency. This approach is followed by the <i>Tur</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 76:6) which state that one should not place a utensil under the meat until the meat is roasted to the extent that it can be eaten.</i>
When roasted meat is sliced over a piece of bread, it is permitted to eat the bread, for [the liquid] which exudes is only fat.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., once the meat is roasted to the extent that it is fit to be eaten, we assume that all the forbidden blood has drained off.</i><br>When fish and fowl are salted together, even in a utensil with holes, the fish are forbidden. [The rationale is that] the fish is soft and will absorb the blood which is being exuded by the fowl.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Generally, when pieces of meat are being salted together and the utensil has holes so that the blood can run off, the meat is permitted even if the blood from other meat flows over it. The rationale is that since it is expelling its own blood and/or other juices, it will not absorb blood. Fish, however, will expel its blood and juices far faster than meat or fowl and will complete that process before the meat completes expelling its blood. Hence, we fear that it will absorb the blood from the meat or fowl (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).<br>All that is forbidden is the external surface of the fish (<i>kedai kelipah</i>). Once that is cut off, the remainder of the fish is permitted [<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 70:1)]. The Rama rules that all the fish are forbidden. This stringency applies when the fishes' scales have been removed. If they have not been removed, the fish are permitted.</i> Needless to say, [this law applies] if one salted fish together with the meat of an animal or beast.
When one leaves fowl whole, stuffs their cavity with meat and eggs, and cooks them, they are forbidden, for the blood flows into them.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., into the stuffings. Afterwards, the blood will become reabsorbed into the fowl itself and cause it to become forbidden.</i> This applies even if one salted them thoroughly,<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> interprets the Rambam's ruling as being dependent on his ruling in Halachah 10 that after being salted, meat must be placed in hot water. In this instance, the fowl's stuffing prevents the boiling water from having the desired effect on the fowl.<br>Alternatively, the Rambam's ruling can be understood according to the statements of the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 77:1) who rules that initially one must salt both sides of the fowl. If the fowl was already stuffed, salting just the exterior is not sufficient, because it will not effect the blood on the inside. See also the Rama who rules that at the outset, fowl must be salted properly and any meat placed within it must be salted properly. Only in such a situation should one cook a fowl with stuffing.</i> and even if the meat inside them was cooked or roasted. If one roasted [the fowl], they are permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">For the fire will cause all the blood to drain out.</i> [This applies] even if the meat inside them was raw and even if their opening was pointed upward.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Even in such a position, the power of the fire will cause the blood to drain downward.</i>
When one filled intestines [that were not salted] with roasted or cooked meat in this manner or with eggs and cooked them or roasted them, they are permitted. [The rationale is that] we do not presume that there is blood in the intestines.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Hence salting is not required.</i> The <i>Geonim</i> ruled in this manner.
[The following rules apply when] one coated fowl<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> interprets this halachah as referring to fowl that were salted, but were not placed in water after the salting. According to the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 78:1), it applies to fowl that were not salted. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> continues, stating that according to the present custom, one should not coat meat that is being roasted unless it has been salted and washed first.</i> with flour and roasted them, whether whole or cut in portions. If they were coated with coarse flour, one may partake of the coating, even if it became reddish. [The rationale is that] coarse flour will crumble<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it will not stick thoroughly to the sides of the fowl. Thus there will be space for the blood to drain through.</i> and the blood will flow outward. When they are coated with wheat flour that was moistened [before being ground],<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">In which instance, the flour is very thin and hence, clings tightly to the fowl.</i> it is permitted to eat from the coating if it is white like silver. Otherwise, it is forbidden. If they were coated with other flours, they are forbidden if they turn red. Otherwise,<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Even if they are not white like silver.</i> they are permitted.
It is forbidden to use a knife that was used for ritual slaughter to cut hot meat<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's ruling is based on the following points. There is a difference of opinion among our Sages (<i>Chullin</i> 8b) whether the portion of the animal's neck where it is slaughtered is considered as "hot" at the time of slaughter, in which instance, when the animal is slaughtered some of its blood would be absorbed into the knife. Or it is not hot, in which instance, there is no such absorption.<br>From the Rambam's ruling (here and in Chapter 17, Halachah 7), it appears that he maintains that the animal's neck is not "hot." Nevertheless, he requires certain safeguards in consideration of the other views.<br>This issue is a matter of concern only when the meat being cut itself is hot. For otherwise, the blood absorbed in the knife will not be transferred to it.<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> mentions that there is another opinion which maintains that even though the animal's neck is cold, the pressure of cutting causes the knife to absorb some blood. See the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 10:2) and commentaries.</i> unless the knife was exposed to fire until it turned white, sharpened in a sharpener or inserted into hard earth ten times. [After the fact,] if one cut hot meat with it, it is permitted.<br>Similarly, one should not cut radishes or other sharp foods<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Since these foods are sharp, they produce an effect similar to actual heat and have the potential to affect forbidden foods absorbed in a knife. Therefore safeguards should be taken.</i> with it at the outset. If one washed the knife or cleaned it with a utensil, it is permitted to cut radish and the like with it, but not hot meat.
When meat has been salted in a bowl,<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Seemingly this refers to a bowl that does not have holes. Nevertheless, there are authorities who also forbid using a bowl that has holes. The Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 69:17) states that at the outset, one should not use even a bowl that has holes, but after the fact, the food is permitted.</i> one is forbidden to eat hot food<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 69:16) also quotes an opinion that forbids using the bowl for cold food at the outset if it has not been washed.</i> in it for all time,<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">One of the fundamental principles of the laws of kashrut is that an earthenware vessel can never be kashered. Once it absorbs forbidden matter, it will never be dislodged from it (see Chapter 17, Halachah 2). Hence once the bowl has absorbed the blood, there is no way that the earthenware utensil will be permitted again.</i> for the blood has already been absorbed in its clay.<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">And will be released when the hot food is placed in it, causing that food to become forbidden.</i> [This applies] even if [the utensil] is coated with lead.<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Were the utensil to be made of lead alone, it could be kashered by boiling water in it (<i>hagalah</i>), as is the law with regard to metal utensils. In this instance, however, the metal is just a coating and the blood will penetrate to the earthenware base. Hence, it remains forbidden.</i>

Chapter 7

When a person willfully eats an olive-sized portion of forbidden fat,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The term forbidden fat refers to the Hebrew term <i>cheilev</i>, to distinguish it from <i>shuman</i> which refers to fat that may be eaten. Unless otherwise mentioned, the term "fat" in this text will refer to forbidden fat.</i> he is liable for <i>kerat</i>. If he partakes of it inadvertently, he must bring a fixed sin-offering.<br>It is explicitly stated in the Torah that he is liable for partaking<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">There is, however, no prohibition against benefiting from forbidden fat. See Chapter 8, Halachah 15.</i> [of the fat] of the three species of kosher domesticated animals alone, as [Leviticus 7:23] states: "Do not partake of any fat from an ox, lamb, or goat."<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 185) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 147) include this prohibition among the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> [This applies] whether one partakes of fat from an animal that is ritually slaughtered or one partakes of fat from a<i> nevelah</i> or a <i>trefe</i> [from a kosher species].<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See the following halachah.</i> With regard to other domesticated animals and wild beasts, whether non-kosher or kosher, their fat is comparable to their meat.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., there is no separate prohibition concerning it. If the animal is kosher, its fat is not considered <i>cheilev</i> and is permitted. If the animal is not-kosher, its fat is of course forbidden, but it is not bound by a separate prohibition. This reflects a contrast to the prohibition against partaking of blood mentioned in the previous chapter.</i> Similarly, the fat of a stillborn fetus of the three species of kosher animals is comparable to its flesh. When one partakes of an olive-sized portion of it, one is liable for lashes for partaking of a <i>nevelah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">There is not, however, a separate prohibition for partaking of its forbidden fat. From <i>Chulin</i> 75a, it appears that this leniency applies only when the fetus is stillborn before its full period of gestation is completed. If, however, the full period of gestation is completed, the prohibition against <i>cheilev</i> does apply (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>; <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 64:5). See also Halachot 3-4.</i>
When a person partakes of the fat of a <i>nevelah</i> or a<i> trefe</i>, he is liable for partaking of fat and for partaking of a <i>nevelah</i> or a<i> trefe</i>. [The rationale]<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., why we do not follow the principle that one prohibition does not fall upon another.</i> is that since a prohibition is added to its meat - for it was permitted beforehand - it is also added to its fat.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The fat was forbidden previously and a further prohibition is added when the meat becomes forbidden.</i> Hence one is liable for two sets of lashes.
When a person slaughters an animal and finds a fetus in its womb, all of its fat is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Contrast this to the prohibition against <i>gid hanasheh</i> as stated in Chapter 8, Halachah 1.</i> [This applies] even if the fetus is alive, because it is considered as a limb of [the mother]. If it was carried for the full period of gestation and discovered to be alive, its fat is forbidden and one is liable for <i>kerat</i> for partaking of it. [This applies] even if [the fetus] never stepped on the ground and does not require ritual slaughter.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Chapter 5, Halachah 14. The Ra'avad mentions an opinion which states that one is not liable. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 64:2) cites both views..</i> [Instead,] we must remove all the forbidden strands of tissue and membranes from it as [is required] with regard to other animals.
When a person inserts his hand into an animal's womb and cuts off and takes out the fat of a fetus that has undergone a full period of gestation, he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">From the Rambam's inclusion of the phrase "takes out," the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> concludes that the prohibition applies only when the fat is taken out from the mother's womb while the animal is still alive. If the fat is left inside the womb and then the animal is slaughtered, he maintains that the fat is permitted. This conclusion is cited by the <i>Turei Zahav</i> 64:4 and the <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 64:6.</i> for it in the same way as if he cut off the fat of the animal itself. [The rationale is that the fulfillment of the gestation period] is what causes the prohibition against fat.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> clarifies that this stringency does not apply when the fetus dies in its mother's womb even if it has completed the nine months of gestation.</i>
There are three types of forbidden fat for which one is liable for<i> kerat</i>: the fat on the digestive organs, on both kidneys, and on the flanks. The fat-tail, by contrast, is permitted to be eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 64:5) states that one must remove the fatty portion on the inner side.</i> It is called fat only with regard to the sacrifices, just as the kidneys and the large lobe of the liver are referred to as "fat" with regard to the sacrifices.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Chulin</i> 117a interprets Leviticus 3:16: "All of the <i>cheilev</i> to God" as referring to the portions of the animal offered on the altar. These include the kidneys and the lobe of the liver although they are not "fat" (see <i>Hilchot Maaseh HaKorbanot</i> 1:18). Instead, the intent of term <i>cheilev</i> in the verse is "choice portions."</i> Similarly, we find the expressions<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Genesis 45:18; Deuteronomy 32:14.</i> "the fat of the land," and "wheat as fat as kidneys" [where the intent is not "fat,"] but "choice."<br>Since these entities are being raised up from the sacrifice to be consumed with fire for God, they are called "the fat," i.e., the choice portion, for there is nothing more choice than the portion consumed with fire for God. For this reason, with regard to <i>terumat ma'aser</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The tenth of the tithe which the Levites who receive the tithe must separate and give the priests.</i> [Numbers 18:30] states: "When you raise up its <i>fat</i> from it."<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Here also the intent of the term <i>cheilev</i> is "choice portions."</i>
The fat on the abdomen<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">An animal has four stomachs. We have chosen synonyms arbitrarily to describe them.</i> and on the gut is what is meant by the term "the fat on the digestive organs." One is liable for the fat at the joints of the thighs on the inside. This is what is meant by the term "the fat on the flanks." There is also fat on the maw which is bent like an arch; it is forbidden. There is a ligament that extends like a lobe; it is permitted. The strands [stemming from] the fat are forbidden, but one is not liable for <i>kerat</i> for them.
Fat which is covered by meat is permitted. Scripture forbids "fat on the flanks," but not within the flanks. Similarly, "fat on the kidneys" is forbidden, but not fat within the kidneys. Nevertheless, a person should remove the white matter within the kidney and only then, partake of it. It is not necessary, however, to remove all traces of it.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 64:12) cites the Rambam's view, but also that of the <i>Tur</i> which requires one to be stringent and remove all of its traces. The Rama states that, after the fact, even the stringent opinions do not consider the kidney forbidden if it was cooked without the traces of this fat being removed.</i>
There are two cords of fat in the primary loin area, near the top of the thigh. While an animal is alive, this fat can be seen on the intestines.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">For while an animal is alive, its meat hangs loosely (<i>Chulin</i> 93b).</i> When, however, it dies, one portion of meat will cling to another and cover this fat. It will not be visible until the portions of meat will be separated from each other. Nevertheless, it is forbidden, because this is not fat that is covered by meat.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">For in its lifetime, it is not covered by meat.</i><br>[In contrast,] wherever you find fat under meat, with the meat covering it and surrounding it in its entirety [so that] it will not be seen until the meat is cut away, it is permitted.
The fat of the heart and the fat of all of the small intestines are permitted. They are considered like <i>shuman</i> which is permitted fat with the exception of the top of the intestine that is next to the maw and is the beginning of the small intestines. The fat must be scraped off it.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">In his <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, Rav Yosef Caro mentions that some interpret <i>Chulin</i> 93b as stating that a cubit of the intestines are forbidden and he quotes this view as halachah in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 64:15). The Ramah, however, states that the forbidden measure is not a full cubit, but close to it.</i> This is the fat of the small intestines that is forbidden. There are some of the <i>Geonim</i> who say that the top of the intestine from which the fat must be scraped off is the large intestine,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">From the Rambam's statements in <i>Hilchot Shechitah</i> 6:10, it is obvious that he favors the first opinion. The Ra'avad differs and maintains that the second view should be followed. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 64:15) states that one should follow the stringencies of both views.</i> i.e., the colon from which feces are excreted which is the last of the digestive organs.
In the body of an animal, there are strands of tissue and membranes that are forbidden. Some are forbidden because of the prohibition against partaking of fat and others because of that against blood.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., there is blood absorbed in the tissue or membrane.</i> Whenever a strand of tissue or a membrane is forbidden because of the prohibition [Leviticus 3:17]: "Do not partake of any blood," one must remove it, and only then salt the meat as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries have cited Chapter 6, Halachot 10-12, as the Rambam's intent, but this point is not explicitly stated there.</i> If one cut [the forbidden blood vessel], it does not have to be removed.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">For the blood will flow out of it.</i> Similarly, if one roasts [the meat], it does not have to be removed.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">For the fire will cause the blood to drain off [see <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 65:1)]. As the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> mentions, there are some authorities who require that the blood vessels be cut open even when one roasts the meat.</i><br>Whenever a strand of tissue or membrane is forbidden because of the prohibition, "Do not partake of any fat," it must be removed from the animal whether one's intent is to cut it or roast it.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Otherwise, the fat will seep through the meat when it is being cooked or roasted.</i>
There are five strands of tissue in the flanks:<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 64:13) explains that these strands of tissue extend from the lower portion of the backbone. As will be explained in the notes to Halachah 13, there are differences between his interpretation and that of the Rambam.</i> three on the right and two on the left. Each of the three on the right splits into two and each of the two on the left splits into three. All are [forbidden] as fat.<br>The strands of tissue from the spleen and from the kidneys are forbidden as fat. Similarly, the membrane on the spleen, the membrane above the flanks, and the membrane on the kidneys are forbidden as fat. One is liable for <i>kerat</i> for the membrane on the thick side<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation follows the commentary of Rashi to <i>Chullin</i> 93a and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 64:10).</i> of the spleen. The remainder of the membrane is forbidden, but one is not liable for it.
The kidney has two membranes. One is liable for <i>kerat</i> for [partaking of] the upper one as one is for [partaking of] the fat of the kidney itself. The lower one is like other membranes.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., they are forbidden, but one is not liable for <i>kerat</i> for them.</i> The strands of tissue in them are forbidden, but one is not liable for <i>kerat</i> for them.
The strands of tissue of the heart, of the foreleg, of the end of the spinal cord,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">This follows the Rambam's interpretation of <i>Chullin</i> 93a. Rashi [and his view is cited by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 64:13)] understands that passage as referred to the strands of fat mentioned in Halachah 11.</i> of the lower jaw, those at either side of the tongue, and those within the fat of the small intestines which are interwoven like spiderwebs,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> and <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 75:3) write that in the present age, leniency is granted with regard to these blood vessels. Nevertheless, it is customary to remove them.</i> and the membrane above the brain in the cranium and the membrane on the testicles are all forbidden because [of the prohibition against partaking] of blood.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., these are blood vessels in which blood will be lodged after the slaughter of the animal. Therefore they must be removed or cut open (see Halachah 10) before the animal is cooked or roasted.</i>
When a kid or a lamb<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> notes that the Rambam (and his source, <i>Chullin</i> 93b) do not mention a calf when stating this leniency. Implied is that even at a younger age, the testicles of a calf are considered as developed.</i> is less than 30 days old, it is permissible to cook its testicles without peeling [the membranes from them].<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Until the kid or lamb reaches that age, the blood vessels are not developed and there is not a large quantity of blood flowing through them.</i> After 30 days, if thin red lines can be seen within them, it is recognizable that blood has circulated through them and one should not cook [the testicles] until their outer membrane has been removed or until they have been cut open and salted, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 6, Halachah 13.</i> If thin red lines have not yet been seen within them, they are permitted.
We do not assume that there is blood in any of the digestive organs<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the organs themselves. Hence they need not be salted (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>, see Chapter 6, Halachah 18). With regard to the fat on these organs, it is possible for there to be blood vessels within them as mentioned above.<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> writes that we do assume that the stomach contains blood. This view is not, however, followed by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 75:1). The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 75:1 and the <i>Turei Zahav</i> 75:1, however, state that we do assume that the stomach of a fowl contains blood.</i> through which the food passes.
It appears to me<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">This expression indicates a conclusion at which the Rambam arrived through his own powers of deduction without any explicit prior Rabbinic source. It appears that the Rambam did not arrive at a conclusive decision that the prohibition was of Rabbinic origin, but that he did favor this understanding.</i> that all of these strands of tissue and membranes are forbidden according to Rabbinic Law. [Even] if one would say that they are forbidden according to Scriptural Law,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The verses mention <i>kol</i>, "any," fat or blood. That term could be understood as an inclusion beyond the ordinary scope of the term and hence, involving these substances as well.</i> and are included in the prohibitions against partaking against any fat or any blood, one is not liable for lashes for them, only stripes for rebellious conduct. [Partaking of] them is comparable to partaking of half the measure of a forbidden substance. This is forbidden by Scriptural Law, yet one is not liable for lashes for it.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 4, Halachah 16, <i>Hilchot Shivitat Esor</i> 2:3, <i>et al</i>.</i>
We do not salt<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">For we fear that the meat will absorb some of the fat.</i> or wash fat together with meat. One should not use a knife used to cut fat to cut meat, nor a container in which fats were washed to wash meat.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">If one cleans the knife or the container first, scrubbing it carefully, it is permitted (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i> in the name of the Rashba).</i><br>Therefore a butcher should prepare three knives: one with which to slaughter, one to cut the meat,<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">For at the outset, the knife used to slaughter should not be used to cut meat (see Chapter 6, Halachah 20).</i> and one to cut fat.
If it is local custom for the butcher to wash the meat in his store, he should prepare two containers of water, one in which to wash meat and one in which to wash fat.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Chulin</i> 8b asks: Why isn't one container sufficient? First he will wash the meat in it and then the fat? The text answers that perhaps he will forget and wash the fat first. In this instance, by contrast, since he has two clearly designated containers, he will not make such a mistake.</i>
It is forbidden for a butcher to spread the fat of the flanks over the meat<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> mentions views that maintain that this restriction only applies directly after slaughter when the fat is still warm. Once it has cooled, it hardens. This ruling is quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 64:18).</i> to make it appear attractive. [The rationale is that] the membrane over the fat is thin. It may become crushed by the butcher's hand and the fat will ooze out and saturate through the meat.<br>[Although] it is forbidden to perform all of these acts, if they are performed, the meat is not forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Although there are some more stringent views regarding certain particulars, the Rambam's view is cited by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 64:19).</i> Nor is the person who performs them given corporal punishment. Instead, he is taught not to act in this manner.
Meat should not be salted before the forbidden membranes and strands of tissue are removed.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">This is a safeguard against the fat and blood being absorbed by the meat.</i> If the meat was salted with them, they must be removed after the salting. Even if the <i>gid hanesheh</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">See the following chapter.</i> was among them, one may remove them after salting and cook [the meat].<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Rambam, after the fact, we do not say that the fat and/or blood was absorbed in the meat (see also Chapter 15, Halachah 32, and notes). The Rashba differs and maintains that one must remove the surface of the meat with them, because that surface also becomes forbidden. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 64:20) quotes both views. The Rama also cites a more stringent perspective, but concludes that one may rely on the Rashba's view.</i>
When a butcher follows the practice of cleaning meat [from forbidden strands of tissue and membranes and] such a strand or membrane is found after he [alleged to have cleaned the meat], we teach him and warn him not to act negligently with regard to prohibitions.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">He is not removed from his position, because these prohibitions are Rabbinic in origin (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>). This ruling is, however, somewhat difficult to understand according to the second view mentioned in Halachah 16.</i> [More stringent rules apply] if forbidden fat is found after he [alleged to have cleaned the meat]. If it is a barley corn in size, he is removed [from his position]. If an olive-sized portion of forbidden fat is found - even in several places - after he [alleged to have cleaned the meat], he is given stripes for rebellious conduct<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">For he transgresses the prohibition: "Do not place a stumbling block before the blind," which is interpreted as a charge forbidding one to cause others to transgress. Nevertheless, lashes are not given for the violation of this prohibition.</i> and he is removed from his position. The rationale is that a butcher's word is relied upon with regard to fat.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">And his customers might cook the meat without checking for fat (or being knowledgeable about the details of the prohibition). Hence, he would cause them to transgress.</i>

Chapter 8

[The prohibition against partaking of] the <i>gid hanesheh</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Genesis, ch. 32, relates that before his confrontation with Esau, Jacob remained alone in his camp. An unidentified being - interpreted by the Torah commentaries to be Esau's archangel - wrestled with him the entire night. When he saw that he could not defeat Jacob, he gave him a blow to his upper thigh, dislocating his <i>gid hanesheh</i>. In commemoration of this event, "The children of Israel do not eat the <i>gid hanesheh</i>.<br>The Rabbis identified the <i>gid hanesheh</i> as the sciatic nerve, the large main nerve running down the back of an animal's hind leg. The term <i>gid</i>, though sometimes translated as "sinew," is a general term. As the Rambam writes in his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 3:4), it is used to refer to arteries, veins, tendons, nerves, and sinews.</i> applies with regard to kosher<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 5.</i> domesticated animals and wild beasts,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 183) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 3) include this prohibition among the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> even <i>nevelot</i> and <i>trefot</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 6.</i> It applies to a fetus<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 65:7 does not cite the Rambam's view, but instead quotes two differing opinions: one that the prohibition does not apply to a fetus at all and another, that it applies only when the fetus has completed the period of gestation and is discovered alive.</i> and to animals that have been consecrated, both those consecrated [for sacrifices] of which we partake and for sacrifices of which we do not partake. It applies to [the <i>gid</i>] on the right thigh and that on the left thigh.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Although the angel only dislodged the nerve on one of Jacob's legs, we are forbidden to partake of the nerves from both sides.</i><br>According to Scriptural Law, only [the <i>gid</i>] on the hip socket is forbidden, as [Genesis 32:33] states: "which is on the hip-socket." The remainder of the <i>gid</i> which is above the socket or below the socket - and similarly, the fat which is on the <i>gid</i> - are forbidden only according to Rabbinic decree.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 7 concerning the removal of this nerve.</i> There are two <i>giddim</i>. The inner one next to the bone is forbidden according to Scriptural Law. The entire outer one is forbidden by Rabbinic decree.
When a person partakes of the inner <i>gid hanesheh</i> on the socket, he is liable for lashes.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Because he violates a Scriptural prohibition.</i> If he partakes of the fat [of the <i>gid</i>], the remainder of the inner <i>gid</i>, or the entire outer one, he is liable for stripes for rebellious conduct.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">For violating a Rabbinic prohibition.</i><br>What is the measure of which one must partake to be liable? An olive-sized portion. If one ate the entire <i>gid</i> on the socket, one is liable, even though it is less than an olive in size. The rationale is that it is considered as a self-contained entity.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Accordingly, even if it is less than an olive-sized portion in size, one is liable. Compare to Chapter 2, Halachah 21, Chapter 15, Halachah 17, and Chapter 16, Halachah 6.</i>
When a person eats an olive-sized portion of the <i>gid</i> on the right side and an olive-sized portion of the <i>gid</i> on the left side, or he ate two entire <i>giddim</i> even if they are not the size of an olive, he receives 80 lashes. He is given lashes for every <i>gid</i> independently.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Moshe HaCohen writes that this ruling applies when the person was given a separate warning for each <i>gid</i>. Otherwise, he receives only one set of lashes. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> states that the Rambam would also accept that interpretation.</i>
The prohibition against <i>gid hanesheh</i> does not apply with regard to a fowl, because it does not have a [round]<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This explanatory addition is based on <i>Chullin</i> 92b.</i> hip-socket. Instead, its thigh is long [and flat]. If there is a fowl whose thigh is shaped like that of the thigh of an animal, i.e., it has a hip-socket, its <i>gid hanesheh</i> is forbidden, but one is not liable for lashes, because of it. Similarly, when there is an animal whose thigh is long like that of a fowl, its <i>gid hanesheh</i> is forbidden, but one is not liable for lashes for it.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Chullin</i> 92b discusses these issues and leaves both matters unresolved; hence, the Rambam's rulings.</i>
When a person eats the <i>gid hanesheh</i> from a non-kosher domesticated animal or wild beast, he is not liable.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Not for partaking of the <i>gid</i> and not for partaking of the meat of a forbidden animal.</i> [The rationale is that this prohibition] does not apply with regard to a non-kosher animal,<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Chullin</i> 101a notes that the confrontation between Jacob and the angel took place before the Giving of the Torah, at a time when the Jews could eat non-kosher animals. Hence, there is reason to say that the prohibition could involve a non-kosher animal, for partaking of such animal was not forbidden until the Giving of the Torah.<br>In response, the Talmud explains that our observance of this prohibition does not stem from the practice observed by Jacob's descendants, but because this prohibition was reiterated at the time of the Giving of the Torah. In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Chullin</i> 7:6, the Rambam elaborates on this point, explaining that our observance of Jewish practice, even the mitzvot which we know that the Patriarchs fulfilled like circumcision, stems from God's command at Sinai and not from our ancestors' observance.</i> only with regard to an animal that is entirely permitted. Nor is he considered as one who partook of the remainder of its body, for the <i>gid</i> is not included as meat, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 4, Halachah 18. See the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 65:9) which states that a <i>gid</i> is "like a piece of wood; it has no flavor."</i> If, however, one partakes of the fat on the <i>gid</i> [of a non-kosher] animal, it is considered as if one ate from its meat.
When a person partakes of a <i>gid hanesheh</i> from a<i> nevelah</i>, a <i>trefe</i>, or an animal consecrated as a burnt offering, he is liable for two [sets of lashes]. Since [the prohibition]<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the prohibition against partaking of a <i>nevelah</i>, <i>trefe</i>, or burnt offering.</i> includes the remainder of its body which was permitted, it also includes the <i>gid</i> and causes another prohibition to be added to it.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Following the concept of <i>issur kollel</i>, "an encompassing prohibition," as explained in the conclusion of Halachah 14 (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> questions the Rambam's ruling, noting that <i>Chullin</i> 82b states that according to the opinion that a <i>gid hanesheh</i> does not have any flavor, one is not liable. Only the opinion that maintains that the <i>gid hanesheh</i> does have a flavor holds one liable. From the previous halachah, it appears that the Rambam follows the former view. Why then does he hold the person liable for two sets of lashes.</i>
One who removes the <i>gid hanesheh</i> must ferret out all traces of it until nothing remains.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <i>gid hanesheh</i> and the <i>gid</i> forbidden by Rabbinic decree subdivide into several branches, this is a rather difficult task. For this reason, in most sectors of the Jewish community today, it is customary not to eat the hind-quarters of an animal. Accordingly, several cuts of meat, e.g., sirloin steak, are not available from kosher butchers.</i> A butcher's word is accepted with regard to the<i> gid hanesheh</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., we rely on his word and do not inspect the meat ourselves.</i> just as it is accepted with regard to forbidden fat. [Accordingly,] we do not purchase meat from every butcher, [only from] an upright man who has established a reputation for observance.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">If the person himself does not have a reputation for observance and knowledge of the laws, he can sell meat if he hires such a person to act as a supervisor. This is the rationale for the practice of<i> hasgachah</i>, kashrut inspection, practiced today.</i> If he slaughters meat himself and sells it, his word is accepted.
Where does the above apply? In the Diaspora. In <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, by contrast, when it is populated entirely by [Torah-observant] Jews, meat may be purchased from anyone.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">In the present era, there is no difference between <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> and other lands, for the majority of the inhabitants of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> are not Torah observant.</i>
[The following rules apply when] a butcher is considered as trustworthy to sell meat, but it is discovered that he sold meat that was <i>nevelah</i> or<i> trefe</i>. He must return the money to its owners.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">He must return the money entirely. This applies even if the customers already partook of the non-kosher meat (<i>Hilchot Mechirah</i> 16:14). The rationale is that a person's soul is revolted by the commission of a transgression and he is not considered to have benefited from the meat at all (<i>Sefer Meirat Einayim</i> 232:4).</i> He is placed under a ban of ostracism and is removed from his position.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 7, Halachah 21.</i><br>There is no way that he can correct [his act] so that people [will be allowed] to purchase meat from him until he goes to a place where his identity is unknown<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">If he performs such an act in a place where his identity is known, it can be said that he did so in order to be reinstated.</i>and returns a lost object of significant worth or slaughters an animal for his own self and has it declared <i>trefe</i> although it involves a significant financial loss. For these actions indicate that he certainly repented without any [intent to] deceive.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., they show that he is willing to forgo his financial benefit in order to keep Torah law. See also <i>Hilchot Shechitah</i> 10:14 and <i>Hilchot Edut</i> 12:9 which deal with the same concept. <i>Hilchot Edut</i> states that in order to be accepted as a witness, he must wear black garments as a sign of repentance.</i>
When a person purchases meat and sends it via a common person, [the latter's] word is accepted with regard to it. Although he has not established a reputation for Torah observance,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">See the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> who maintains that the Rambam would rule in this manner even when a Jew is reputed to transgress various prohibitions. He also mentions the opinion of the Rashba who maintains that further precautions must be taken if an article is entrusted to a non-observant Jew. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 118:8) quotes the Rambam's ruling and then cites the Rashba's view without indicating which opinion should be followed.</i> we do not suspect that he will exchange [the meat for a non-kosher cut].<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., we do not expect that he will steal. Moreover, he will derive no benefit from doing so, for he will have to supply an equivalent piece of meat for the one he exchanges. We do not expect him to cause sin without deriving any benefit. If, however, he has a reputation for stealing, his word is not accepted (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> Even the servants and maidservants<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., Canaanite servants, non-Jews purchased as servants who have undergone a partial conversion process (<i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 12:11).</i> of the Jews are trusted with regard to such a matter. A gentile, by contrast, is not [trusted], for we fear that he will exchange [the meat].<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">For a gentile is never trusted in any matters involving Jewish observance. When one desires to send food that involves prohibitions with a gentile, it is necessary to take precautions as stated in Chapter 13, Halachot 8-10.</i>
[The following rule applies when there are] ten stores, nine sell kosher meat and one sells <i>nevelot</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even if the proportions are heavily weighted in favor of the conclusion that the meat is kosher, we accept the possibility that it is non-kosher.</i> If one purchased meat from one of these stores and did not know which one he purchased from, [the meat] is forbidden. [The rationale is that] whenever [the presence of a forbidden entity] is firmly established, the situation is considered as half and half.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">This is a general principle applying in many other contexts as well, e.g., <i>Hilchot Chametz UMatzah</i> 2:10, <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 18:15.</i><br>If, however, meat is found cast away in the street,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Hagahot Maimoniot</i> explains that this law applies only when the meat is discovered in the public domain. If a person is seen taking meat from a store, but it is not known which store he took it from, the previous law applies.</i> [it is judged] according to the majority. For [we follow the assumption:] Anything that was separated, separated from the majority.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">This also is a frequently employed Talmudic principle.</i> If the majority of sellers were gentile, [the meat] is forbidden. If the majority were Jewish, it is permitted.
Similarly, when meat is found in the hand of a gentile and it is not known from where he purchased it, if [the majority of] the sellers of meat were Jewish, it is permitted.<br>This reflects the ruling according to Scriptural Law. [Nevertheless,] our Sages have already forbidden any meat found in the marketplace or in the possession of a gentile<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned by the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>, there are <i>Rishonim</i> who permit meat found in the possession of a gentile when the majority of the sellers are Jewish, maintaining that this is evident from <i>Chullin</i> 95a. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 63:1, however, quotes the Rambam's view.</i> even though all the slaughterers and all the sellers are Jewish. Moreover, even if one purchased meat, left it in his house, and it disappeared from one's sight, it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">According to the literal meaning of the Rambam's words, if meat was placed in the freezer, it would be forbidden. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 63:2) cites, however, the opinion of Rashbam which permits the meat if it is found in the same place that it was placed. The Rama writes that it is customary to follow this view.</i> unless it had a distinguishing mark, he was familiar with it and could recognize it definitely as [the piece of meat lost],<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> states that if a person has a reputation for upright conduct, his word is accepted in this concept even if he is not a Torah scholar. Note the contrast to <i>Hilchot Gezeilah V'Aveidah</i> 14:12 which accepts only the word of a Torah scholar if one claims to recognize a lost object, but cannot identify it with distinctive marks.</i> or it was bound and sealed.
[The following rule applies when] one hung a container filled with pieces of meat, the container broke, and the pieces fell to the earth.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, he found it as he left it, it is certainly permitted (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i> If there is no distinguishing mark [on the meat] and he was not able to recognize it, it is forbidden. [The rationale is that] it is possible to say that the meat that was in the container was dragged away by a wild beast or creeping animal and this is other meat.
It is permitted to derive benefit from a <i>gid hanesheh</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> notes that according to <i>Pesachim</i> 22a, it would appear that the authorities who maintain that the <i>gid hanesheh</i> has no flavor also maintain that it is forbidden to benefit from it. Now the Rambam follow the perspective that the <i>gid hanesheh</i> has no flavor (see Halachah 5). Hence his position here is somewhat difficult. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains, however, that the two positions are not necessary interrelated and both rulings of the Rambam can be upheld.</i> Therefore it is permissible for a person to send a thigh which contains a <i>gid hanesheh</i> to a gentile.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., because there is no prohibition against receiving benefit from the <i>gid hanesheh</i>, he does not have to remove it before selling the meat.</i> He may give him the entire thigh intact in the presence of a Jew. We do not suspect that [the other] Jew will partake of this meat before the <i>gid</i> is removed, because its place is recognizable.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Jew sees a co-religionist giving the gentile the meat, he will assume that it was ritually slaughtered and that the meat was kosher. [This applies in a place where public announcements are made when an animal is discovered to be <i>trefe</i> (<i>Chullin</i> 93b). Otherwise, the Jew must tell the gentile that the animal is kosher (<i>Shulchan Aruch, Yoreh De'ah</i> 65:11)].<br>Nevertheless, since the place of the <i>gid hanesheh</i> is recognizable - i.e., it is obvious whether the <i>gid</i> is still in the thigh or has already been removed - he will not partake of the meat until the <i>gid</i> is removed.</i> Accordingly, if the thigh was cut into pieces, he should not give it to a gentile in the presence of a Jew, lest the other Jew partake of it.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Since the place of the <i>gid hanesheh</i> is not obvious, the other Jew may think that ordinary kosher meat is being given and may partake of it.</i>
Wherever the Torah states: "Do not eat," "You shall not eat,"<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Both of these commands are in the second person: one singular, one plural.</i> "They shall not eat," or "It shall not be eaten," the intent is that it is forbidden both to partake of or benefit from the forbidden entity<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Sometimes the command is stated in an active voice; sometimes, it is passive; sometimes, singular and sometimes plural. The passive form implies that it is forbidden to derive any benefit that could lead to one's eating, e.g., selling it for money that could be used to purchase food.</i> unless:<br>a) a verse explicitly states otherwise, as it does with regard to a <i>nevelah</i> [Deuteronomy 14:21]: "Give it to the stranger in your gate and he shall partake of it," or with regard to forbidden fat [Leviticus 7:24]: "You may use it for any task"; or<br>b) the Oral Law states explicitly that it is permitted to benefit from it, as is the case with regarding to teeming animals, swarming animals, blood, a limb from a living animal, and the <i>gid hanesheh</i>. For according to the Oral Tradition, it is permitted to benefit from all these prohibited entities, even though it is forbidden to partake of them.
Whenever it is forbidden to benefit from a substance, if a person derives benefit without partaking of it, e.g., he sold or gave to a gentile or gave it to dogs, he is not liable for lashes.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Moshe HaCohen questions this ruling, stating that if the intent of the Scriptural prohibition is that it is forbidden to benefit from these substances, why is one not liable for lashes for deriving such benefit? The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains that he is not liable, for one is liable for lashes only when he derives benefit from the food in the ordinary manner one derives benefit from food. This includes only eating. Receiving money, by contrast, is not considered as benefiting from food in the ordinary manner. Rav Moshe HaCohen, however, anticipated that attempted resolution and explains that, on the contrary, selling edible food is an ordinary way of deriving benefit.</i> He should, however, be given stripes for rebellious conduct. The money [he received] is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">There is one exception to this: money received in return for a false deity or articles associated with it. That money is itself forbidden (see Chapter 13, Halachah 15; <i>Hilchot Avodat Kochavim</i> 7:19).</i><br>Whenever it is forbidden to partake of a substance, but it is permitted to benefit from it, even though it is permitted to benefit from it, it is forbidden do business with such articles or establish oneself in a profession that involves forbidden entities.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., one's livelihood may not revolve around the sale of these forbidden entities or performing work with them (e.g., serving as a chef in a non-kosher restaurant). The rationale for the prohibition is that we fear that a person who has extensive involvement with forbidden substances may come to partake of them (Rashba).<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> clarifies that the above applies only with regard to food from forbidden species. One may choose a profession that involves employing a horse or a donkey as a beast of burden.</i> [There is] an exception, forbidden fat, for concerning it, it is written: "You may use it for any task." For this reason, we do not do business with <i>nevelot, trefot</i>, teeming animals, and swarming animals.
When a trapper happens upon a non-kosher wild animal, fowl, or fish, and he snares them or he traps both kosher and non-kosher animals, he may sell them.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">He must sell them immediately. He may not raise them until they become large [Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 117:4)]. See also <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 117:6 who questions whether the leniency is granted only to a professional trapper or to any person.</i> He may not, however, intend to have his profession concern non-kosher species.<br>It is, however, permitted to do business with milk that was milked by a gentile without being observed by a Jew, cheeses made by gentiles, and the like.
This is the general principle: Whenever a prohibition is forbidden by Scriptural Law, it is forbidden to do business with it. Whenever the prohibition is Rabbinic in origin, it is permitted do business with it, whether we are certain of the existence of the prohibition or it is a matter of question.

Chapter 9

It is forbidden to cook meat and milk together and to partake of them according to Scriptural Law.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandments 186-187) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvot 113 and 92) include these prohibitions among the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> It is forbidden to benefit from [such a mixture]. It must be buried. Its ashes are forbidden like the ashes of all substances that must be buried.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 19:11,13.</i><br>Whenever a person cooks an olive-sized portion of the two substances together,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., together the mixture is the size of an olive. It is not necessary that one have an olive-sized portion of milk and an olive-sized portion of meat.</i> he is worthy of lashes, as [Exodus 23:19] states: "Do not cook a kid in its mother's milk." Similarly, a person who partakes of an olive-sized portion of the meat and milk that were cooked together<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, the meat and milk have not been cooked together, there is no Scriptural prohibition against partaking of them together (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>). According to Rabbinic Law, it is forbidden to partake of them in any manner.</i> is worthy of lashes even though he was not the one who cooked them.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Even if a prohibition was not violated when cooking them together (e.g., they were cooked by a gentile), it is forbidden for a Jew to partake of the mixture. The implication is that the prohibitions against cooking the mixture and partaking of it are separate issues that do not necessarily share a connection (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>).</i>
The Torah remained silent concerning the prohibition against partaking [of meat and milk]<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., no where in the Torah does it state that it is forbidden to partake of such a mixture.</i> only because it forbade cooking them. This is as if to say: Even cooking it is forbidden, how much more so partaking of it.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Significantly, in his <i>Sefer HaMitzvot, loc. cit.</i>, the Rambam explains that the prohibitions against partaking of and benefiting from milk and meat are derived from the fact that the Torah repeats this prohibition three times. Perhaps the reason the Rambam does not mention this means of derivation here is to avoid the following question being raised: Why are lashes not given for benefiting from milk and meat?<br>To explain: In Chapter 8, Halachah 16, the Rambam writes that one is not liable for lashes for deriving benefit from a forbidden substance. As explained in the notes to that halachah, the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> explains that one is liable for lashes only when he derives benefit from the food in an ordinary manner from food. This includes only eating and not other forms of deriving benefit. Nevertheless, seemingly this should not apply with regard to benefiting from a mixture of milk and meat. For, as stated in Chapter 14, Halachah 10, in that instance, one is liable even if one does not derive benefit in the ordinary manner. Hence, it would appear that one should be liable for lashes for partaking of such a mixture.<br>Among the explanations given why one is not liable is that the prohibition against deriving benefit from a mixture of milk and meat is derived from an inference from a more lenient instance to a more stringent one (a <i>kal vichomer</i>; see <i>Chullin</i> 115b). And we follow the principle that punishment is not meted out when a prohibition is derived in such a fashion, only when it is stated explicitly (<i>Sifri, Naso</i>). If, however, there was an explicit prohibition in the Torah teaching us that deriving benefit from a mixture of milk and meat was forbidden. Seemingly, one would be liable for lashes (<i>Lechem Mishneh</i>).</i> [To cite a parallel:] The Torah did not mention the prohibition against relations with one's daughter, because it forbade those with the daughter of one's daughter.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 2:6.</i>
According to Scriptural Law, the prohibition involves only [a mixture of] meat from a kosher domesticated animal<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">With regard to the meat or milk of a kosher wild beast or fowl, see the following halachah and notes.</i> and milk from a kosher domesticated animal, as implied by the verse: "Do not cook a kid in its mother's milk."<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the prohibition involves only a kid that could be eaten and milk of which one could partake.</i> The term "a kid" includes the offspring of an ox, the offspring of a sheep, and the offspring of a goat unless the verse states explicitly, a goat-kid.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the term <i>gidi</i> translated as "kid," commonly means "a kid-goat." Nevertheless, according to the Bible, it is not necessarily restricted to this meaning unless the verse specifies so explicitly, as in Genesis 27:16; 38:20.</i> The term "a kid in its mother's milk" [does not exclude all other situations].<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the intent is not that one is liable only for cooking an offspring in the milk of its mother and not in any other situations. See the conclusion of the following halachah.</i> Instead, the Torah is speaking regarding the commonplace circumstance.<br>With regard to the meat of a kosher animal which was cooked in the milk of a non-kosher animal or the meat of a non-kosher animal which was cooked in the milk of a kosher animal, by contrast, cooking is permitted, and deriving benefit is permitted. One is not liable for [transgressing the prohibition against partaking of] meat and milk if one partakes of it.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Needless to say, one is liable for partaking of the non-kosher meat or the non-kosher milk.</i>
Similarly, the meat of a wild beast and the meat of a fowl together with the milk of a wild beast or the milk of a domesticated animal is not forbidden according to Scriptural Law.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">There is a difference of opinion among the Rabbis in <i>Chullin </i>116a whether the prohibition against eat the meat of a wild beast [cooked] in milk is Scriptural or Rabbinic in origin. According to some interpretations, that difference of opinion is perpetuated among the <i>Rishonim</i> (see <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 87:4). Nevertheless, the overwhelming majority of <i>Rishonim</i> and <i>Achronim</i> follow the opinion the Rambam states here. This is also the ruling of the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 87:3).<br>Significantly, in (<i>Hilchot Mamrim</i> 2:9), the Rambam states that the meat of a wild beast that is cooked in milk is forbidden according to Scriptural Law. In their glosses to <i>Hilchot Mamrim</i>, the Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explain that there, the Rambam is speaking theoretically: Were the halachah to follow the opinion that the meat of a wild beast is forbidden according to Scriptural law, the ruling would be such and such. The <i>Merkevat HaMishneh</i>, however, maintains that a printing error crept into the text in <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> and the text should be changed to fit the Rambam's ruling in <i>Hilchot Mamrim</i>.</i> Therefore it is permitted to cook it and it is permitted to benefit from it. It is forbidden to partake of it according to Rabbinic Law so that people at large will not be negligent and come to violate the Scriptural prohibition against milk and meat and partake of the meat of a kosher domesticated animal [cooked] in the milk of a kosher domesticated animal. For the literal meaning of the verse implies only the meat of a kid in the milk of its actual mother.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Hence were the Sages to allow one to partake of the meat of a wild beast and fowl cooked in milk, one might think that the prohibition applies only in its most literal context. As a safeguard to prevent this error from occurring, they instituted this prohibition.</i> Therefore, they forbade all meat in milk.
It is permitted to partake of fish and locusts [cooked] in milk.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Turei Zahav</i> 87:3 and the <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 87:5 mention that there are authorities who forbid eating fish and milk together because it can cause health dangers. They, however, reject that ruling.</i><br>When a person slaughters a fowl and finds eggs that are completed within it, it is permitted to partake of them together with milk.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to eggs that already have a yolk and whites, but are still connected to the chicken's body (<i>Maggid Mishneh</i>). See the <i>Turei Zahav</i> 87:6 and the <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 87:9 who quote authorities who explain that even though such eggs are considered as meat in certain contexts, there is no prohibition against partaking of them together with milk.</i>
When [milk and meat] are smoked, cooked in the hot springs of Tiberias, or the like, one is not liable for lashes.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Nevertheless, there is a prohibition against partaking of all these mixtures and those mentioned in the following clause.</i> Similarly, when meat is cooked in whey, milk from a dead animal,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., milk that was in the animal's udders when it died or was slaughtered. See Halachah 12.</i> or milk from a male,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 87:6) states that, after the fact, there is no prohibition against a mixture of milk from a male and meat. The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 87:16 explains that this refers to milk from a male human. Even the Rama would forbid milk from a male animal according to Rabbinic Law.</i> or if blood is cooked with milk, one is absolved and is not liable for partaking [of the mixture] because of [the prohibition against partaking of] milk and meat.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Implied is that in the latter instance, one is liable for partaking of blood. The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 87:15 notes that according to many authorities, one is not liable for lashes for partaking of blood that has been cooked.</i><br>When, however, a person cooks the meat of a dead animal, forbidden fat, or the like in milk, he is liable for lashes for cooking.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Since we are speaking about meat or fat from a kosher species, the prohibition against cooking applies. In this instance, we do not say that "one prohibition does not fall upon another," because there is no prohibition against cooking a <i>nevelah</i> or forbidden fat.</i> He is not liable for lashes for partaking [of the mixture] because of the prohibition against meat and milk.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">He is, however, liable for partaking of a <i>nevelah</i> or of forbidden fat.</i> For the prohibition against [mixtures of] meat and milk does not take effect with regard to [entities] prohibited as <i>nevelah</i> or forbidden fat, because we are not speaking about a more encompassing prohibition, a prohibition which adds a new dimension, or [two] prohibitions that take effect at the same time.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Chapter 14, Halachah 18, and in <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 17:8, it is only in these circumstances, that we do not follow the general principle: One prohibition does not fall upon another prohibition.<br>The commentaries ask: Seemingly, the prohibition against a mixture of meat and milk does add a new dimension to this prohibition, because it is forbidden to benefit from such a mixture. Why then does the prohibition against partaking of milk and meat not apply?<br>The Rambam attempts to resolve this question in his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Keritot</i> 3:4) by explaining that since the prohibition against benefiting from the mixture is an extension of the prohibition against partaking of it, when - as in the present instance - the prohibition against partaking of it does not apply, the prohibition against benefiting from it also does not apply.</i>
When a person cooks a fetus in milk, he is liable. Similarly, one who partakes of it is liable. When, however, one cooks a placenta, skin, sinews, bones, the roots of the horns, or the soft portion of the hoofs [cooked] in milk,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">For as stated in Chapter 4, Halachah 18, such substances are not fit to be eaten and thus are not considered as meat.</i> he is not liable. Similarly, one who partakes [of such a mixture] is not liable.
When meat falls into milk or milk falls into meat and they are cooked together, the minimum measure [for which one is liable is] enough for one substance to impart its flavor to the other.<br>What is implied? When a piece of meat falls into a bubbling pot<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The pot must be boiling hot. If meat falls into cold milk, it will not absorb it. See Halachah 17.</i> full of milk, a gentile should taste [the contents of] the pot.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Chullin</i> 97a states "An Aramean chef shall taste it." <i>Tosafot</i> and others explain that only a chef's word is accepted. He will not lie, because if his falsification is discovered, his professional reputation will be tarnished and he will suffer a loss. We suspect that an ordinary gentile, by contrast, will lie. His word is only accepted with regard to ritual matters when he makes statements in the course of conversation, without knowing that a Jew is depending on his word.<br>This interpretation is not evident from the Rambam's words. On the contrary, it appears that according to the Rambam, the statements of any gentile are acceptable (see Chapter 15, Law 30, and notes). The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 92:1 quotes the Rambam's words. The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 92:1 mentions the view of <i>Tosafot</i>. The Rama states that in the Ashkenazic community, the custom is not to rely on the word of a gentile in this context. Instead, we require sixty times the volume of the meat in all instances. Otherwise, both the milk and the meat are forbidden.</i> If it has the flavor of meat, it is forbidden. If not, it is permitted, but the piece of meat is forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">For it certainly absorbed milk.</i><br>When does the above apply? When he hurried and removed the piece of meat before it discharged the milk that it absorbed. If he did not remove it [that quickly], we require 60 times its volume,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">As will be explained (see Chapter 15, Halachah 6, and notes), our Sages received the tradition that a forbidden substances will not impart its flavor to a mixture when the mixture contains sixty times its volume.</i> because the milk that it absorbed became forbidden. It was discharged and then mixed together with the remainder of the milk.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">There is no way of distinguishing the remainder of the milk from the forbidden milk. Hence the entire mixture is forbidden unless there is more than 60 times the amount of the forbidden substance.</i>
When milk falls [onto a piece of] meat [being cooked] in a pot,<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">As evident from the continuation of the Rambam's words in this and the following halachah, we are speaking of an instance where milk falls on a piece of meat that is not in the sauce. According to Rashi, the lower portion of the meat is resting within the sauce in the pot and its upper portion - on which the milk falls - projects beyond it. According to Rabbenu Yitzchak, the entire portion is outside the sauce. See <i>Turei Zahav</i> 92:2; <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 92:4. (From the Rambam's wording at the beginning of the following halachah, it would appear that he follows Rabbenu Yitzchak's position.)</i> we taste<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., we have the meat tasted by a gentile as above.</i> the piece on which the milk fell. If it does not have the flavor of milk, everything is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the piece itself is permitted and therefore all the contents of the pot.</i> [More stringent rules apply] if the piece of meat has the flavor of milk. Even though if the piece of meat was pressed to remove [the absorbed liquid], the flavor [of milk] would not remain, since it has the flavor of milk now, it is forbidden and we must measure its entire volume.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Since the meat becomes forbidden, because it is meat that has been mixed with milk, tasting the mixture for milk is not sufficient. Instead, we consider the meat as a forbidden article and measure 60 times its volume. It is not possible to distinguish between the flavor of the forbidden meat and that of the permitted meat.</i> If everything in the pot - the other meat, the vegetables, the sauce, and the spices - is great enough so that the piece is one sixtieth of the entire [volume], that piece of meat is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Once it becomes forbidden, it is considered as a prohibited entity and cannot become permitted again. Our Sages [<i>Chullin</i> 108b; Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 92:3-4)] use the expression: "The piece becomes like carrion," i.e., as if it is inherently forbidden.</i> and the remainder is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">As if it was mixed with 60 times its volume of non-kosher meat. If the entire mixture is not 60 times the volume of the forbidden piece, the entire mixture becomes forbidden.</i>
When does the above apply? When he did not stir the pot at the outset when the milk fell into it. [He did so] only at the end<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Since he mixed at the end, after the meat became forbidden, the entire mixture may become forbidden.</i> and did not cover the pot.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Thus the piece of meat on which the milk fell remained a discrete entity, separate from the entire mixture. Hence it becomes forbidden.</i><br>If, however, he stirred the pot from the beginning until the end<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 82) states that it is sufficient for him to stir the mixture at the beginning. This will cause the milk to be blended throughout the entire mixture. There is no need for him to continue stirring the pot.
