Rav Yaakov ibn Chaviv maintains that the Rambam would also accept this position. The Rambam mentions stirring the put until the end only for stylistic reasons. This interpretation is also apparent in the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Chullin</i> 8:3).<br>In his <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> and <i>Beit Yosef</i>, Rav Yosef Caro differs and maintains that the Rambam's words here should be understood literally. Unless he mixed the pot from the beginning until the end, we fear that it was not mixed well. Hence in his <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 92:2, he quotes the Rambam exactly. The Rama, however, cites the Tur's position.</i> or covered [the pot]<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">For covering the pot also causes the flavor of the milk to be blended throughout the entire mixture.</i> from the time [the milk] fell until the end, [the question of whether a prohibition exists depends] on whether [the milk] imparted its flavor.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">And we have a gentile taste the mixture as above.</i><br>Similarly, if the milk fell into the sauce or onto all the pieces and it was not known on which piece [the milk] fell,<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur</i> and the Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 92:2) emphasize that if the person does not stir the pot immediately after the milk fell in, the piece on which the milk fell becomes forbidden. Since its identity is unknown, all the pieces are forbidden unless the entire mixture is 60 times larger than its largest piece.<br>Rav Yaakov ibn Chaviv and Rav Yosef Caro (in his <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> and <i>Beit Yosef</i>) interpret the Rambam's intent as analogous to that of the <i>Tur</i>. They maintain that the Rambam also would agree that if person waited after the milk fell on the piece, that piece - and perhaps all the pieces - become(s) forbidden.<br>The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> offers a different interpretation, explaining that in this instance, we do not say that the piece of meat on which the milk fell becomes forbidden because we do not know which piece it is. Hence rather than have the taste of the milk affect that piece, we stir the entire mixture so that the milk will become blended into it and become nullified as explained in the following note.<br>The <i>Turei Zahav</i> 92:6 and the <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 92:8 follow the interpretation of the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>, explaining that in this instance, the principle (<i>Beitzah</i> 4b): "We do not nullify the existence of a forbidden substance at the outset," does not apply. For since the identity of the forbidden substance was never established, there is no specific prohibited substance involved. Hence at the outset, the entire pot is considered as subject to being forbidden. To prevent that from happening, we stir it so that the prohibition will not take effect.</i> he should stir the entire pot so that all its contents will be mixed [thoroughly].<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., intentionally mixing the milk throughout the entire pot and thus nullifying its presence. As the <i>Tur</i> (<i>loc. cit</i>) writes, if the milk fell into the sauce, even if the person did not stir the mixture, this would be the ruling. Nevertheless, the Rambam advises the person to stir the mixture so that it will be mixed thoroughly and no trace will remain.</i> If the flavor of milk [can be detected] in the entire pot, it is forbidden. If not, it is permitted. If a gentile to taste [the pot] whom we can rely on cannot be found, we require a measure of sixty whether for meat in milk or milk in meat. If there is one measure in sixty,<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">More precisely, the permitted substance must be sixty times the volume of the forbidden substance. Thus we are speaking about the forbidden substance being one sixty-first.</i> it is permitted. If there is less than sixty, it is forbidden.
When meat has been cooked in a pot, milk should not be cooked in it.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">This applies even on a later day. According to Scriptural Law, after 24 hours, there is no prohibition. Nevertheless, according to Rabbinic Law, at the outset, one should be stringent and not cook milk in a pot in which meat was cooked previously even if it had been cooked several days beforehand.</i> If one cooked [milk] in it, [it is forbidden] if it imparted its flavor.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it should be tasted by a gentile. According to the Ashkenazic custom not to rely on a gentile, we require that the contents be 60 times the volume of the pot. The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 93:1 states that it would be very rare for such a situation to exist. Generally, the ratio between a pot and its contents is less than 60. Hence, in most instances, the food would be prohibited.</i>
The udders [of an animal] are forbidden according to Rabbinic Law.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., we are afraid that a certain amount of milk remained in the udder or that the udder absorbed a certain amount of milk. Since we do not know how much milk it absorbed, we assume that it is entirely forbidden.</i> [The prohibition is not of Scriptural origin, because] meat that was cooked in milk from an animal that was slaughtered is not forbidden according to Scriptural Law, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Halachah 6.</i><br>Therefore if one cut it open and discharged the milk it contained, it is permitted to roast it and eat it. If one cut it both horizontally and vertically and then pressed it into a wall until none of the moisture of the milk remained, it may be cooked with other meat.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The ruling regarding roasting is more lenient than the ruling regarding cooking, because when meat is roasted, any fluids it contains are discharged and flow outward without being absorbed (see Halachah 14). When it is cooked, by contrast, it stews in its juices and it and any other meat will absorb the milk it discharges.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 90:2) writes that the accepted custom is not to cook it with other meat at all and to cook it alone only after it has been cut vertically and horizontally and pressed into a wall. The Rama adds that it is Ashkenazic custom not to cook it at all.</i><br>When an udder has not been cut open, when from a young animal that never nursed<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., our Sages enforced their decree universally, without differentiating between one animal and another.</i> or from an older one, it is forbidden to cook it. If one transgressed and cooked it alone, it is permitted to partake of it. If one cooked it with other meat, we require 60 times its volume. The udder itself is calculated in the 60.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">In Chapter 15, Halachah 18, the Rambam explains that since only a Rabbinic prohibition is involved, our Sages were more lenient. Thus the Rambam interprets this ruling as being of general significance. The Rashba offers a different rationale for this ruling, explaining that since the meat of the udder is acceptable, we include it in the reckoning of 60. Thus in contrast to other instances where 60 times the amount of the forbidden substance is required, here, we require only 59.</i>
What is implied? If the entire mixture together with the udder was sixty times the volume of the udder, the udder is forbidden,<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi, <i>Chullin</i> 97b, explains that we assume that the milk imparted its flavor to the udder. Hence it becomes forbidden as the Rambam proceeds to state.</i> and the remainder is permitted. If there was less than 60 times its volume, the entire mixture is forbidden. Regardless of [the ruling applying to the entire mixture], if the udder fell into another pot, it can cause it to be forbidden. We require 60 times its volume as in the original instance.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Thus according to the Rambam, only 59 times its volume is required. This view is quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 90:1). The <i>Tur</i> and the Rama differ and maintain that the second mixture is judged more stringently than the first. They follow the rationale of the Rashba cited above. Thus they maintain at first, the udder is included in the reckoning, because its meat is permitted. With regard to the second mixture, by contrast, it is the meat, not the milk of the udder which is forbidden. Hence 60 times its volume is required.</i> [The rationale is that] the udder which is cooked becomes considered as a forbidden piece of meat.<br>We measure [the volume of] the udder at the time that it was cooked, not according to its state when it fell [into the mixture].
We do not roast an udder that has been cut above meat on a spit.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">This is a safeguard so that it will not discharge milk which will flow over other pieces of meat.</i> If, however, one roasted it [in that manner], everything is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">For even if its milk does flow over other pieces of meat, they are not forbidden. The rationale is that since it has been cut open, we do not suspect that perhaps some milk remained. Since the entire prohibition is Rabbinic in origin, we are not overly stringent. The Rama states if the udder was not cut open beforehand, the meat that is lower on the spit is forbidden.</i>
A stomach that is cooked with milk inside it<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a calf that had drank its mother's milk and was cooked with that milk in its stomach.</i> is permitted. [The rationale is that] it is no longer considered as milk.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> states that according to the Rambam, this applies even to milk that is still liquid. Since it has already undergone preliminary digestive processes, it is no longer considered as milk. See Chapter 4, Halachah 19.</i> Instead, it is considered as a waste product, because it undergoes a change in the digestive system.
It is forbidden to place the skin of a kosher animal's stomach [in milk] to serve as a catalyst for it to harden into cheese. If one used it as a catalyst, [a gentile] should taste the cheese. If it has a taste of meat, it is forbidden. If not, it is permitted. [The rationale is that] the catalyst is itself a permitted entity,<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Hence the logic mentioned in the following note does not apply.</i> for it comes from the stomach of a kosher animal. [The only question] is [whether] the prohibition against meat and milk [was violated] and that is dependent on whether the flavor was imparted.<br>[Different laws apply, however, when] one uses the skin of the stomach of a <i>nevelah</i>, a <i>trefe</i>, or a non-kosher animal. [The rationale is that] since the catalyst is forbidden in its own right, the cheese becomes forbidden, not because of the prohibition of meat and milk, but because of the prohibition against a <i>nevelah</i>. For this reason, [our Sages] forbade cheeses made by gentiles, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 3, Halachah 13. As the Rambam states in that halachah, since the amount of skin used is minimal, we might think that no prohibition is involved, for the forbidden substance would be nullified. Nevertheless, the Rabbis ruled stringently, explaining that since the catalyst which causes the milk to curdle is forbidden, everything is forbidden.</i>
Meat alone is permitted and milk alone is permitted. It is [only] when the two become mixed together through cooking that they both become forbidden.<br>When does the above apply? When they were cooked together, when a hot object fell into a hot object,<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">For in this instance, the two substances will be absorbed by each other just as if they had been cooked together.</i> or when a cold object fell into a hot object.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">For we follow the principle (<i>Pesachim</i> 76a): "The lower one dominates," and the food is considered as hot.</i> If, however, [milk or meat] that is hot fell into the other when it is cold, [all that is necessary is to] remove the surface of the meat which touched the milk; the remainder may be eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">We assume that the meat's surface absorbed a small amount of milk while it was cooling down (<i>ibid.</i>). Hence the surface is forbidden and must be removed. The milk does not, however, permeate beyond the surface. Therefore the remainder is permitted. With regard to the milk, it appears that there is no prohibition. The Radbaz explains that since it is not possible to remove the surface of the milk, there is no prohibition whatsoever. Other <i>Rishonim</i> require that the milk be sixty times the volume of the surface of the meat. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 91:4) quotes the Rambam's ruling and the <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 91:8 states that this decision is accepted by the Rama despite the fact that this might appear incompatible with some of the other rulings of the Rama. The <i>Turei Zahav</i> 91:7, however, argues in favor of the view of the other <i>Rishonim</i>.</i><br>If cold [meat] fell into cold [milk or the opposite], one must wash the piece of meat thoroughly.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Since they are both cold, there is no suspicion that one will be absorbed by the other. Washing the meat is necessary only to remove any traces of milk that might be left.</i> [Afterwards,] it may be eaten. For this reason, it is permitted to [carry] meat and milk bound together in a single handkerchief, provided they do not touch each other. If they do touch each other, one must wash the meat and wash the cheese.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Bayit Chadash</i> rules that this applies only when one of them is moist. If they are both solid, they need not even be washed.</i> [Afterwards,] he may partake of them.
When a substance is salted to the extent that it cannot be eaten because of its salt,<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">In previous eras, before the advent of refrigeration, meat was salted thoroughly to preserve it. Afterwards, when one desired to partake of it, he would soak it in water to remove the salt (Rashi, <i>Chullin</i> 112a). The Radbaz states that we are speaking about salting meat in a manner similar to the way it is salted to remove its blood. If less salt than that is used, these laws do not apply. See the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 91:5) which discusses these laws.</i> is considered as if it is boiling.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., we assume that it will cause substances to be discharged and absorbed as cooking does. This is merely a Rabbinic stringency.<br>It must be emphasized that the comparison to cooking is not total. Generally, salting only causes the surface of the substance to become forbidden. If, however, the substance is fatty, the entire substance becomes forbidden (<i>ibid.</i>:6).</i> If it can be eaten in its present state like <i>kutach</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">A mixture of milk, breadcrumbs, salt, and spices, commonly served as a dip in Babylon.</i> it is not considered as if it is boiling.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">All that is necessary is to wash the meat and/or cheese thoroughly.</i>
[The following rules apply when] a fowl that has been slaughtered falls into milk or <i>kutach</i> that contains milk: If it is raw, it need only be washed thoroughly and it is permitted. If it was roasted, one should remove its surface.<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">There is a difference of opinion among the Rabbis if this is speaking about a roasted fowl that is hot, or even one that is not hot. According to the latter opinion, it will still absorb some milk because it has become soft and permeable. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 91:7) quotes the first view while the <i>Tur</i>and the Rama mention the second.</i> If it has portions where it is open<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., instead of being a solid surface, the surface of the meat cracks open in several places.</i> or it is spiced and it falls into milk or <i>kutach</i>, it is forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Because the cracks in its surface or the spices will cause it to absorb the milk to a greater extent than it would otherwise. This clause appears also to be referring to meat that has been roasted. There are, however, opinions that interpret it as referring to raw meat. See <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 91:21.</i>
It is forbidden to serve fowl<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Needless to say, this applies to meat (see <i>Lechem Mishneh</i>).</i> together with milk on the table upon which one is eating.<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">They may, however, be placed together on a serving table (<i>Chullin</i> 104b).</i> This is a decree [enacted] because habit [might lead] to sin.<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Since both substances are permitted and they are served together, one might accidentally partake of them together.<br>Implied is that if substances are forbidden and one would not ordinarily partake of them, there is no difficulty in having them served on the table at which one is eating. See <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 88:2.</i> We fear that one will eat one with the other. [This applies] even though fowl with milk is forbidden only because of Rabbinic decree.<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">See the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 88:2) which explains that if a distinction is made, e.g., the milk is placed on one type of placemat and the meat on another, there is no prohibition.</i>
When two guests who are not familiar with each other are eating at the same table, one may eat the meat of an animal and one may eat cheese. [The rationale is] that they are not well-acquainted with each other to the extent that they will eat together.<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Thus there is little likelihood that they will share their food together.</i>
We do not knead a loaf with milk. If one kneaded it [with milk], the loaf is forbidden,<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Even to be eaten alone [<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 97:1)]. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, however, grants license if only a small amount of bread was prepared in this manner and thus it can be eaten at one time.</i> because habit [might lead] to sin, lest he eat it together with meat. We do not dab an oven with animal fat.<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the fat is kosher.</i> If in fact one dabbed an oven [with fat], any loaf is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">We fear that the fat from the oven became absorbed in the bread, causing it to become <i>fleishig</i>.</i> until one fires the oven,<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Firing the oven to the point that it becomes red-hot will burn away all traces of the fat.</i> lest one eat milk with [that loaf]. If one altered the appearance of the bread so that it will be evident that one should not eat meat or milk with it, it is permitted.
When a loaf has been baked together with roasted meat, or fish were roasted together with meat,<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Even if they did not touch each other. Note the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 116) and the <i>Turei Zahav</i> 95:3) which mention that there is a prohibition against eating fish roasted with meat because it could cause a health problem.</i> it is forbidden to eat them together with milk.<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">For the vapors from the meat become absorbed in the bread or in the fish. In Chapter 15, Halachah 32, the Rambam rules that vapors do not cause an object to become forbidden. There is not necessarily a contradiction between these two rulings, for here we are speaking about a small oven [Radbaz,<i> Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 97:3). Even though, after the fact, kosher meat roasted together with non-kosher meat in a small oven is permitted, here one is not deeming the bread or fish forbidden, one is merely prohibiting that it be eaten with milk (<i>Siftei Cohen</i> 97:4).</i> When meat was eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Or cooked [Radbaz, <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 95:1)]. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> adds that this ruling applies only when the dish was thoroughly washed and no trace of meat remains.</i> in a dish and then fish were cooked in it, it is permitted to eat those fish together with <i>kutach</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the <i>kutach</i> contains milk. The rationale is that although the flavor of the meat was imparted to the dish and from the dish, it was imparted to the fish. Nevertheless, since it went through these two intermediate stages, it is not considered significant and does not cause the fish to be considered <i>fleishig</i>. The Rabbis referred to this concept as <i>nat bar nat - notain taam bar notain tam</i> ("imparting merely flavor a second time").<br>It must be emphasized that <i>nat bar nat</i> is permitted only with regard to a mixture of milk and meat. The rationale is that both milk and meat are permitted, a prohibition only exists when they are mixed together and if one of them has been weakened to the extent that it is <i>nat bar nat</i>, it is not considered significant. When, however, an entity is inherently forbidden, e.g., non-kosher meat, when its flavor becomes absorbed into a dish, that dish becomes forbidden and it may not be used again for hot food (Radbaz).</i>
When a knife was used to cut roasted meat<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's wording implies that the meat was hot (Radbaz). This ruling applies also to hot cooked meat (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>). There are opinions that maintain that this ruling also applies when the meat was cold (Radbaz).</i> and then was used to cut radish or other sharp foods, it is forbidden to eat them together with<i> kutach</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi, <i>Chullin</i> 111b, states that the rationale is that it is likely that there will be a small amount of fat left on the knife. Thus when the knife is used to cut the sharp food, its sharpness will cause that the flavor of the fat will be imparted to it. According to this view, if the knife was cleaned or used to cut another substance first, it does not cause the radish to be forbidden [<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 96:5); note also the dissenting view of the Rama]. (This opinion speaks of fat being left on the knife, for if there was no fat there, seemingly, this instance would resemble the concept of <i>nat bar nat</i> mentioned in the previous halachah.)<br>There are, however, other opinions (<i>Tosafot, Sefer HaTerumot</i>) which maintain that this ruling would apply even if the knife was clean. The rationale is the pungency of the food and the pressure of the knife cause it to absorb more than an ordinary instance of <i>nat bar nat</i>.<br>From the Rambam's wording, it appears that the entire radish is forbidden. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 96:1), however, rules that it is sufficient to remove a piece a fingerbreadth in thickness. The Rama, however, mentions the Rambam's view.</i> If, however, one cut meat [with a knife] and afterwards cut zucchini or watermelon,<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., substances that are not pungent and soft and contain moisture. If one cuts a vegetable that is not soft and moist, it is sufficient to wash it [<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 96:5)].</i> one should scrape away the place where the cut was made and the remainder may be eaten with milk.
We do not place a jar of salt near a jar of <i>kutach</i>, because it will draw out its flavor.<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad and the Radbaz note that the Rambam apparently had a slightly different version of <i>Chullin</i> 112a, the source for this halachah, than that found in the standard printed texts of the Talmud. According to the standard version, the rationale is that we fear that some drops of <i>kutach </i>will fall into the salt. The Radbaz adds that according to the Rambam, the prohibition applies only with regard to earthenware jugs. If they are made from metal, the material will be too dense to allow for the flavor to be drawn out.</i> Thus one will cook meat with this salt that has the flavor of milk. One may, however, place a jar of vinegar near a jar of <i>kutach</i>, because the vinegar will not draw out its flavor.<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">According to the other rationale, the <i>kutach</i> will remain a distinct entity if it falls into the salt, but it will become mixed with the vinegar and nullified if it falls into it (Radbaz; <i>Turei Zahav</i> 95:16).</i>
When a person eats cheese or milk first, it is permitted for him to eat meat directly afterwards. He must, however, wash his hands<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 89:2) states that if one sees that his hands are clean, it is not necessary to wash them.</i> and clean his mouth between the cheese and the meat.<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">The Rama quotes a view that requires one to wait six hours after eating hard cheese. It is, however, questionable if this would be required for most hard cheese commercially produced today.</i><br>With what should he clean his mouth? With bread or with fruit that [require him] to chew and then swallow or spit them out.<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) states that one should also wash his mouth.</i> One may clean his mouth with all substances with the exception of dates, flour, and vegetables, because they do not clean effectively.
When does the above apply? With regard to the meat of a domesticated animal or a wild beast.<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The requirement of this stringency for the meat of a domesticated animal is understandable, for the prohibition is of Scriptural origin. Nevertheless, according to the standard text of Halachah 4, the prohibition against a mixture of milk and the meat of a wild beast is also Rabbinic in origin. What then is the difference between the meat of a wild beast and that of a fowl? The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, however, explains that the meat of a domesticated animal resembles the meat of a wild beast. Hence it was necessary for the Rabbis to forbid it. Alternatively, Rabbenu Tam explains that the meat of a wild beast will stick to a person's mouth and hands more than the meat of a fowl.</i> If, however, one [desires to] eat the meat of a fowl after eating cheese or milk, it is not necessary for him to clean his mouth or wash his hands.<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Since only a Rabbinic prohibition is involved, our Sages did not enforce any further stringency.</i>
When a person ate meat first - whether the meat of an animal or the meat of a fowl - he should not partake of milk afterwards unless he waits the time for another meal, approximately six hours.<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">This is the view stated in the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 89:1) and in the Rama's conclusion, although the Rama does mention that there are some more lenient views.</i> This stringency is required because meat that becomes stuck between teeth and is not removed by cleaning.<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 89) gives a different rationale: that because meat is fatty, its taste persists for a long time.</i>

Chapter 10

All the prohibitions we mentioned involve living beings. There are also other Scriptural prohibitions that involve the produce of the earth. They include:<i> chadash, kilai hakerem, tevel,</i> and <i>orlah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam proceeds to define each of these terms in the subsequent halachot. The prohibitions the Rambam mentions in this chapter apply universally. There are other prohibitions involving the consumption of agricultural products that apply with regard to non-priests, e.g., <i>terumah</i>, as explained in <i>Sefer Zeraim</i> and others involving the sacrifices as explained in <i>Sefer HaAvodah</i> (Radbaz).</i>
What is meant by <i>chadash</i>?<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The term <i>chadash</i> literally means "new." It refers to new grain, i.e., grain that is harvested before the sixteenth of Nisan, as the Rambam proceeds to explain.</i> It is forbidden to partake of any of the five species of grain<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">These five species are commonly identified as wheat, barley, rye, oats, and spelt. As mentioned in the notes to <i>Hilchot Berachot</i> 3:1, there is some discussion whether these are in fact the species of which the Talmud and the Rambam speak.</i> alone<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., other species commonly identified as grain, e.g., corn, rice, and millet, are not included in this ban.</i> before the<i> omer</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">A measure of barley that is offered on the sixteenth of Nisan, as explained in Leviticus 23:9-15; <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i>, ch. 7.</i> is offered on the sixteenth of Nisan, as [Leviticus 23:14] states: "You may not partake of bread, roasted kernels, or fresh kernels."<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandments 189-191) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvot 303-305) include these prohibitions among the 613 mitzvot of the Torah. The Radbaz states that the Rambam mentions <i>chadash</i> before the other prohibitions, because it occurs most frequently, recurring every year.</i><br>Anyone who partakes of an olive-sized portion of fresh grain before the offering of the <i>omer</i> is liable for lashes. [This applies] in every place and at all times, whether in <i>Eretz [Yisrael</i>] or in the Diaspora,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">In the Diaspora, where crops of grain are sometimes planted after Pesach, this prohibition could present a problem. For all grain planted after Pesach, will not be permitted until the following year. In resolution, the Rama writes that unless we know otherwise, all grain is permitted after Pesach, based on the concept of <i>sefek-sefekah</i> - multiple doubt. It is possible it is from the previous year's crop. Even if it is from this year's crop, perhaps it took root before Pesach and is thus permitted.<br>The <i>Bayit Chadash</i> in his gloss to the <i>Tur</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 293) elaborates on the concept that the prohibition against <i>chadash</i> applies only to grain belonging to a Jew. Grain belonging to a non-Jew is not bound by this prohibition. The <i>Turei Zahav</i> 293:2 differs and maintains that it applies also to grain grown by a gentile. Most of the authorities respect the <i>Bayit Chadash</i> for his attempt to absolve most of the Jewish people from the prohibition (since by and large, the grain available in the Diaspora is grown by non-Jews) but accept the logic of the <i>Turei Zahav</i>. In practice, however, it is customary to rely on the leniency of the Rama.</i> whether at the time of the Temple or when the Temple is no longer standing.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the prohibition is not dependent on the offering of the <i>omer</i>, but instead applies even when no sacrifices are offered.</i><br>[The only difference in observance is that] while the Temple is standing, once the <i>omer</i> has been offered, it is permitted [to partake of] new grain in Jerusalem. Distant places<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., places that will not be able to hear a report when the <i>omer</i> was actually offered before noon because they are far removed from Jerusalem.</i> are permitted [to partake of new grain] after midday. For the court will not be indolent with regard to [the offering of <i>omer</i>] beyond midday.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., by midday, one can be certain that the <i>omer</i> had been sacrificed.</i> [Now] when the Temple is no longer standing, the entire day is forbidden according to Scriptural Law. In the present age, in the places where the festivals are observed for two days,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Kiddush HaChodesh</i>, ch. 5, which explains that in places where messengers from Jerusalem would not inform the Jewish community when the new month had been sanctified, the festivals are observed for two days, for perhaps the day considered as the fifteenth of Nisan was really the fourteenth.</i> <i>chadash</i> is forbidden on the entire day of the seventeenth of Nisan until the evening according to Rabbinic Law.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Since the observance of the second day was instituted because there was a doubt concerning the day on which the festival should be observed, our Sages ordained that the prohibition concerning <i>chadash</i> should be observed by Rabbinical decree until the conclusion of the seventeenth so as not to minimize the importance of the festival. Even though there is no longer any doubt concerning the day on which the festival should be observed, we heed this prohibition to maintain our Sages' original decree (Radbaz).</i>
When a person partakes of an olive-sized portion of bread from each of roasted kernels, or fresh kernels [from <i>chadash</i>], he is liable for three sets of lashes,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Provided he is given a separate warning for each one (Radbaz).</i> as [implied by the verse]: "You may not partake of bread, roasted kernels, or fresh kernels." According to the Oral Tradition, we learned that all three involve separate prohibitions.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">All three are mentioned in one verse. Thus one might think that we are speaking of a prohibition of a general nature and we follow the principle (see <i>Hilchot Sanhedrin</i> 18:2-3) that lashes are not given for a prohibition of a general nature. Nevertheless, <i>Keritot</i> 5a explains why this instance is an exception.</i>
Whenever grain took root before the offering of the <i>omer</i>, it is permitted to be eaten after the offering of the <i>omer</i> despite the fact that it did not reach maturity until after that offering. Grain that took root after the offering of the <i>omer</i> is forbidden until the offering of that sacrifice the following year even though it was planted before that offering was brought. This law applies in every place<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even in places that follow a different agricultural cycle than <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>.</i> and in every era according to Scriptural Law.
[There is an unresolved halachic difficulty in the following situations:] Grain took root after the <i>omer</i>. One harvested it and sowed this wheat in the ground. Afterwards, the <i>omer</i> of the following year was offered, while these kernels of wheat were still in the ground.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">And had not sprouted (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 293:4).</i> There is a doubt whether [the offering of] the <i>omer</i> caused [these kernels] to be permitted as if they had been stored in a jar or it did not cause them to be permitted, because they have been nullified in the ground.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">And thus they would not be permitted until the following year.</i> Therefore if a person collected them and partook of them, he is not liable for lashes, but [instead] is given stripes for rebellious conduct.<br>Similarly, when a stalk reached a third of its growth before the offering of the<i> omer</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Thus had one left it in the ground, it would have been permitted.</i> one uprooted it and then replanted it after the offering of the <i>omer, </i>and it increased in size. There is a doubt whether it is forbidden because of the increase in size until the offering of the <i>omer</i> the following year or it is not forbidden because it took root before the offering of the <i>omer</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 293:5) rules that such grain is forbidden because of the doubt.</i>
What is meant by <i>kilai hakerem</i>?<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The Hebrew term literally means "mixed species in a vineyard."</i> Sowing a species of grain or a type of vegetable together with a vine.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's statements have attracted the attention of the commentaries, for they represent a contradiction to his statements in <i>Hilchot Kelayim</i> 8:1: "One is not liable for sowing <i>kilai hakerem</i> unless he sows wheat, barley, and grape seeds together." The Radbaz explains that the laws governing sowing mixed species are different than those regarding partaking of them.</i> [This applies] whether they were sown by a Jew or a non-Jew,<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">For as above, the prohibition against sowing mixed species and partaking of them are distinct.</i> whether they grew on their own, or whether one planted a vine among vegetables, we are prohibited against partaking and benefiting from both of them, as [Deuteronomy 22:9] states: "Lest the fullness of the seed which you sowed and the produce of the vineyard become hallowed."<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 193) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 549) include this prohibition among the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> ["Becom[ing] hallowed"] means being set apart and forbidden.
One who eats<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The Torah does not specifically mention that it is forbidden to partake of the mixed species. Nevertheless, it states that it is forbidden to derive benefit from them. There is no greater derivation of benefit than eating (Radbaz). From the Rambam's wording, one would surmise that one is not liable for lashes from merely benefiting from <i>kilai hakerem</i>.</i> an olive-sized portion from <i>kilai hakerem</i>, whether from the vegetables or from the grapes is liable for lashes according to Scriptural Law. The two can be combined to reach this measure.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., when one eats some of the grapes and some of the vegetables, if the combined volume is the size of an olive, he is liable.</i>
When does the above<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Liability for lashes.</i> apply? When they were sown in <i>Eretz Yisrael.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Orlah</i> 3:10), the Rambam writes that this concept is derived from Deuteronomy 22:9: "Do not sow <i>kilayom</i> in your vineyard." For the only vineyard that can truly be considered as "yours," i.e., belonging to a Jew, is one in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>.<br>The <i>Mishneh LiMelech</i> writes that in the present era, when the observance of the agricultural laws in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> is only a matter of Rabbinic Law (see <i>Hilchot Terumah</i> 1:26), the prohibition against <i>kilai hakerem</i> is also of Rabbinic origin.</i> </i>In the Diaspora, by contrast, <i>kilai hakerem</i> are forbidden by Rabbinic decree.<br>In <i>Hilchot Kelayim</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">One of the <i>Halachot</i> in <i>Sefer Zeraim</i>.</i> it will be explained which species are forbidden as <i>kilai hakerem</i> and which are not forbidden, how they become forbidden, when they become forbidden, in which situations, produce causes vines to become "hallowed," and when they do not cause them to become "hallowed."
What is meant by <i>orlah</i>?<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The term literally means "covered." Thus the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Ma'aserot</i> 4:4) speaks of "something which covers (<i>oral</i>) its fruit." For that reason, a foreskin is described as an<i> orlah</i>, because it covers the male organ. Here also this fruit is "covered" by a forbidden quality.</i> Whenever anyone plants<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., replants an existing seedling or plants a seed and grows the tree from the outset (Radbaz).</i> a fruit tree, it is forbidden to partake of or benefit from all of the fruit the tree produces for three years after being planted, as [Leviticus 19:23] states: "For three years, they shall be closed off for you, they may not be eaten."<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 192) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 246) include this prohibition among the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> Whoever eats an olive-sized portion of such fruit is liable for lashes according to Scriptural Law.
When does this apply? When one plants in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, as [the above] verse states: "When you enter the land...." With regard to the prohibition against <i>orlah </i>in the Diaspora, it is a <i>halachah</i> transmitted to Moses at Sinai<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., an element of the Oral Tradition that has no explicit source in the Written Law.</i> that fruit that is definitely <i>orlah</i> is forbidden. If there is a doubt regarding the matter, it is permitted. In <i>Hilchot Ma'aaser Sheni</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">One of the <i>Halachot</i> in <i>Sefer Zeraim</i>.</i> it will be explained which [growths] are forbidden as <i>orlah</i> and which are permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., which type of planting or replanting incurs the prohibition against <i>orlah</i> and which does not.</i>
When there is a doubt whether produce is <i>orlah</i> or <i>kilai hakerem</i> in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, it is forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">A Scriptural prohibition is involved. Hence we follow the principle: Whenever a Scriptural prohibition is involved, we rule stringently.</i> In Syria, i.e., the lands that David conquered,<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <i>Hilchot Terumah</i> 1:3, King David conquered parts of Syria before he completed the conquest of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>. Hence, these lands were not considered as part of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> proper. Nevertheless, certain laws concerning the produce of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> were applied there by Rabbinic decree.</i> it is permitted.<br>What is implied? If there was a vineyard [containing] <i>orlah</i> and grapes were being sold outside it, or there were vegetables sown inside it<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Thus violating the prohibition against <i>kilai hakerem</i>.</i> and vegetables were being sold outside it, [in which instance,] there is a doubt whether [the grapes or the vegetables] came from it or from another place, in Syria, they are permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., permission is granted even in an instance were it is highly likely that the produce is prohibited.</i> In the Diaspora, even if one sees grapes being taken out from a vineyard that is <i>orlah</i> or vegetables being taken out from a vineyard, one may purchase them provided one does not actually see <i>orlah</i> being reaped or the vegetables being harvested [from the vineyard].
When there is a doubt whether a vineyard [contains] <i>orlah</i> or <i>kilai hakerem</i>, in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, it is forbidden. In Syria, it is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., one may purchase the produce even if one is certain it comes from the vineyard in question. Indeed, one may even pick the produce from that vineyard by oneself (Radbaz; <i>Hilchot Ma'aser Sheni</i> 10:11).</i> Needless to say, that ruling prevails in the Diaspora.
It is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">This represents the Rambam's interpretation of <i>Bava Batra</i> 24a. Others interpret that passage as stating that we are even permitted to partake of the wine. This is the view quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 294:11).<br><i>Tosafot</i> questions: Why isn't the wine forbidden as <i>yayin nesech</i>, wine touched by a gentile? They reply that we assume that the thieves were Jewish. Thus if there is reason to think that the thieves are gentile, the wine is forbidden (<i>Siftei Cohen</i> 294:22).</i> to drink a jug of wine that is found hidden in an orchard [whose produce is]<i> orlah</i>. It is permitted to benefit from it. [The rationale is] that a thief will not steal from a place and hide [what he stole] there. Grapes that are hidden there are forbidden, lest they have been harvested from there and stored away there.
[The following laws apply when] a gentile and a Jew are partners in planting [an orchard]. If at the beginning of the partnership, they agreed that the gentile would benefit from the produce during the years of <i>orlah</i> and the Jew would benefit from it for three years when the produce is permitted afterwards, it is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the gentile takes care of the orchard in the <i>orlah</i> years, and the Jew in the subsequent years. He is not considered as benefiting from the produce that is <i>orlah</i>, because it never belonged to him.</i> If they did not make such an agreement at the outset, it is forbidden [to make one afterwards].<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">For the crops that are <i>orlah</i> are considered to have become the Jew's property and he is bartering them. Rashi and Rabbenu Asher (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 22a) differ and permit such an arrangement to be made. They explain that although if such an arrangement is not made at the outset, it is forbidden to make it with regard to the Sabbath, different rules apply here. On the Sabbath, it is forbidden for the gentile to perform work on behalf of the Jew. In this instance, no such prohibition exists (<i>Siftei Cohen</i> 294:28). The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 294:13) follows the Rambam's approach, while the Rama follows that of Rabbenu Asher.</i> [Even when the agreement was made at the outset,] they may not make a reckoning.<br>What is meant [by making a reckoning]? I.e., to calculate the quantity of produce from which the gentile benefited in the years of <i>orlah</i> so that the Jew will benefit from exactly that amount. If they made such an agreement, it is forbidden, for this is exchanging the produce which is <i>orlah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Here also, the authorities who rule leniently as mentioned in the previous note would rule leniently (<i>Siftei Cohen</i> 294:29).</i>
It appears to me that the laws of <i>neta reva'i</i><sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">This term refers to the produce of the fourth year of a tree's growth. This produce must be taken to Jerusalem and eaten in a state of ritual purity or redeemed and the money taken to Jerusalem to be used for food to be eaten there.</i> do not apply in the Diaspora. Instead, one may eat the produce of the fourth year without redeeming it at all. Our Sages mentioned only <i>orlah</i>.<br>An extrapolation can be made from a more stringent instance to [this one] which is less stringent. In Syria, the laws governing the tithes and the Sabbatical year apply by Rabbinic decree,<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Terumot</i> 1:4; <i>Hilchot Shemitah</i> 4:27.</i> nevertheless, the laws governing <i>neta reva'i</i> do not apply, as will be explained in <i>Hilchot Ma'aser Sheni</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Ma'aser Sheni</i> 9:1.</i> Thus, in the Diaspoa,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Where the laws of the tithes and the Sabbatical year do not apply at all.</i> how much more so should [we conclude] that the laws governing <i>neta reva'i</i> do not apply.<br>In <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, however, these laws apply whether or not the Temple is standing.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's wording is somewhat misleading. The laws of <i>neta reva'i</i> are not dependent on the existence of the Temple. Even if the Temple is not standing, this mitzvah applies. Nevertheless, according to Scriptural Law, they apply only when the sanctity of <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> is intact. And according to Scriptural Law, that sanctity was nullified after the conquest of the land by the Assyrians and Babylonians. According to Rabbinic Law, however, these laws do apply in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> in the present age.</i> Some of the <i>Geonim</i> ruled that <i>kerem reva'i</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The harvest of a vineyard must be redeemed, but not that of any other type of tree.</i> alone must be redeemed in the Diaspora before it is permitted to be eaten. There is no basis for this [ruling].<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 294:7) quotes opinions that state that the laws applying to <i>neta reva'i</i> also apply in the Diaspora, as well as the Rambam's view that they do not apply. The <i>Tur</i> and the Rama quote the opinion of the <i>Geonim</i> who maintain that these laws apply with regard to a vineyard, but not with regard to any other types of produce.</i>
In <i>Eretz Yisrael</i>, it is forbidden to eat any of the produce of the fourth year<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz emphasizes that even if the fourth year passes, fruit which grew during that year is forbidden in the fifth year until it is redeemed. This is also included as one of the 613 mitzvot. The Rambam, however, lists that mitzvah in <i>Hilchot Ma'aser Sheni</i>.</i> until it is redeemed.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., in the present era. See the following note.</i> In <i>Hilchot Ma'aser Sheni</i>, we will explain the laws governing the redemption [of the produce], how it should be eaten, and when we begin calculating the growth of a tree with regard to <i>orlah</i> and [<i>netah] reva'i</i>.
How is produce which is <i>neta reva'i</i> redeemed in the present age?<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Ma'aser Sheni</i>, the Rambam explains that in the era of the Temple, the produce would be taken to be eaten in Jerusalem in a state of ritual purity (or redeemed for its value and that money taken to Jerusalem to be used to purchase food to be eaten there in a state of ritual purity). Since that is not possible in the present era, different laws apply.</i> After [the produce] is collected, one recites the blessing: Blessed are You, God, our Lord, King of the earth, who has sanctified us with His commandments and commanded us to redeem <i>neta reva'i</i>. Afterwards, one redeems the entire crop even with one <i>p'rutah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">A copper coin of minimal value. In the era of the Temple, it was necessary to redeem the produce for its value and add a fifth (<i>Hilchot Ma'aser Sheni</i> 4:1,9). In the present era, since there is no opportunity to use the money as required, a <i>p'rutah</i> is sufficient for one's entire harvest (<i>Arachin</i> 29a).</i> This <i>p'rutah</i> is then cast into the Dead Sea.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a place where no one will benefit from it. In <i>Hilchot Ma'aser Sheni</i> 2:2, the Rambam states that the <i>p'rutah</i> should be cast into the Mediterranean Sea. At times, the term <i>Yam HaMelech</i> which is commonly translated as the Dead Sea is used to refer to the Mediterranean. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 294:6) mentions the Mediterranean. It also states that one may grind the coin into dust.</i><br>Alternatively, one may transfer the holiness to other produce that is worth a <i>p'rutah</i> by saying:<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Seemingly, the wording of the blessing should also be altered as indicated by <i>Hilchot Ma'aser Sheni</i> 4:3.</i> "The holiness of all of this produce is transferred to this wheat," "...to this barley," or the like.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">This is also a leniency granted in the present age. In the era of the Temple, at the outset, one should not transfer the holiness from one species of produce to another (<i>Hilchot Ma'aser Sheni</i> 4:2).</i> Afterwards, one burns the [latter quantity of produce] so that they will not cause difficulty to others.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">These kernels of grain must also be eaten in Jerusalem in a state of ritual purity. Since this is impossible, they should be destroyed, lest another person transgress by eating them elsewhere.</i> He may then partake of all the produce [he harvested].
Some of the <i>Geonim</i> ruled that even though one redeemed the produce of the fourth year or transferred its holiness, it is forbidden to partake of it until the entrance of the fifth year. This ruling has no foundation. It appears to me that it is in error. [Although Leviticus 19:25] states: "And in the fifth year, you shall partake of its produce," the intent of the verse is that in the fifth year you will partake of its produce without redeeming it like any ordinary produce in the world. One should not heed the above ruling.
What is meant by <i>tevel</i>? Any produce from which one is obligated to separate <i>terumah</i> and tithes is called <i>tevel</i> before one separates these portions.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Before produce may be eaten, a person must separate <i>bikkurim</i> (the first fruits), <i>terumah gedolah</i> (a small portion - 1/40 to 1/60 of the produce) which is given to the priests, <i>ma'aser</i> (tithes, which is given to the Levites), and <i>ma'aser sheni</i> (the second tithe, which is eaten in a state of purity in Jerusalem) or <i>ma'aser oni</i> (the tithe given to the poor). From the tithe which the Levites receive, they must give a tithe to the priests as <i>terumat ma'aser</i>.</i> [In that state,] it is forbidden to partake of it,<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 153) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 284) include this prohibition among the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> as [Leviticus 22:16] states: "And they shall not desecrate the sacraments of the children of Israel which they will dedicate to God," i.e., one should not treat them in an ordinary manner while the sacred elements that will be separated in the future have not yet been separated.<br>When a person<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">There is a question if this prohibition applies even to a priest who would later be permitted to partake of <i>terumah</i>. To explain: There is a discussion among the<i> Achronim</i> if <i>tevel</i> is considered as an independent prohibition or if it is forbidden because of <i>terumah</i> that has not been separated (see <i>Tzaphnat Paneach; Atvan D'Oraita</i>). According to the former view, even a priest is liable, while according to the second view, there is room for leniency.</i> partakes of an olive-sized portion of <i>tevel</i> before he separates <i>terumah gedolah</i> and <i>terumat ma'aser,</i> he is liable for death by the hand of heaven,<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he will die before his time.</i> as [Leviticus 22:15-16] states: "And they shall not desecrate the sacraments of the children of Israel... And they will bear the sin of guilt."
When, however, one partakes of food from which <i>terumah gedolah </i>and <i>terumat ma'aser</i><sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Terumat Ma'aser</i> should be separated after the tithes are separated. Nevertheless, after the fact, if one separated it beforehand, the separation is valid.</i> have been separated, but from which tithes - even the tithes of the poor have not been separated - he is worthy of lashes for partaking of <i>tevel</i>. He is not worthy of death. For only with regard to<i> terumah gedolah</i> and <i>terumat ma'aser</i> is the sin worthy of death.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad explains the Rambam's ruling on the basis of the prooftext cited in the previous halachah which puts an emphasis on "the sacraments of the children of Israel." That term refers to <i>terumah</i>. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, however, notes that the second tithe is also referred to as "a sacrament." The <i>Lechem Mishneh</i>, however, offers a resolution.</i>
The warning against partaking of <i>tevel</i> from which tithes were not separated is included in [Deuteronomy 12:17 which] states: "You may not eat the tithes of grain... in your gates."<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Although that prohibition forbids eating the second tithe outside of Jerusalem, it also has this intent.</i><br>In <i>Hilchot Terumot</i> and <i>Hilchot Ma'aserot</i>, it will be explained which produce is obligated to have <i>terumah</i> and the tithes [separated from it] and which is not, when does the obligation stem from Scriptural Law and when is it Rabbinic. When a person partakes of an olive-sized portion of produce that is <i>tevel</i> according to Rabbinic Law<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., produce that grows in a flower pot without a hole on the bottom.</i> or <i>kilai hakerem</i> or <i>orlah</i> from the Diaspora,<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 8 and 10.</i> he is liable for stripes for rebellious conduct.
The juices that come from produce that is <i>tevel, chadash,</i> consecrated to the Temple, growths of the Sabbatical year, <i>kilayim</i>, and <i>orlah</i> are forbidden as they are. One is not, however, liable for lashes for partaking of them.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> (in his gloss to Chapter 8, Halachah 16) states that this is not the ordinary way which one benefits from such produce. The <i>Lechem Mishneh</i> explains that other juices, in contrast to wine and oil, are not considered to have the substance of the fruit.</i> Exceptions are wine and oil that are <i>orlah</i> and wine that is <i>kilai hakerem</i>. One is liable for lashes for them just as one is liable for lashes for the olives and grapes,
There are other prohibitions involving foods applicable to consecrated entities. They are all of Scriptural origin, e.g., there are prohibitions against partaking of <i>terumot</i>, the first fruits, <i>challah,</i> and the second tithe. And there are prohibitions involving sacrifices consecrated [to be offered] on the altar, e.g., <i>piggul</i>, sacrificial meat that remains past its time, and sacrifices that have become impure.<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 18 of <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMekedashim</i> for a definition of these prohibitions.</i> All of these [prohibitions] will be explained in the appropriate place.
The measure for which one is liable - whether for lashes or for <i>kerait</i> is an olive-sized portion. We have already explained the prohibition against [partaking of] leaven on Pesach and the [relevant] laws in <i>Hilchot Chametz UMatzah</i>. The prohibition against eating on Yom Kippur is a different type of prohibition.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">In all other instances, it is the substance (<i>cheftzah</i>) that is forbidden. On Yom Kippur, the prohibition does not apply to the substance, but to the person (the <i>gavra</i>). He is forbidden to partake of all foods.</i> The prohibition against all products of the vine that applies to a Nazirite does not apply equally to all. Therefore, all of these prohibitions, the measure for which one is liable, and the [relevant] laws are explained in the appropriate place.

Chapter 11

When wine has been poured as a libation to a false divinity,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <i>Hilchot Avodat Kochavim</i> 3:3, pouring a libation and sacrificing are among the four acts of service for which one is liable to any false deity, even if this is not its mode of service.</i> it is forbidden to benefit from it. A person who drinks even the smallest quantity<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See the following halachah.</i> of [such wine] is liable for lashes according to Scriptural Law. Similarly, anyone who partakes of the smallest quantity of something offered to a false deity, e.g., meat or fruit, even water or salt, is worthy of lashes, as [implied by Deuteronomy 32:38]: "The fat of whose offerings they would eat; they would drink the wine of their libations. Let them stand."<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 194) includes this prohibition among the 613 mitzvot of the Torah. According to the Rambam, it is actually the last of the mitzvot which the Torah mentions.<br>Although the verse does not specifically mention a prohibition, the Rambam derives the prohibition as follows: As stated in <i>Hilchot Avodat Kochavim</i> 7:2, we are forbidden to derive benefit from anything offered to a false deity. Since the prooftext quoted establishes an equation between a libation and an offering, we conclude that just as an offering is forbidden by a negative commandment; so, too, there is a negative commandment involving a libation (see <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 29b).<br>The Ramban (in his <i>Hasagot</i> to <i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i>) and the <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (Mitzvah 111) maintain that both are included in a single prohibition. They should not be counted as separate negative commandments. They all agree, however, that the prohibition against such wine is Scriptural in origin. As the Rambam explains in <i>Sefer HaMitzvot, loc. cit.</i>, there are statements of our Sages that appear to imply that the prohibition is Rabbinic in origin. Those statements, however, apply to wine handled by gentiles (see Halachah 3) and not to wine that was actually used for a libation.</i>
Wine poured as a libation to a false deity is like a sacrifice offered to it. Since this prohibition stems from [the prohibition against] the worship of false deities, there is no minimum measure involved, as stated with regard to the worship of false deities [<i>ibid.</i> 13:18]: "Let no trace of the condemned [entity] cling to your hand."<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">This verse is most particularly related to the prohibition against benefiting from the property of a city who were drawn after idol worship (<i>ir hanidachat</i>). Nevertheless, since all false deities can be considered as "condemned," the verse applies to them as well (<i>Megillat Esther, Sefer HaMitzvot</i>, negative commandment 25). The expression "any trace" implies even the slightest amount of benefit is prohibited.</i>
When we do not know whether wine belonging to a gentile was used for a libation or not, it is called "ordinary [gentile] wine." It is forbidden to benefit from it, as it is forbidden to benefit from wine used as a libation. [This matter] is a Rabbinic decree.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">We find an allusion to this decree in Scripture itself for Daniel 1:8 speaks of how Daniel refrained from drinking the king's wine. <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 36b states that the decree against drinking wine handled by gentiles was instituted lest this lead to familiarity and ultimately, to intermarriage. From the Rambam's wording in the following halachah, however, it would appear that the prohibition was instituted as a safeguard against benefiting from idolatry (<i>Ma'aseh Rokeach</i>; see also Halachah 7 and notes).</i> When a person drinks a <i>revi'it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">One fourth of a <i>log</i>, 86 cc. According to <i>Shiurei Torah</i> and 150 cc. According to <i>Chazon Ish</i>. This is the standard liquid measure involved in ritual matters.</i> of "ordinary [gentile] wine," he is liable for "stripes for rebellious conduct."<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">It is forbidden to drink even the slightest amount, but one is liable only for drinking a <i>revi'it</i> (<i>Lechem Mishneh</i>).</i>
It is forbidden [to benefit from] any wine that a gentile touches;<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 12, Halachot 1-2, which define what is meant by a gentile touching wine. As implied by the contrast to the following halachah, for it to be forbidden to benefit from the wine, the gentile must touch it intentionally. Similarly, he must know that it is wine (Radbaz).</i> for perhaps he poured it as a libation. For the thought of a gentile is focused on the worship of false deities.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore even if there is no false deity present, it is possible that the gentile intended to use it as a libation. See Halachah 7 and notes which discuss which gentiles we are referring to.</i> From this, we learn that it is forbidden to benefit [even from] wine belonging to a Jew which was touched by a gentile; it is governed by the laws that apply to ordinary gentile wine.
When a gentile touches wine unintentionally<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 12, Halachah 5.</i> and similarly, when a gentile child<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Here the term child is not defined chronologically, but in terms of his relation to idolatry. Does he praise the name of a false deity or not? [<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 57a; <i>Tur</i>, <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 124:1)].</i> touches wine, it is forbidden to drink it,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">In both instances, we cannot say that the person had the intent to use the wine as a libation. In the first instance, he did not intend to touch the wine and in the second, the child does not know about idolatrous worship. Nevertheless, the wine is still forbidden as a safeguard. See Chapter 12, Halachah 5. See also the Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 124:24) who states that in the present era, most gentiles are not idolaters. Even so, if they touch wine unintentionally, although there are authorities who say there is room for leniency, the prevailing custom is to be stringent unless a significant loss is involved. See <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 124:71.</i> but it is permitted to benefit from it.<br>When one purchases servants from a gentile and they were circumcised and immersed [in the <i>mikveh</i>] immediately,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Thus reaching the intermediate stage of Jewish servants, as <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 12:11 states, they "have departed from the category of gentiles, but have yet to enter the category of Jews."</i> they no longer pour libations to false deities.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Their "conversion" to Judaism will prevent them from offering such a libation. See the <i>Tur</i> who also mentions the opinion of Rabbenu Chananel who maintains that a gentile servant causes wine to be forbidden for twelve months.<br>The <i>Tur</i> clarifies that the debate concerns only a servant, because his acceptance of Judaism is forced. All agree that no such strictures apply to a convert who willingly accepts Judaism.</i> It is permitted to drink wine which they touch even though they have yet to conduct themselves according to the Jewish faith and they still speak of idolatry.
[With regard to] the children of gentile maidservants that were born in a Jewish domain<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">If they were not born in a Jewish domain, the circumcision alone is of no consequence and even minors cause wine to become forbidden to drink (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> and circumcised, but were not immersed yet:<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">If they were not immersed yet, even young children cause wine they touch to become forbidden to drink (the <i>Kessef Mishneh's</i> interpretation of the Rambam's opinion). The Rashba, however, differs and maintains even if these children were neither circumcised or immersed, they do not cause wine to be forbidden. The <i>Turei Zahav</i>124:3 and the <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 124:9 differ and maintain that even the Rambam would accept the Rashba's approach.</i> The older ones cause wine that they touch to become forbidden. The younger ones<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Here the term child is not defined chronologically, but in terms of his relation to idolatry. Does he praise the name of a false deity or not? [<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 57a; <i>Tur</i>, <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 124:1)].</i> do not cause it to become forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it is permitted entirely, even to drink it.</i>
With regard to a resident alien, i.e., one who accepted the observance of the seven universal laws [commanded to Noah and his descendants],<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibitions against the worship of false deities, blasphemy, murder, theft, incest and adultery, eating the flesh of a living animal, and the obligation to establish courts. See <i>Hilchot Melachim</i> 8:10.</i> as we explained:<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 14:7.</i> It is forbidden to drink his wine, but it is permitted to benefit from it.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that there are two dimensions to the prohibition against drinking the wine of gentiles:<br>a) The desire to limit familiarity with gentiles, lest it lead to intermarriage. This applies to resident aliens as well. Therefore there is a prohibition against drinking their wine.<br>b) A safeguard against benefiting from wine used as libations. This does not apply with regard to resident aliens. Therefore there is no prohibition against deriving benefit from their wine.</i> We may deposit wine in his possession for a short time, but may not entrust it to him for a lengthy period.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Since we do not suspect that he will use the wine for a libation - or allow other gentiles to do so - we do not forbid one to leave it there for a short while. Nevertheless, if it is left there for a long time, we fear that the gentile will exchange it with his own wine and as stated above, it is forbidden for Jews to drink his own wine (see Rashi, <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 64b).</i><br>With regard to any gentile who does not serve false deities, e.g., the Arabs:<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's wording has attracted the attention of the commentaries, for from the beginning of the halachah, it appears that the gentile must accept all seven mitzvot, while this clause appears to imply that it is sufficient for him to accept only the prohibition against idolatry. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that when the entire nation does not worship false deities, then we do not fear that wine will be used as a libation. When, however, that is not the case, a gentile must accept all seven mitzvot for his wine to be permitted.</i> It is forbidden to drink his wine, but it is permitted to benefit from it. The <i>Geonim</i> rule in this manner. With regard to those who worship false deities,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation follows the standard version of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. The uncensored text reads: "Christians, by contrast, are idolaters. It is forbidden to benefit...." The Rama (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 155:1) rules that Christianity violates only the prohibition against<i> shituf</i>, worshipping another entity together with God, and gentiles are not prohibited against such worship. It must be emphasized that today, though many gentiles are nominally Christian, their observance is minimal and they have an awareness of monotheism.<br>See also the statements of the Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 123:1, 124:24) who quotes opinions that maintain that in the present age, it is not customary for gentiles to pour wine as libations to false deities. Nevertheless, the prohibition against drinking such wine, however, remains intact.</i> by contrast, it is forbidden to benefit from their ordinary wine.
Whenever it is stated that wine is forbidden in this context, if the gentile who causes the wine to be forbidden worships false deities, it is forbidden to benefit from it. If he does not worship false deities, it is merely forbidden to drink it. Whenever we refer to a gentile without any further description, we mean one who worships false deities.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">For in the Rambam's age, most gentiles were idolaters. The Rabbinic authorities question whether one can make such an assumption in the present age. For many gentiles do not worship according to any religious rites at all and others, like the Arabs, have a conception of monotheism.</i>
Only wine that is fit to be offered on the altar is used for libations for false deities. Therefore when [our Sages] decreed against ordinary gentile wine, ordaining that it is forbidden to benefit from any wine touched by a gentile, their decree involved only wine that is fit to be used as a libation. Accordingly, wine that was boiled<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeach</i> 6:9 states that wine that was cooked to the extent that its taste changed is forbidden to be used as a libation on the altar. To put the concept in contemporary terms, wine that was pasteurized is included in this category.</i> that was touched by a gentile is not forbidden. It is permitted to drink it together with a gentile<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Avodah Zarah</i> 30b relates that the Sage Shmuel actually drank boiled wine together with a gentile.<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> quotes Rabbenu Asher who asks: If the decree against wine touched by a gentile was instituted to prevent intermarriage, what difference does it make if it was boiled or not? Will boiling the wine prevent familiarity from arising with gentiles?<br>In resolution, he explains that perhaps since boiled wine is uncommon, our Sages did not apply their decree in such a situation. Even though today, it has become common to drink boiled - i.e., pasteurized wine - our Sages decree has not been expanded. It must be emphasized that this leniency applies to wine belonging to a Jew that was boiled. Wine belonging to a gentile becomes forbidden before it is boiled and thus cannot be drunken.</i> in one cup. If, however, [a gentile] touches wine blended [with water] and wine that began to turn into vinegar,<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Although they are unfit to be used for a libation.</i> but can still be drunken it is forbidden.
The <i>Geonim</i> of the west ruled that if a small amount of a sweetener<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">This includes any wine to which sugar was added.</i> or yeast became mixed with Jewish wine, since it is no longer fit for the altar,<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeach</i> 6:9.</i> it is considered as if were boiled or as if it were beer and will not be used as a libation. It is permitted to drink it together with a gentile.
When does wine belonging to a gentile become forbidden? When the grapes have been crushed and the wine begins to flow,<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">In their days, grape presses were built on an incline, so that after the grapes were pressed, the juice would flow naturally toward a cistern.</i> even though it has not descended into the cistern and is still in the wine press, it is forbidden. For this reason, we do not crush grapes together with a gentile in a wine press,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Turei Zahav</i> 123:14 states that some interpret the Rambam as speaking only about a winepress that is open. If it is plugged close, there is room to say that the prohibition does not apply. Nevertheless, the <i>Turei Zahav</i> quotes other views that maintain that the prohibition applies even in such an instance.</i> lest he touch it with his hand<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Implied is that a libation cannot be offered with one's feet (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i> based on <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 56b; the <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 123:43, however, maintains that this is not the correct understanding of the Rambam's words). The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 124:11), however, rules that a gentile who touches the wine with his feet causes it to be come forbidden. The Rama, however, rules leniently and maintains that the prohibition applies only to drinking such wine.</i> and offer it as a libation. [This applies] even if he is bound. [Similarly,] we do not purchase a wine press [filled with] crushed [grapes] even if the wine is still mixed with the seeds and peels and has not descended into the cistern.
When a gentile crushes [grapes for] wine without touching it<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Turei Zahav</i> 124:17 interprets this as referring to an instance where he does not touch the wine at all, not even with his feet.
