The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, however, explains that this is referring to a situation where the gentile touches the wine with his feet, but not with his hands.</i> and a Jew is standing over him,<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">And watching that the gentile does not touch it.</i> and a Jew is the one who collects it in jugs, it is forbidden [only] to be drunken.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">It is, however, permitted to benefit from it.</i>
It is forbidden to benefit from vinegar belonging to a gentile, because it became [forbidden like] wine offered as a libation before it became vinegar.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The fact that it becomes vinegar afterwards does not cause it to become permitted.The Radbaz states that one can conclude from the Rambam's wording that if a gentile touches vinegar belonging to a Jew, it is permitted, for it is no longer wine.</i><br>When a gentile is crushing grapes in a barrel, we are not concerned that the wine [becomes forbidden] as wine used for a libation. If a gentile was eating from the baskets [of grapes brought to a winepress] and left over, a <i>se'ah</i> or two and threw them into the winepress, he does not cause the wine [to become forbidden] as wine used for a libation, even though it spatters over the grapes.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on authoritative manuscripts and early printings of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. The standard published text is difficult to understand. As the Radbaz and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 125:6) state, the Rambam is referring to a situation where a certain amount of grape juice collects in the bottom of the baskets. Even though that juice spatters of the grapes, it does not cause the wine to be considered forbidden, for this prohibition does not apply until the wine begins to flow, as stated in Halachah 11 (Radbaz).</i>
Grape seeds and peels belonging to a gentile are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Even to benefit from them [<i>Kessef Mishneh; Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 123:14)]. See also the Rama who states that the prohibition applies only when the peels were in contact with gentile wine. If the gentiles had merely crushed the grapes, but the wine had not begun flowing from the winepress, the peels are not forbidden.</i> for twelve months. After twelve months, they have already dried out; they contain no moisture and they are permitted to be eaten. Similarly, the dregs of wine that have dried out are permitted to be eaten after twelve months.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">See the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>ibid. </i>) which quotes more stringent views in certain circumstances.</i> [The rationale is that] no trace of wine remains; they are just like dust or earth.
It is forbidden to put wine in wineskins or barrels in which gentiles had kept wine<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">For a certain quantity of wine is absorbed in the container. Afterwards, when the kosher wine is placed in the container, it will be soaked into the container and the wine in the container will be released into it.</i> until:<br>a) they are allowed to dry for twelve months;<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">See the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 135:16) which explains that even if one used the containers for water during this period, this does not prevent the containers from becoming permitted.</i><br>b) they are placed in a fire until their pitch becomes soft or they become hot;<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if they are not covered with tar (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>). By heating them, one will achieve the results of <i>libbun</i> and purge any absorbed wine through heat.<br>See also the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 135:15) which state that <i>hagalah</i>, filling the containers with boiling water is also acceptable.</i><br>c) water is placed in them for three days for a full 24 hour period; [one places water in them], pours it out after 24 hours, and puts other water in. [This should be done] three times, [once a day] for three days.<br>[This applies] whether the containers belong to [the non-Jews] or they belonged to a Jew from whom [the non-Jews] borrowed them and then placed their wine into them. If one put wine in them before purifying them, it is forbidden to drink [that wine].<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">It is, however, permitted to benefit from this wine (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i>
It is permitted to place beer, fish brine, or fish oil in these containers immediately.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">For these substances nullify the taste of wine (Rashi, <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 33b).</i> None of these [purging processes] are necessary. After one placed fish brine or fish oil in them, one may place wine in them, for the salt [in the fish brine or fat] will burn out [any residue of wine].
When a person purchases new utensils that were not covered with pitch from a gentile, he may place wine in them immediately, he need not worry that gentile wine had been placed in them. If they were covered with pitch, he should wash them thoroughly even though they are new.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even though they appear new, we suspect that a gentile used them to store wine. Hence they must be washed. Nevertheless, the fact that they appear new indicates that they were not used for a long time. Hence, washing them is sufficient.<br>This stringency applies only with regard to containers covered with pitch. Since they are dark black, it is not evident whether they were used previously or not. With regard to other containers, it is much more clearly apparent whether or not they were used. Hence there is no need for this stringency (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i><br>Similarly, [any] utensil in which gentile wine was placed, but was not stored there for an extensive period, e.g., a bucket used to draw wine from a cistern, a funnel, or the like, should have water swished in them. That is sufficient for it.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> notes that <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 74b appears to require that such utensils be dried. He questions why the Rambam does not mention this point. As a possible resolution, he suggests that perhaps the Talmud is speaking about utensils belonging to a gentile, while the Rambam is speaking about those belonging to a Jew.</i>
Similarly, it is forbidden to drink from an earthenware cup that a gentile had drunk from. If one washed it thoroughly three times, it is permitted, for all traces of wine have been washed away.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's ruling is dependent on his interpretation of <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 33b. Other authorities including Rashi and the Ra'avad have a different understanding of the passage. Their view is cited by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 135:4).</i> This applies provided it is glazed with lead as potters do or covered with pitch. If, however, it is of earthenware, washing it thoroughly [once] is [all that is] required.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that we are speaking of an instance where the glazing of the lead or the pitch was not completed in a thorough manner and the surface of the utensil is not smooth. Therefore such a utensil will absorb wine more easily than an ordinary earthenware utensil. Hence, three washings are required. The following halachah, by contrast, is speaking about a utensil that is glazed in a more thorough manner, producing a smooth surface. Hence it is less likely to absorb the wine than an ordinary earthenware utensil.</i>
When earthenware utensils that are glazed with lead<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned above, the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> interprets this to mean that they were glazed in a manner that produced a smooth surface. Hence they do not absorb the wine easily.</i> are used for gentile wine, they are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">After being washed alone.</i> if they are white, red, or black. If they are green, they are forbidden, because they absorb.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">In order to produce a green color, a substance called <i>netar</i>, alum crystals [Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 135:5)], is mixed into the glazing. This substance is very absorbent. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah, loc. cit.</i>) states that utensils made from this substance can never be purified.</i> If they have a portion where the earthenware is revealed,<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the glazing does not cover the entire utensil.</i> they are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Until the wine is purged as mentioned in Halachah 15.</i> whether they are white or green, because they absorb.<br>It appears to me that this ruling applies only when wine was placed in them for long term storage.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> quotes the Rashba as stating that from the fact that these statements are made about earthenware utensils, one can conclude that metal utensils do not absorb even when gentile wine was placed in them for an extended period of time. They will absorb only when liquids are heated.</i> If, however, it was not placed in them for long term storage, [it is necessary merely to] wash them.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">For even if the wine was not placed in them for an extended period, it is possible that there will be a certain amount of residue left in the container.</i> They are then permitted, even if they are earthenware.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">For over a short period of time, they will not absorb.</i>
When a gentile treads on grapes in a winepress of stone or of wood<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">That is not covered with pitch (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>) .</i> or a gentile applied pitch to a winepress of stone<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 74b) and the <i>Tur</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 138:1) explain that after pitch is applied to a vat, a small amount of wine is placed in the vat to remove the unfavorable odor of the pitch.</i> even though he did not tread the grapes there, one must wash [the press] thoroughly with water and ashes<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., rubbing the walls with ashes and then washing them (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> four times. Afterwards, one may tread grapes there. If [the press] is still moist, one should place the ashes in before the water. If it is not moist, one should place the water in first.
When a gentile treaded [grapes] in a stone winepress covered with pitch or [applied] pitch to a wooden winepress<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Since a larger amount of pitch is necessary, it will absorb more.</i> even though he did not tread grapes there, one must peel the pitch.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 138:1) follows the position of other <i>Rishonim</i> who maintains that even after peeling the outer layer of the pitch, one must apply ashes and water, as stated in the previous halachah.</i> If one left it for twelve months or placed water in it for three days, it is not necessary to peel [the pitch off].<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">As stated with regard to barrels in Halachah 15. The Ra'avad and most other<i> Rishonim</i> differ with regard to this ruling and require the barrel to be pealed.</i> [The laws applying to] a winepress need not be more stringent than those applying to barrels.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Indeed, one would suspect the laws governing barrels to be more stringent, for wine is stored there for long periods. It remains in a winepress, by contrast, for only a short time.</i> [The option of] peeling was given only to allow [the winepress to be used] immediately.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Without having to wait any time at all.</i>
An earthenware winepress [is governed by more stringent rules].<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">For earthenware absorbs more readily than other substances. In the previous halachah, we assume that the winepress itself did not absorb any wine. In this case, we assume that it did (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> Even if one peels the pitch, it is forbidden to tread grapes in it immediately. [Instead, one must] heat it with fire until the pitch softens. If, however, one leaves it for twelve months or places water in it for three successive days, it is permitted,<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Without peeling off the pitch as stated in the previous halachah. Here also the Ra'avad differs and rules that the pitch must be peeled off. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) follows the Rambam's ruling.</i> as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">In Halachah 15.</i>
[The following laws apply to] a filter that had been used for wine belonging to a gentile. If it is made of hair, it should be washed thoroughly<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Hair does not absorb liquid at all. Hence, it need only be washed to remove the wine that may be sticking to its surface.</i> and then it may be used as a filter. If it is made from wool, it should be washed thoroughly four times with water and ashes and then left<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation follows the authoritative manuscripts and early printings of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. The standard printed text differs slightly.</i> until it dries. If it was from flax,<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Which is more absorbant.</i> it should be left for twelve months. If it has knots, they should be untied [before the filter is washed out].<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">So that the residue will not collect there.</i><br>Similar [laws apply with regard to] utensils from reeds,<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 135:8).</i> from date bast, or similar utensils like wicker baskets that are used to tread grapes. If they were sewed with ropes, they should be washed thoroughly. If they are tangled together with snarls that are difficult to undo, they should be washed four times with ashes and with water. [After] they are dried, they may be used. If they are sewed with flax, they should be left unused for twelve months. If they have knots, they should be untied.<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">When citing this law in his <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 138:7, 9), Rav Yosef Caro does not mention the need to untie the knots in this instance. As evident from his <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, he follows the approach of the Rashba who maintains that when an object is left for twelve months, there is no need to untie the knots. The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 138:8 differs and states that the Rambam's ruling should be followed.</i>
How can the utensils of a winepress used by a gentile for gentile wine be purified so that a Jew may use them? The boards,<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Upon which the grapes are placed.</i> the balls of clay,<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Used to crush the grapes (see the conclusion of the gloss of the <i>Lechem Mishneh</i> to Halachah 17).</i> and the palm branches<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Which are used as brooms to collect the grapes (Rashi, <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 75a).</i> should be washed thoroughly. The restraints<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishneh, <i>Taharot</i> 10:8, the Rambam states that this refers to the restraints placed around olives (and grapes) when they are being squeezed to gather them together.</i> of wood and of canvas should be dried out.<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">This term refers to the process of applying ashes and water mentioned above.</i> Those from water grasses and from bullrushes should be left unused for twelve months.<br>If one desires to purify them immediately, he should place them in boiling water,<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">To purge the wine absorbed in this fashion.</i> seal them with water used to cook olives,<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Cooking them in such water will cause whatever wine that was absorbed to be sealed in its place and never to be released.</i> or place them under a drain through which water flows continually or in a stream of running water for twelve hours.<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">This will also purge the absorbed wine. See <i>Hilchot Tumat Ochalin</i> 11:17 which mentions these same processes in a different context.</i> Afterwards, they are permitted.
In the era when the land of Israel was entirely within the possession of the Jewish people, it was permitted to purchase wine from any Jewish person without holding anyone in suspicion.<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">A Jew who worships false divinities, does not observe the Sabbath, or denies the Torah and its mitzvot is considered equivalent to a gentile and his wine is forbidden just as a gentile's is (see <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 30:15). When <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> was populated solely by Jews, our Sages maintained that there was no need to suspect that a person fell into the above categories.</i> In the Diaspora, they would only purchase [wine] from a person whose reputation [for observance] has been established. In the present age, in every place, we only purchase wine from a person whose reputation for observance has been established.<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad objects to the Rambam's ruling, stating that a common person will not necessarily cause another to transgress. The Radbaz states that even if the common people will not necessarily transgress themselves, they will not be careful about protecting another person's observance and may sell him forbidden articles. This view is cited by the Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 119:1).</i> These laws also apply to meat, cheese, and a cut of fish that does not have a sign as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 3, Halachah 21; Chapter 8, Halachah 7.</i>
When a person enjoys the hospitality of a homeowner in any place and at any time and that homeowner brings him wine, meat, cheese, or a piece of fish, it is permitted. There is no need to inquire concerning it.<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">We assume that the host is observant and that he is giving his guest the same food that he eats himself (Radbaz).</i> [This law applies] even if he does not know him at all; all that he knows is that he is Jewish.<br>If [the host] has an established reputation for non-observance and for not paying attention to these matters, it is forbidden to accept his hospitality. If one transgresses and accepts his hospitality, it is forbidden to eat meat and drink wine [despite] his assurances unless a person who has an established reputation for observance testifies [to their acceptability].

Chapter 12

How do we define the term touch when we say that a gentile<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">As the Rambam stated in Chapter 11, Halachah 8, unless otherwise specified, when he uses the term "gentile," he is referring to an idolater.</i> who touches wine causes it to be forbidden? Touching the wine itself whether with his hands<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Or with an article held in his hand [<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 124:11)].</i> or with any of his other limbs with which it is customary to pour a libation<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">As evident from Chapter 11, Halachah 11, according to the Rambam, it is not customary to pour a libation with one's feet. Note, however, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 124:11) which forbids wine that a gentile touched with his feet. The Rama, however, quotes the Rambam's view.</i> and shook the wine.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, he did not cause the wine to move, it is forbidden to drink it, but one is allowed to benefit from it (Radbaz). In his <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, however, Rav Yosef Caro notes that although there are authorities who agree with the ruling of the Radbaz, from the Rambam's wording, it appears that the wine is permitted entirely. In his <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 124:13), he follows the majority view and rules that it is permitted only to benefit from such wine.</i><br>If, however, he extended his hand to a barrel, but his hand was grabbed before he could remove [any wine] or shake it, [there is room for leniency]. If the barrel was opened from below and the wine was allowed to flow out to the extent that it reached below his hand, the wine is not forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam is citing an incident that transpired as recorded by <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 59b. It is not forbidden to benefit from the wine. The question of whether or not it is forbidden to drink it depends on the difference of opinion mentioned in the previous note.</i><br>Similarly, if he held an open container<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">With regard to a closed container, see Halachah 4.</i> of wine and shaked it, the wine becomes forbidden even though he did not lift up the container or touch the wine.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad objects to this ruling, maintaining that as long as the gentile does not touch the wine itself, lift the container, or cause the wine to spatter, moving an open utensil does not cause it to be forbidden. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 124:17) quotes the Rambam's ruling as a minority opinion and the Rama states that it need not be followed if financial loss is involved.</i>
If he took an [open]<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This addition is made on the basis of the gloss of the Radbaz.</i> container of wine, lifted it up, and poured it out, the wine becomes forbidden,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">There is a difference of opinion among the commentaries if only the wine that is poured out is forbidden or also the wine which remains in the container (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>). The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 125:1) quotes the more stringent view. The Rama mentions the more lenient opinion, but states that it may be followed only in a case of severe loss.</i> even though he did not shake it. For the wine moved as a result of his power. If he lifted the container up, but did not shake it or touch it, it is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even to drink the wine. For merely lifting up the wine is of no consequence.</i>
When a gentile was holding a container on the ground and a Jew poured wine into it, the wine is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">One may even drink it [<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 125:8)].</i> If the gentile shakes the container, the wine becomes forbidden.
It is permitted to have a gentile move a closed [container of wine] from one place to another even though the wine moves. For this is not the manner in which a libation is made.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">A libation is made only from an open container (see <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 60a).</i><br>When [a gentile] moves a wineskin containing wine from one place to another while [a Jew]<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">This addition was made on the basis of the gloss of the Radbaz.</i> was holding the opening of the wineskin with his hand, it is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">For this is equivalent to closing it.</i> [This applies] whether the wineskin was entirely full or not and [applies] even though the wine moves.<br>[When a gentile] transfers<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's source (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 60a) and also the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 125:10) emphasize that we are referring to a situation where the Jew is following the gentile. Otherwise, the wine is certainly forbidden.</i> an open earthenware<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz states that the same laws apply regardless of what the container was made of. Therefore he maintains that the word "earthenware" is a printer's error.</i> vessel that is filled with wine, it is prohibited,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Although the Rashba maintains that one may benefit from the wine, most authorities rule that it is prohibited to benefit from it as well as to drink it [<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>)].</i> for perhaps he touched it.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Since the container is both open and full, it is highly likely that the gentile touched the wine (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 60a).</i> If it was only partially full, [the wine] is permitted unless he shook it.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">For shaking the wine is equivalent to pouring it as a libation, as stated in Halachah 1.</i>
When a gentile touches wine without intending to, it is permitted only to benefit from the wine.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it is forbidden to drink it, as stated in Chapter 11, Halachah 5.<br>The Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 124:24) states that in the present era, most gentiles are not idolaters. Therefore, if they touch wine unintentionally, the wine is not forbidden at all.</i> What is implied? He fell on an [open]<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">This addition was made on the basis of the gloss of the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>.</i> wineskin or stretched his hand out to a barrel under the impression that it contained oil and it actually contained wine.
If wine moves because of a gentile's power although he did not intend to do so, since he did not touch the wine, it is permitted to drink it.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">According to Scriptural Law, as long as the gentile does not touch the wine, it is not forbidden. Although our Sages forbade wine which he shook without touching as a safeguard, that applies only when the gentile intentionally touches the container of the wine (see <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 58a).</i> What is implied? If he lifted up a container of wine and poured it into another container while thinking that it was beer or oil, [the wine] is permitted.
If a gentile entered a house or a store seeking wine and extended his hand to search for it and touched wine,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Shaking it (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>). According to the Rambam, this applies even though he did not know for certain that the article he touched was wine. The Ra'avad differs and maintains that the gentile must know that the container contains wine when shaking it. Otherwise, it is not forbidden. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 124:12) quotes the Rambam's ruling.</i> [the wine] is forbidden. [The rationale is that] he was intending [to touch] wine. This is not considered as touching without intent.
When a barrel is split lengthwise and a gentile comes and embraces it so that the halves will not separate<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">And thus the wine will not spill.</i> it is permitted to benefit from [the wine].<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 124:22).</i> If, however, it split widthwise and he grabbed the upper half so that it will not fall, it is permitted to drink [the wine]. For the wine is not affected by the gentile's power.
When a gentile fell into a cistern of wine and was hoisted up dead,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, it is permitted to benefit from the wine, because the gentile is considered to have touched it without intending to. See Halachah 5. According to the Rama's view that the gentiles of the present age are not considered as idolaters, there is no prohibition against using such wine at all. This leniency should be accepted if a significant loss is involved (<i>Siftei Cohen</i> 124:55). This concept also applies to the remainder of the instances mentioned in this halachah.</i> measured a cistern containing wine with a reed, swatted away a fly or a hornet from it with a reed,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">In these instances, since the gentile did not touch the wine directly, merely by means of another entity, it is not forbidden to benefit from it.</i> patted a boiling bottle of wine so that the boiling would cease<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi, <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 60a, states that it is not forbidden to benefit from this wine, because this is not the ordinary way that one makes a libation. <i>Kin'at Eliyahu</i> asks: Since the wine is boiling, the entire prohibition against gentile wine seemingly should not apply, as stated in Chapter 11, Halachah 9?</i> or took a barrel and threw it into the cistern in anger,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance also, the gentile did not touch the wine directly. Hence it is permitted to benefit from it.<br>The Ra'avad protests to this ruling, stating that if he threw the barrel into the cistern in anger, the wine in the cistern is not forbidden at all. Even if he intentionally threw the barrel into the cistern, it is still permitted to benefit from the wine for the reason mentioned. The Radbaz notes that the wording of <i>Avodah Zarah, loc. cit.</i>, appears to support the Ra'avad's perspective, for it states that our Sages <i>hikshiru</i>, "considered acceptable," the wine. He, however, cites a passage from the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 4:11) which appears to fit the Rambam's perspective. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 124:19) quotes the Rambam's view. Nevertheless, the commentaries note that in <i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 125:5, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i>, appears to support the Ra'avad's view.</i> it is merely permitted to benefit from the wine. If, [in the first instance,] the gentile was raised [from the cistern] alive, it is forbidden to benefit from the wine.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">For we assume that in his happiness over being saved, he will offer the wine as a libation to his false deity (<i>Avodah Zarah, loc. cit.</i>). The <i>Turei Zahav</i> 124:19 states that if the gentile was alive when taken from the cistern, the wine is forbidden even if he dies immediately afterwards.</i>
When there is a hole on the side of a barrel, the stopper slips away from the hole, and a gentile places his finger over the hole so that the wine will not flow out, all of the wine from the top of the barrel until the hole is forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">One may not even benefit from it. Since the wine would have flowed out had the gentile not place his finger there, our Sages considered it as if he touched all of that wine.</i> It is, however, permitted to drink the wine beneath the hole.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Because this wine was not affected by the gentile's touch at all. Although this wine is touching the wine that is forbidden, it is not forbidden. The Ra'avad objects to such a ruling, maintaining that the entire barrel should be considered as mixed together. The Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> justify the Rambam's ruling, explaining that had the gentile inserted his finger in the hole and touched the wine, the entire barrel would have been forbidden. Here, however, we are speaking about an instance where the gentile stopped the wine from flowing by placing his finger on the outside. Therefore the wine above the hole is forbidden because it was affected by his power, as stated in the following halachah. This is merely a Rabbinic decree. Hence, the wine below the hole is not forbidden at all. The <i>Tur</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 124:23) follow the Ra'avad's view. See also <i>Hilchot Tum'at Ochalin</i> 8:6.</i>
[The following rules apply when] one end of a bent outflow pipe made from metal, glass, or the like is placed in wine and the other end extends out of the barrel. If one sucked on the wine and the wine began flowing out as is always done, and a gentile came and placed his finger at the end of the outflow pipe and prevented the wine from flowing outward, all of the wine in the barrel is forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">This ruling applies when the opening to the outflow pipe is placed at the bottom of the barrel, so that all the wine would actually have flowed out had the gentile allowed it to. If it was not placed at the bottom of the barrel, the laws mentioned in the previous halachah apply (Radbaz; <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 124:69).</i> [The rationale is that] were it not for his hand, everything [in the barrel] would have flowed out. Thus all the wine is affected by his power.
When a person pours wine into a receptacle containing gentile wine, all of the wine in the upper container is forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">From the Rambam's wording, it appears that this ruling applies with regard to all gentile wine, even when it was not known to have been used as a libation for a false deity. The Rambam, moreover, appears to forbid benefit from the wine, not only partaking of it. The Ra'avad rules that it is permitted to benefit from the wine, but not to partake of it. The <i>Tur</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 126) mentions the opinion of Rabbenu Tam which is more lenient, ruling that this stringency does not apply to ordinary gentile wine. He rules that it is even permitted to partake of the wine. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 126:1-2) follows the ruling of the Maharam of Rutenburg, who states the Rambam's stringency should be followed only when a small loss is involved. If there is a significant loss involved, we may rely on the perspective of Rabbenu Tam.<br>See also the <i>Tur</i> who mentions a perspective that maintains that the above stringency applies only to wine used as a libation for a false deity, but not to ordinary gentile wine.</i> [The rationale is that] the column of wine being poured connects<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Compare to <i>Hilchot Tumat Ochalin</i> 7:1,5 where this principle is not applied. It appears that it is applied in this instance because of the stringency of the prohibition against gentile wine.</i> between the wine in the upper container and the wine in the lower container. Therefore when a person is measuring wine for a gentile into a container in the latter's hands, he should interrupt [the column of wine before it reaches the utensil] or throw the wine so that [the column of wine] being poured will not establish a connection and cause the wine remaining in the upper container to become forbidden.
When a funnel that was used to measure wine for a gentile has an obstruction that prevents wine [from flowing] the funnel should not be used to measure wine for a Jew<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Because the wine held back in the funnel is forbidden because of the connection to the column of wine that extends to the gentile's utensil.</i> until it was washed thoroughly and dried.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Drying refers to the process of applying water and ashes mentioned in Chapter 11, Halachah 20.</i> If he did not wash it thoroughly,<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> offers the following interpretation of the Rambam's wording: As long as the funnel was washed thoroughly, even if it was not dried out, it does not cause other wine to become forbidden. He also, however, makes a distinction between a funnel that has been used by a gentile frequently and one that was used just once. In the former instance, he states, it is possible that washing it thoroughly alone is not sufficient.</i> [the Jew's wine] is forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it is forbidden to benefit from the entire quantity of wine [<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 124:11)]. According to the Rama, one is permitted to benefit from the wine if the loss will be significant (<i>Siftei Cohen</i> 124:23).<br>The rationale for the prohibition is that the wine in the container will mix with the small quantity of gentile wine in the funnel and become forbidden.</i>
[The following rules apply with regard to] a container possessed by a Jew that has two "nostrils,"<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., outflow pipes.</i> that emerge from it, like containers that are used to wash hands, and is filled with wine. If a Jew is sucking and drinking from one nostril and a gentile is sucking and drinking from the other nostril, this is permitted,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Since the wine which the Jew is drinking and that which the gentile is drinking are flowing in opposite directions, they are not considered to be connected.</i> provided the Jew begins [drinking] and concludes while the gentile is still drinking. When the gentile stops drinking, all the wine that was in the nostril will return to the container and cause all the wine in it to be forbidden. [The rationale is that] the wine [in the nostril] was moved by [the gentile's] power.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">For it was his sucking that drew it into the outflow pipe. When that wine returns to the container and becomes mixed with the wine in the container, all the wine becomes forbidden as indicated by the following halachah.</i>
When a gentile sucks wine from a container with an outflow pipe, all the wine in the container becomes forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the wine never touched his mouth (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> For when he ceases [sucking], all of the wine that entered the outflow pipe through his sucking will return to the barrel and cause it to become forbidden.
When a gentile is transferring barrels of wine from one place to another together with a Jew and [the Jew] is walking after them to protect them, they are permitted even if he separates from him for a <i>mil</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">A Talmudic measure equivalent to approximately a kilometer. The <i>Lechem Mishneh</i> notes that as stated in <i>Hilchot Mitamei Moshav UMerkav</i> 13:5, a <i>mil</i> is not a cut off point. As long as the gentile has reason to fear that the Jew will appear suddenly, the wine is permitted.</i> [The rationale is] that he is afraid of him and will say: "He will suddenly appear before us and observe us."<br>[More stringent rules apply if the Jew] tells [gentile porters]: "Proceed and I will follow after you."<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">These words will imply to the porters that he will not be coming immediately. Hence there is reason to fear that they will take from the wine.</i> If they pass beyond his sight to the extent that [they have time] to uncover the opening of the barrel, seal it again, and [allow it] to dry out,<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., so that it would not be apparent that they touched it.</i> it is forbidden to drink all of the wine.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Implied is that one is permitted to benefit from it. The rationale is that since the barrel is sealed, we follow the principle stated in Chapter 13, Halachah 9. See also the Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 129:1) who rules that if the loss is significant, we may rely on the views that one seal is sufficient.</i> If for a lesser [time], [the wine] is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., one may even partake of it.</i>
Similarly, if a Jew leaves a gentile in his store, even though he departs and enters, [going back and forth] the entire day, the wine is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">One may drink it. For the gentile will be afraid to touch the wine, for he will never feel that the Jew has left him alone with the opportunity to do whatever he wants.</i> If he informs him that he is departing for a significant period, should he wait long enough [to enable the gentile] to open the barrel, seal it again, and [allow it] to dry out, it is forbidden to drink the wine.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">As in the previous halachah, since the gentile knows that the Jew is departing for a significant period, we fear that he will use the opportunity to take the wine.</i><br>Similarly, if a person left his wine in a wagon or a ship<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">See the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 5:4) which explains why it is necessary to mention all three instances: the store, the wagon, and the ship.</i> with a gentile and enters a city to tend to his needs, the wine is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 129:1) state that this applies only when the Jew went on a side path that would enable him to surprise the gentile. If, however, he follows the ordinary path, the wine is forbidden. For the gentile will watch to see whether he is coming.</i> If he informs him that he is departing for a significant period, should he wait long enough [to enable the gentile] to open the barrel, seal it again, and [allow it] to dry out, it is forbidden to drink the wine.<br>All of the above rulings apply with regard to closed barrels. If they are open,<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">And thus: a) the wine is easily accessible, and b) the barrel does not have to be sealed close to hide the fact that one took from the wine.</i> even if he did not wait, since he told him that he was departing for a significant period, the wine is forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">It would appear that according to the Rambam, it is even forbidden to benefit from the wine (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i>
When a Jew was eating together with a gentile, left wine open on the table and on the counter, and departed, the wine on the table is forbidden, while that on the counter is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">We assume that he will touch the wine on the table, because it is open before him. But we don't think that he will take the risk of appearing as a thief by touching the wine on the counter. For it is not proper for a guest to take food left on the counter until the host has it brought to the table [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 5:5)].<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 129:7) rules slightly more stringently, stating that any wine which is in the gentile's reach is forbidden.</i> If [the Jew] told him: "Mix [the wine] and drink," all the open wine in the house is forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Jew gave him license, we have no reason to think that he will restrain himself. Here, too, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>) rules more stringently, stating that if the Jew remains outside for a prolonged period (as mentioned in the previous halachah), even the closed barrels are forbidden.</i>
When [a Jew] was drinking together with a gentile and he heard the sound of prayer in the synagogue and departed, even the open wine is permitted. For the gentile will say: "Soon he will remember the wine, come hurriedly and see me touching his wine." Therefore [we do not suspect that] the gentile will move from his place. Hence only the wine that is before him<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the wine on the table, as in the previous halachah (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> becomes forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">As above, when quoting this law, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 129:7) mentions the possibility of the Jew coming from a side path and surprising the gentile. If this is not possible, that source does not accept this leniency.</i>
[The following rules apply when] a gentile and a Jew are living together in one courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Avodah Zarah</i> 70a (the Rambam's source) and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 129:9) state that this leniency applies when the Jew and the gentile live in a two-storey home, with the Jew living in the upper storey. The Rambam does not appear to think that is necessary (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> and they both left in agitation<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> states that the Rambam chose his words carefully. This leniency is granted because they left in agitation. Hence, it was probable that they would not notice each other outside. If, however, they left with calm reserve, it is possible that the gentile would have looked to see that the Jew was not returning and then entered his home and touched his wine.<br>The Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 129:9) quotes this understanding as halachah. The <i>Turei Zahav</i> 129:19, however, differs, explaining that even when a person leaves his home in an agitated state, he will not necessarily return in an agitated state.</i> to see a bridegroom or a funeral. If the gentile returns and closes the entrance and the Jew comes later, the open wine in the Jew's home remains permitted. [We assume that] the gentile closed [the entrance] with the assumption that the Jew had already entered his home and no one remained outside; [i.e.,] he thought that the Jew came before him.
[The following rules apply when] wine belonging to both a Jew and a gentile [is being stored] in one building and [the Jew's] barrels were open. If the gentile entered the building and locked the door behind him,<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, we do not know that the door was locked - even though it was closed and it has a lock - the wine is permitted (<i>Turei Zahav</i> 128:5).</i> all the wine is forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, the Jew's barrels were closed, the wine in the closed barrels is permitted unless the gentile remained in the closed building alone for the time it would take to open a barrel, seal it closed again, and for its lid to dry, as stated in Halachah 16 [<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>; <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 128:3)].</i> If there is a window in the door that enables a person standing behind the door to see in front of him, all of the barrels that are opposite the window are permitted. Those on the sides are forbidden. [The leniency is granted,] because the gentile will fear from those who can see him.
Similarly, if a lion roared or the like and the gentile fled and hid among the open barrels, the wine is permitted. For he will say, "Perhaps another Jew also hid here and will see me if I touch [the wine]."
[The following laws apply with regard to] a wine cellar whose barrels were open, a gentile also stored wine in that inn,<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., in an inn, there were several wine cellars, one in which a Jew stored wine and one in which a gentile stored wine (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> and the gentile was discovered standing among the open barrels belonging to the Jew. If he was frightened when discovered and it would be considered as if he was a thief,<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if he was brought before the judges of a city on the complaint that he touched the wine, the judges would consider him a thief [Rashi (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 61b)]. The <i>Kessef Mishneh </i>states that it is possible that the Rambam interprets the term differently, understanding it as meaning "if he would think he would be considered a thief." According to this interpretation, it could refer not only to the gentile's touching the wine, but also entering the wine cellar.<br>To explain: Since the gentile also stores wine in that inn, he has permission to be in the inn, but he does not necessarily have permission to be in the Jew's wine cellar. This is precisely the question the Rambam is focusing on. Would the gentile be considered as a thief for being found in the Jew's wine cellar or not?</i> it is permitted to drink the wine. For because of his fear and dread, he will not have the opportunity to pour a libation. If he would not be considered as a thief, but instead, he feels secure there, the wine is forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Since he feels unthreatened, there is a high likelihood that he touched the Jews' wine.</i><br>When a [gentile] young child is discovered among the barrels, regardless of whether he would be considered like a thief or not, all of the wine is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">For a young child never pours wine as a libation. In Chapter 11, Halachah 5, the Rambam states that it is forbidden to drink wine touched by a gentile young child. Here, he permits the wine entirely, because we are not certain that the young child in fact touched the wine. The Radbaz explains the rationale for the Rambam's ruling.: Since the young child does not think of using the wine as a libation, there is no reason for it to trouble itself and touch it.</i>
When a battalion [of soldiers] enter a country with an approach of peace, all of the open barrels [of wine] in the stores are forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's wording appears to imply that the open barrels in the homes are permitted. The soldiers would take the liberty of entering stores and making themselves free with their contents. They would not, however, feel that confident to enter homes. The Radbaz objects to this interpretation, noting that we see that soldiers often enter homes to loot. Indeed, when mentioning this law, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 129:12) speaks of homes and not stores.<br>Compare also to <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah </i>18:26 which discusses a similar situation with regard to the question whether the women of the town have been raped.</i> The closed ones, by contrast, are permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">We can be certain that had the soldiers open the wine for use as a libation, they would not have taken the trouble of closing them again [<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>)].</i> At a time of war, however, if a battalion spread through a city and moved on, both are permitted,<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> states that if a barrel was closed and it is discovered open, it is forbidden. For we see that the soldiers did have time to touch the wine.</i> because they do not have time to make libations.
[The following laws apply when] a gentile is discovered standing next to a cistern of wine [belonging to a Jew]. If [the Jew] owes him a debt for which this wine serves is collateral, [the wine] is forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains the Rambam's ruling as follows: If the wine is security for a debt owed the gentile, the gentile will certainly not be considered a thief for touching the wine. Therefore it is forbidden. If the wine is not considered as security for a loan, when the gentile would be considered as a thief, the wine is permitted. When he would not be considered as a thief, it is forbidden.</i> Since he feels privileged, he will extend his hand and make a libation. If it is not collateral for a debt, it is permitted to drink the wine.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">This applies even if the Jew owes him money, and the loan is due, but he has not designated the wine as security for the debt [Rashi (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 60a); <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 128:2)].</i>
When a gentile harlot is present at a Jewish feast, the wine is permitted. For she is in dread of them and will not touch [the wine].<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Although the Jews are willing to give in to their lust for forbidden relations, they are not suspect to drink gentile wine (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 69b). Even the gentile harlot realizes this.</i> When, however, a Jewish harlot is present at a gentile feast, her wine<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., wine that she herself brought.</i> that is before her in her utensils is forbidden, for [the gentiles] will touch it without her consent.<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Since they are employing her as a harlot, they look down upon her and show no consideration for her religious obligations.</i>
[The following laws apply when] a gentile is discovered in a winepress:<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a winepress that does not contain any wine, except for some remnants on the floor.</i> If there is enough moisture from wine that when one places his hand in it, [the hand] will become moist to the extent that if it touches his other hand, that hand will become moist,<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">This is the meaning of the Hebrew phrase <i>tofach al minat litfiach</i>.</i> it is necessary to wash out the winepress thoroughly and dry it out.<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Applying water and ashes, as stated in Chapter 11, Halachah 20.</i> If this amount is not present, all that is necessary is to wash it out thoroughly. This is an extra measure of stringency.<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">We are not certain that the gentile try to touch the wine. Even if he did try to touch the wine, there is no reason for a prohibition, for we are speaking of a dry winepress. Hence washing it out is certainly sufficient (Radbaz).</i>
[The following rules apply with regard to] a barrel floating in the river. If it was found near a city populated primarily by Jews, we are permitted to benefit from it.<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 129:17) quotes this law as applying only in situations when there are obstructions in the river that prevent wine from being carried down the river from other places. In such a situation, we follow the principle of <i>rov</i>, i.e., since the majority of the city's inhabitants are Jewish, we assume that the barrel came from one of them. We are, nevertheless, forbidden to drink the wine. See the notes to the following halachah.</i> Near a city populated primarily by gentiles, it is forbidden.
In a place where most of the wine merchants are Jewish, if one discovers large containers that are generally used only by wine merchants to store wine and which are filled with wine, it is permitted to benefit from [the wine].<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">In his <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, Rav Yosef Caro questions the Rambam's ruling. The Rambam's logic appears to be that since it is obvious that the wine came from a wine merchant and most of the wine merchants are Jewish, we follow the majority and rule that the wine is permitted. Nevertheless, since the majority of the inhabitants of the town are gentile, we forbid drinking the wine. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> asks: "If we fear that the gentile touched the wine, it should be forbidden to benefit from it as well. And if not, it should be permitted to drink it." Indeed, he proposes that perhaps the Rambam's intent is that it is permitted to benefit only from the barrels. In his <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 129:19), he follows the Rambam's ruling. Based on his <i>Beis Yosef</i>, it is possible to explain that we are speaking about closed barrels. We assume that had a gentile opened them and touched the wine, he would not have closed them again. Alternatively, since we do not know for certain that the gentile touched the wine, we do not forbid benefiting from it.</i><br>When a barrel has been opened by thieves, if most of the local thieves are Jewish, it is permitted to drink the wine. If not, it is forbidden.

Chapter 13

[The following rules apply when a Jew] purchases or rents a building in a courtyard belonging to a gentile and fills it with wine. If the Jew lives in that courtyard, the wine is permitted even if the entrance is open. [The rationale is that] the gentile will always worry, saying: "He may suddenly enter his building and find me there." If the Jew lives in another courtyard,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">And thus it is less likely for him to come at frequent intervals.</i> he should not depart until he closes the building and keeps the key and the seal<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Implied is that the entrance is closed with two seals, as required by Halachah 8. The Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 130:2) writes that since in the present age, most gentiles are not idolaters, only one seal is necessary. The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 130:11) states that this principle should be applied in the present instance.</i> in his possession. He need not fear that the gentile will make a copy of the key to the building.
When [the Jew] left [the building] without closing the entrance or closed it and gave the key to the gentile, it is forbidden to drink the wine. Perhaps the gentile entered and poured a libation, for the Jew is not present there.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Nevertheless, since we do not know for certain that the gentile touched the wine, we do not forbid benefiting from it (Radbaz).</i><br>If [the Jew] told [the gentile]: "Hold the key for me until I come," the wine is permitted. He did not entrust him with guarding the house, only with guarding the key.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Since the gentile was not given permission to enter the house, he would be considered as a thief if he did so. Hence, we assume that he did not enter the home to pour a libation.<br>The Ra'avad states that the Rambam's words apply only when the house belongs to the Jew. When, however, the house belongs to the gentile, the wine is forbidden, even if he did not entrust him with the key. The rationale is that since the gentile has a connection to the house, he will have an excuse to enter it. Hence we fear that he entered it and touched the Jew's wine. The Radbaz defends the Rambam's ruling explaining that since the house is rented the owner does not have the right to enter it at will. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 129:5) quotes the Rambam's ruling.</i>
[The following laws apply when] a gentile hires a Jew to prepare wine for him in a state of ritual purity<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">We have translated the Rambam's words literally. The intent, however, appears to be not ritual purity per se, but "without contact with gentiles."</i> so that it will be permitted to the Jews and they will purchase it from him. The wine is [stored] in a building belonging to the gentile. If the Jew who is guarding the wine lives in that courtyard, the wine is permitted. [This applies] even if the entrance is open and the [Jewish] guard goes out and returns.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he is not present at all times. Nevertheless, it is possible that he will return at any given moment. Hence, the gentile will not take liberties. See Halachah 4.</i><br>If the guard lives in another courtyard,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Since he does not live on the premises, he is not considered as a permanent watchman. Hence, the fact that he enters from time to time during the day is not significant (<i>Lechem Mishneh</i>). The Ra'avad differs and maintains that as long as the Jew enters and leaves at will, that is sufficient to inhibit the gentile from touching the wine. [Significantly, in his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 4:11), the Rambam adopts a position similar to that of the Ra'avad.]<br>The <i>Tur</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 131:1 adopt an intermediate position, stating that if there is another Jew living in that city and the entrance to the building where the wine is stored is visible from the public domain, the wine is permitted. For the owner will be afraid to break the lock to the door lest he be seen and the matter become known. (This approach is also mentioned in the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>loc. cit</i>).</i> the wine is forbidden even though the key and the seal are in the possession of a Jew. [The rationale is that] since the wine belongs to the gentile and is found in his domain, he does not fear falsifying [the seal and/or key] and to enter the building. He will say: "What could be? If they find out about this, they will not purchase [the wine] from me."<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">There is, however, nothing preventing him from selling it to other gentiles.</i>
Even if a gentile wrote [a legal document] for the Jew stating that he received the money for which he agreed to sell him the wine,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he wrote the bill of sale in advance, before the Jew actually paid to clarify that his intent was to sell it to him.<br>The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 131:1 writes that these stringencies apply only if the Jew did not pay the gentile anything at all. Once the Jew pays the gentile something, the wine is considered his and more lenient rules apply. It is questionable, however, if the Rambam would accept this leniency, for as stated in Chapter 12, Halachah 25, he rules that as long as wine is security for a debt, a gentile creditor will feel free to do with it as he desires.</i> since the Jew cannot remove the wine from the gentile's domain until he pays him the money, the wine belongs to the gentile and it is forbidden unless the guard lives in the courtyard.<br>The guard does not have to sit and guard [the wine] at all times. Instead, he may come in and go out, as explained. [This applies whether the wine is stored] in the domain belonging to the owner of the wine or in a domain belonging to another gentile.
When the pure wine belonging to a gentile was placed in the public domain or in a building that is open to the public domain and there are Jews going back and forth, it is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Since Jews can see whether or not the gentile touches it, he is afraid to do so, lest his investment be ruined. See the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 4:11).</i> For it has not entered the gentile's domain.
[When wine is located] in a garbage dump, a window, or under a palm tree even if it does not have fruit, it is as [if it is located in] the public domain.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Because these places are also in public view and/or acces.</i> When a gentile is located near wine located in such a place, it is not forbidden. A house which is open to such a place is considered as if it as open to the public domain.
[The following rule applies when] there is a courtyard divided by low barriers,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation follows Rashi's commentary to <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 70a. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 129:16) defines the term as meaning pillars.</i> on one side there is a gentile and on the other, a Jew, there are two roofs, with the Jew's roof located above the gentile's roof, or [the two roofs are located] side by side, but there are dividers separating them. Even though the gentile can reach the Jew's portion, he need not worry about [the gentile pouring] his wine as a libation<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Since the gentile would be considered as a thief for overstepping these boundaries, we do not fear that he would do so.</i> or [disqualifying] articles that are ritually pure.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Were a gentile to touch them, they would be disqualified.</i>
It is permitted for a Jew to entrust his wine to a gentile for safekeeping in a closed container, provided it has two distinguishing marks. This is referred to as "a seal within a seal."<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale is that we assume that a gentile will not trouble himself to reseal the container with two seals as the Jew had sealed it. Hence the fact that he found it with the two seals he left, it is a sign that it has not been tampered with.</i><br>What is implied? [A Jew] closed a barrel with a utensil that is not tightly fitting as most people do and then sealed it with clay, it is considered as one seal. If the container is tightly fitting and he applied clay to it from above, it is considered as "a seal within a seal."<br>Similarly, if one tied the opening to a wineskin close, it is considered as one seal. If he turned the opening to the wineskin inside and then tied it close, it is considered as "a seal within a seal." Similarly, any deviation from the ordinary pattern people follow is considered as one seal and applying clay or tying it is a second seal.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">To apply these concepts in contemporary terms: When a bottle of wine is closed with a cork or a bottle-cap, that is one seal. If there is a paper or plastic wrapper around the cork or the cap, that is the second seal.</i>
If [a Jew] entrusted [wine that was closed] with one seal to a gentile for safekeeping, it is forbidden to drink it, but it is permitted to benefit from it provided he designates a [specific] corner for it.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Based on <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 31a, some interpret this as speaking about an instance where the corner the gentile grants the Jew is closed off with a seal. The Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 130:2) writes that there are opinions which rule that after the fact, one seal is sufficient in this situation.<br>The <i>Lechem Mishneh</i> explains that even if the place is not closed off, since it is designated for the Jew, one seal is sufficient. See <i>Turei Zahav</i> 130:4).</i>
Two seals are not necessary when one deposits boiled wine, beer, wine which is mixed with other substances, e.g., honey or oil,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">For in none of these instances do we fear that the gentile will use the beverage for a libation, as stated in Chapter 11, Halachot 9-10.</i> – and similarly, vinegar, cheese, and any substance that is forbidden only according to Rabbinic Law – with a gentile. Instead, one seal is sufficient.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">In these instances, we fear that the gentile will exchange another substance, for the substance deposited. One seal is sufficient to dispel these suspicions (<i>Lechem Mishneh</i>).</i> Nevertheless, two seals are necessary for wine, meat, and pieces of fish that do not have signs and which were entrusted to a gentile.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Since the prohibition involved in these instances is Scriptural in origin, we are more stringent.</i>
It appears to me that anywhere in this context that we have stated that our wine is forbidden to be drunk, but it is permitted to benefit from it because of the possibility that a gentile touched it, we are speaking about an instance where the gentile is an idolater. If, however, the prohibition has arisen because of a gentile who is not an idolater, e.g., an Arab,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 11, Halachah 7. That halachah states that when a gentile who is not an idolater touches wine, it is only forbidden to drink it. In this instance, since the gentile did not intend to touch the wine, we are more lenient and do not forbid it at all (Radbaz).<br>As mentioned previously, the Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 124:24) rules that in the present era, none of the gentiles are considered as idolaters and the leniency suggested by the Rambam applies universally. On that basis, he and the subsequent Ashkenazic authorities have suggested several leniencies.</i> who touched our wine unintentionally or tapped the top of a barrel,<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 12, Halachot 5 and 9.</i> [the wine] is permitted to be drunken. Similar laws apply in all analogous situations.
When, however, one deposits wine in the domain of a resident alien<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">A gentile who has made a formal commitment to accept the Seven Universal Laws Commanded to Noah and His Descendants. These include the prohibition against worshipping false divinities.</i> sends wine with him and departs for an extended period, or leaves one's home open in a courtyard that [one shares with] a resident alien, it is forbidden to drink the wine. For it appears to me that the suspicions that a gentile will exchange [wine] and forge [a seal] apply equally to all gentiles. Since the wine enters their domain,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a place where it can be exchanged without a Jew noticing.</i> it is forbidden at least to drink it.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">For we fear that he exchanged it with his own wine and it is forbidden to drink such wine. Although a resident alien also accepted the prohibition against theft, we fear that he - and certainly, other gentiles - will not abide by his commitment (Radbaz).</i>
There are situations where the prohibition against wine poured as a libation does not apply at all, yet our Sages forbade them as a safeguard against libation. They are: a gentile should not mix water into wine in a Jew's possession lest he come to pour wine into water. A gentile should not bring grapes to the winepress lest he come to press them or touch the wine. He should not help a Jew when he pours wine from one container to another lest he leave the wine in the possession of the gentile and the wine [will flow] because of [the gentile's] power. If the gentile assists [the Jew], mixes water [into wine] or brings grapes, [the wine] is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">For these are merely safeguards. Although Rashi (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 58b) and other <i>Rishonim</i> rule more stringently, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 125:3,6,7) accepts the Rambam's position.</i>
Similarly, it is permitted for a gentile to smell the fragrance of our wine<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Doing so does not arouse a suspicion that perhaps he used it as a libation for his false deity. Smelling is not considered as tasting or drinking.</i> and it is permitted for a Jew to smell the fragrance of a barrel of wine that had been used as a libation.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">It is not included in the prohibition mentioned at the beginning of Ch. 11.</i> There is no prohibition against this, because fragrance is of no consequence since it has no substance.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">See the conclusion of Ch. 5 of <i>Hilchot Meilah</i>, where the Rambam delivers a slightly contradictory ruling.</i>
We already explained,<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 8, Halachah 16. See also <i>Hilchot Avodat Kochavimn</i> 7:9 and <i>Hilchot Ishut</i> 5:2.</i> that whenever it is forbidden to benefit from a substance, if one transgresses and sells it, it is permitted [to make use of] the money with the exception of false deities, their accessories, offerings made to them, and wine poured as a libation to it. Our Sages were stringent with regard to ordinary gentile wine [and ruled that] money given for it is forbidden like money given for wined poured as a libation to a false deity.<br>Accordingly, when a gentile hires a Jew to work with him with wine, his wages are forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">For he is deriving benefit from gentile wine.</i>
Similarly, when a person rents a donkey or a boat to transport wine, the payment for them is forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the Jew himself does nothing to help transport the gentile wine.</i> If he gave him money, he should bring them to the Dead Sea.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., throw in a place where neither he nor anyone else will benefit from them.</i> If he gave him clothes, utensils, or produce as payment, he should burn it and bury the dust so that he<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Nor others.</i> does not benefit from it.
If a gentile rented a donkey to ride and placed containers of wine on it, the rental fee for the donkey is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">For the rental fee was not primarily paid for the sake of the wine (<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 133:3). The Radbaz emphasizes that this leniency applies when the donkey was rented primarily for human transport and, by the way, the gentile placed wine upon it. If, however, he rented it primarily to transport packages - and later the owner discovered that wine was included among them - the rental fee is forbidden even if the person also rides on the donkey.</i> If [a gentile] hires a Jew to break barrels of wine used as a libation, his fee is permitted. May he be blessed because he eliminated obscenity.
When a person hires a worker and tells him: "Transport 100 barrels of beer for me for 100 <i>p'rutot</i>," and it is discovered that one of them is [gentile] wine, his entire wage is forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">He is being paid for the entire work as a collective entity. Were he not to have transported all the barrels, he would not be paid at all (Rashi, <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 65a). Accordingly, the payment for transporting the beer was never distinct from that of the wine. Hence his entire wage is forbidden.<br>The Ra'avad differs and maintains that it is sufficient to destroy the wage paid for the forbidden barrels. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 133:3) follows the Rambam's stringency.</i>
If he told him: "Transport barrels for me at a <i>p'rutah</i> each," and he transported them and barrels of wine were discovered among them, the wage for the barrels of wine is forbidden. The remainder of the wage is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Since the wage was paid for each barrel individually, the wage paid for the barrels of beer is a separate and distinct entity. Hence it is not forbidden. Nevertheless, at the outset, it is forbidden to accept such a job [<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i>)].</i>
When a gentile sends Jewish craftsmen a barrel of wine as part of their wages, it is permitted for them to tell him: "Give us its worth."<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">For the craftsman have not accepted the wine and the employer owes them money.</i> Once it enters their domain, it is forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">For then it is as if they are exchanging the wine for wine.<br>The Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 132:3) writes that in the present age, (when gentiles are not actually idolaters,) a worker may return the barrel of wine even though it has entered his domain.</i>
When a gentile owed a Jew a <i>maneh</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">One hundred silver <i>zuzim</i>.</i> it is permitted for the gentile to sell a false deity and wine that had been poured as a libation and bring him the money. If, before he sells them, he tells [the Jew]: "Wait until I sell the false deity or libation wine that I own and [then] I will bring you [the money]," if he sells it and brings [the money] to him, [the money] is forbidden. [This applies] even with regard to ordinary gentile wine. [The rationale is that] the Jew desires that [the false deity or the wine] to continue to exist so that he will be able to pay him his debt.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Hence he has benefited from existence of the gentile wine. Hence, it is forbidden.<br>The Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 132:7) states that, even if the Jew desires that the false deity continue to exist, leniency can be granted in an instance where the gentile has other resources to pay the debt or alternatively, when the debt is secured by a guarantor. Moreover, if all that is concerned is ordinary gentile wine, in the present age, there is no prohibition for the reason stated above.</i>
Similarly, when a convert and a gentile were partners and they came to divide the resources [of the partnership], the convert may not tell the gentile: "You take the false deity and I will take the money. You take the wine and I will take the produce." [The rationale is that] he desires that [the forbidden entities] continue to exist so that he will be able to receive something in exchange for them.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Here leniency is not granted, because the convert has a share in the entities belonging to the partnership. Thus he is exchanging money for a false deity.</i><br>When, by contrast, a convert and a gentile inherit the estate of their father who was a gentile, [the convert] may tell [the gentile]: "You take the false deity and I will take the money. You take the wine and I will take the oil." This is a leniency granted with regard to an estate inherited by a convert so that he will not return to his deviant ways.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., our Sages feared that the convert will be so disturbed about being unable to receive his inheritance, that he will forsake Jewish practice and return to his previous mode of conduct. This is undesirable, because once a person converts, he is a full-fledged Jew. If he conducts himself undesirably, his conduct affects the entire Jewish people.</i> If [the forbidden entities] entered the domain of the convert, it is forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">For they have already entered the domain of the convert and are, therefore, forbidden.
