Hence it is forbidden to exchange them for others, for then one will be deriving benefit.</i>
[The following rules apply when] a Jew sells his wine to a gentile. If he established a price before he measured out [the wine], the money is permitted. [The rationale is that] from the time a price was established, [the gentile] definitely agreed [to the purchase] and when he pulled [the wine] into his domain, he acquired it.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he acquires the wine through the <i>kinyan</i> of <i>meshichah</i> [see <i>Maggid Mishneh, Hilchot Zechiyah UMatanah</i> 1:14; <i>Turei Zahav</i> 132:4) and the money is considered as a loan which he owes the Jew.<br>The Radbaz questions why the Rambam mentions <i>meshichah</i>, drawing the wine into his own domain. Seemingly, once a price was established and the wine was poured, the gentile acquires it whether or not he performs <i>meshichah</i> immediately. Conversely, if <i>meshichah</i> finalizes the transaction, seemingly as long as a price was set before <i>meshichah</i>, the wine should be permitted<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that the Rambam is speaking according to the common practice. It was customary to establish a price either before measuring the wine or after <i>meshichah</i>.</i> And it does not become [comparable to] wine offered as a libation until he touches it. Therefore at the time of sale, it was permitted.<br>If he measured it out for him before he established a price, the money is forbidden. [The rationale is that the gentile] did not definitely agree [to the purchase], even though he pulled [the wine] into his domain.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">For he fears that the Jew will ask an exorbitant price (Radbaz). Hence he always keeps the option of negating the sale.</i> Thus at the time he touched [the wine], he had not definitely agreed to the purchase. Hence the wine becomes forbidden because of his touch and it is as if [the Jew] is selling gentile wine.
When does the above apply? When the Jew measured [the wine] into his own containers. If, however, he measured it into the gentile's containers or to a container belonging to a Jew in the gentile's possession, he must take the money,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">For the payment of the money formalizes the transfer of the wine (effecting a <i>kinyan</i>), Thus the gentile has paid for the wine before it entered his domain and became forbidden.</i> before measuring out [the wine]. If he measured out [the wine,] but did not take the money, the money is forbidden even though he established a price. As soon as [the wine] enters [the gentile's] container, it is forbidden as ordinary gentile wine.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">There are several explanations for this ruling. The gentile left some of his wine in the container and thus as the Jew was pouring the new wine in, it became forbidden. Alternatively, the gentile was holding the container and moved it (see Chapter 12, Halachah 3). This is sufficient to cause the wine to become forbidden (Radbaz).<br>The Ra'avad objects to the Rambam's ruling, stating that (as the Rambam himself rules in Chapter 16, Halachah 29) if kosher wine becomes mixed with non-kosher wine, it is forbidden to drink it, but one may benefit from it. Nevertheless, he does not provide a rebuttal to the second explanation given above.<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that since the wine in the container the gentile is holding becomes forbidden, the wine the Jew is pouring also becomes forbidden, as stated above in Chapter 12, Halachah 12.</i>
When [a Jewish employer] gives a <i>dinar</i> to a gentile storekeeper and tells his gentile employee: "Go, drink, and eat [on my account] from the storekeeper and I will settle the accounts with him," he must show concern lest [the employee] will drink wine.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Since he gave the storekeeper the money in advance, it is as if he paid the storekeeper for what his worker would eat. Thus it is as if the worker is drinking the employer's wine.</i> Thus it will be as if he purchased wine used as a libation and gave it to him.<br>A similar arrangement with regard to the Sabbatical year<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's source (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 58b) mentions both produce from the Sabbatical year and untithed produce, because it is possible that a common person is lax in his observance of both these mitzvot. Apparently, the Rambam also had this intent because he begins by mentioning produce of the Sabbatical year and concludes by mentioning untithed produce.</i> is also forbidden; i.e., one gives a <i>dinar</i> to a Jewish storekeeper who is a common person and tells his Jewish employee: "Go, drink, and eat [on my account] from the storekeeper and I will settle the accounts with him." If the worker eats food that was not tithed, it is forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it is forbidden for the employer to do this, because it would be considered as if he personally gave his employee produce from the Sabbatical year or untithed produce.</i>
If, however, he told them: "Eat and drink the worth of this<i> dinar</i>," or "Eat and drink from the storekeeper on my account and I will pay him," this is permitted. Although the Jew becomes liable, his liability is not specifically related [to the foods from which the employees partake].<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he undertakes a financial obligation to the storekeeper, but since he does not pay him the money beforehand, that obligation is not explicitly associated with the food or drink of which the worker partakes.</i> [Therefore,] he need not be concerned, not about wine used as a libation, not about produce of the Sabbatical year, nor about untithed produce.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 450:6) mentions opinions that are more stringent with regard to an employer taking financial responsibility for the food a gentile will eat on Pesach. The <i>Turei Zahav</i> 460:4 explains that with regard to Pesach, there is a greater reason for stringency, for it is almost certain that the gentile will eat <i>chametz</i>. In the situations mentioned in our halachah, by contrast, it is possible that none of the prohibitions will be violated, for the gentile will not want wine, nor the Jewish workers, the untithed or Sabbatical produce.</i>
[The following rules apply when] a [gentile] king distributes his wine among the people and takes money for it, as he desires.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">A gentile king produced wine from the royal vineyard as a means of financing his nation's expenses. He would obligate each of the person's in his kingdom to buy a standard amount of wine. For a Jew, that represents a problem for the wine is gentile wine. Not only is it forbidden to drink it, it is forbidden to benefit from it. Thus not only may a Jew not partake of such wine, nor may he take it and sell it. He is forbidden even to purchase it from the king.<br>This represents the Rambam's interpretation of <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 71a. It is quoted by the Rashba and other <i>Rishonim</i>. Rashi, the Ra'avad, and others, however, have different interpretations of the passage. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 132:6) quotes the Rambam's interpretation.</i> A [Jew] may not tell a gentile: "Take 200 <i>zuz</i> and go into the king's storehouse in place of me," so that the gentile will take the wine designated for the Jew and give the money to the king.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz explains that in this way, the gentile is purchasing the wine from the Jew. Others explain that the gentile is acting as the Jew's agent.</i> He may, however, tell him: "Here is 200 <i>zuz</i> for you. Save me from [going to] the storehouse."<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">For in this way, the gentile is not acting as the Jew's agent.</i>
When a gentile touches<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">This law applies when the gentile intentionally touches the wine. If the gentile touches it unintentionally, he is not liable. The rationale is that this is damage which is not outwardly noticeable (i.e., although the ritual status of the wine has changed, outwardly it is the same). In such an instance, <i>Hilchot Chovel UMazik</i> 7:3 states, one is not liable for causing damage inadvertently.<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> states that even if the gentile intentionally touched the wine, but did not know that by touching it, he caused it to be forbidden, the gentile is not liable and this leniency does not apply. The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 132:2, however, interprets this wording as implying that even if the gentile caused it to become forbidden inadvertently, the Jew may sell it to him.<br>See also <i>Hilchot Chovel UMazik</i> 7:4 and commentaries, where a similar concept is discussed.</i> a Jew's wine against [the Jew's] will,<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">For if the Jew could have stopped the gentile from touching the wine and didn't, he is responsible for the loss (Radbaz).</i> it is permitted to sell that wine to that gentile alone.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 124:2) rules that in the present age, when it is not customary for gentiles to use wine as libations, the wine may be sold to any gentile.</i> [The rationale is] since that gentile wished to cause a Jew a loss [by] having his wine forbidden, it is as if he destroyed it or burnt it, in which instance, he would be obligated to pay. Thus the money [the Jew] takes from him is money for the loss and not money for a sale.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Avodah Zarah</i> 59b states that in such a situation, he may charge the gentile the full price of the wine.</i>

Chapter 14

The minimum measure for which one is liable for partaking of any of the forbidden foods in the Torah is [the size of] an average olive.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The measure of "the size of an olive" cannot be determined by measuring an average olive today. Instead, this refers to a measure established by our Sages and is the subject of debate by later Rabbinic authorities. The <i>Pri Chadash</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 486) states that the Rambam considers an olive as one-third the size of an egg with its shell (17.3 grams according to <i>Shiurei Torah</i> 3:13, 24 grams according to <i>Chazon Ish</i>). <i>Tosafot, Chullin</i> 103a, differs and defines an olive as one-half the size of a shelled egg (25.6 grams according to <i>Shiurei Torah</i> 3:12, 36 grams according to <i>Chazon Ish</i>). In practice, with regard to questions of Scriptural Law, the more stringent opinion should be followed. With regard to questions of Rabbinic Law, one may rely on the more lenient view.</i> [This applies] whether for lashes,<i> kerait</i>, <sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Literally, the soul's being cut off. This involves premature death in this world (before the age of 50, <i>Mo'ed Kattan</i> 28a) and the soul not meriting a portion in the world to come (<i>Hilchot Teshuvah</i> 8:1).</i> or death at the hand of heaven.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Premature death before the age of 60 (<i>Mo'ed Kattan, loc. cit.</i>).</i> We already explained<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 1:7.</i> that anyone who is liable for <i>kerait</i> or death at the hand of heaven for [partaking of] forbidden food, should receive lashes.
This measure, as all the other measurements, is a halachah conveyed by Moses from Sinai. It is forbidden by Scriptural Law to eat even the slightest amount of a forbidden substance. Nevertheless, one receives lashes only for an olive-sized portion. If one partakes of any amount less than this measure, he is given stripes for rebellious conduct.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The punishment given for the violation of Rabbinic commandments or Scriptural Laws for which there is no specific punishment outlined.</i>
The measure of "the size of an olive" that we mentioned does not include what is between one's teeth.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shiurei Torah</i> suggests including slightly more than 3 grams in the measure of an olive-sized portion to compensate for this factor.</i> What is between one's gums,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Even though it was not swallowed.</i> however, is included in what one swallows, for his palate benefited from an olive-sized portion of food.<br>Even if one ate half of an olive-sized portion, vomited it, and then ate the same portion that was half the size of an olive that he vomited, he is liable.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to a situation in which the person ate the vomited food a second time shortly after he ate it the first time. To explain: For a person to be liable, he must eat not only a specific amount (an olive-sized portion), but he must eat it in a specific time: <i>k'dai achilas p'ras</i>, as explained in Halachah 8.</i> For the liability is for the benefit one's palate receives from a forbidden substance.
When an olive-sized portion of forbidden fat, a <i>nevelah, piggul,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">A sacrifice which the priest thought to have its blood or limbs offered on the altar after the time when they should be offered or have its meat eaten after the time it should be eaten (<i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 13:1).</i> notar</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Sacrificial meat that remained after the time when it is required to be eaten (<i>op cit.</i> 13:3).</i> or the like was left in the sun and was reduced in volume, one who eats it is not liable.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">As <i>Menachot</i> 54b states, the size of a portion of food at the time one partakes of it determines whether he is liable or not.</i><br>If, afterwards, one left it in the rain and it expanded, one is liable for either <i>kerait</i> or lashes.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">For, at the outset, it was the size of an olive.</i> If, originally, it was smaller than an olive-sized portion and then expanded to the size of an olive, it is forbidden to partake of it, but one is not liable for lashes for it.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">For its natural size is not an olive.</i>
We already explained<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 4, Halachot 16 and 17.</i> that all of the forbidden substances in the Torah are not combined with each other to reach the minimum measure of the size of an olive with the exception of the meat of a <i>nevelah</i> and the meat of a <i>trefe</i><sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">For a <i>trefe</i> is the beginning of a <i>nevelah</i>, as stated there.</i> and the prohibitions involving a nazirite,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">For they are all grape products, as stated in <i>Hilchot Nazirut</i> 5:3.</i> as explained in the appropriate places. The five types of grain,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Wheat, barley, rye, oats, and spelt. Since they are all grain, they are combined to reach the minimum measure.</i> their flour, and the dough made from them all can be combined with each other to reach the minimum measure of the size of an olive with regard to the prohibition against leaven on Pesach, the prohibition against partaking of <i>chadash</i> before the offering of the<i> omer</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 10 for a definition of this prohibition.</i> and the prohibitions involving the second tithe and the <i>terumot</i>.
It appears to me that all [produce] from which we are required to separate <i>terumah</i> and tithes can be combined to reach the minimum measure of the size of an olive with regard to [the prohibition against] <i>tevel</i> because a single prohibition is involved. To what can the matter be compared? To [meat from] the corpse of an ox, the corpse of a sheep, and the corpse of a deer which can be combined to reach the minimum measure of the size of an olive as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 4, Halachah 7.<br>The Ra'avad qualifies the Rambam's statement, maintaining that it applies only when the types of produce consumed have a similar taste. The Radbaz, however, justifies the Rambam's view.</i>
When a person partakes of a large amount of food from a forbidden substance, he is not liable for lashes or <i>kerait</i> for every olive-sized portion he eats. Instead, he is liable once for all he ate.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Nazir</i> 6:4), the Rambam states that this leniency applies only with regard to an earthly court. God, however, holds the person liable for each forbidden measure he eats.</i> If, however, the witness gave him a warning for every olive-sized portion, he is liable for every warning even though he ate it in one sitting, without interruption.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">For the warnings create a distinction between the food eaten before and afterwards.</i>
[The following rules apply when] a person partakes of a barley-corn or mustard-seed-sized portion of any forbidden food, waits, and then partakes of another mustard-seed-sized portion whether inadvertently or intentionally. If he waited from the beginning to the end the time it takes to eat a portion of bread with relish the size of three eggs<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on <i>Hilchot Tumat Tzara'at</i> 16:6.</i> [or less], everything [he ate] is combined.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">This is one of the fundamental concepts with regard to the mitzvot and prohibitions concerning eating. Just as there is a minimum amount, a <i>k'zayit</i> (an olive-sized portion), which one must eat for the mitzvah or prohibition to be fulfilled; so, too, there is a minimum measure of time,<i> k'dei achilat p'ras</i>, in which that amount of food must be eaten. If one takes a longer time to eat the prohibited food, his eating is not significant, like one who eats less than the minimum amount.<br>Rashi (<i>Pesachim</i> 44a ) offers a different view and maintains that this measure is defined as the time it takes to eat four eggs. <i>Shiurei Torah</i> mentions several different opinions from between four minutes until nine minutes for this figure.</i> He is liable for <i>kerait</i>, lashes, or a sacrifice as if he ate an olive-sized portion at one time. If he waits a longer time from the beginning to the end, [the small portions] are not combined. Since he completed the olive-sized portion only in a longer time than <i>k'dei achilat p'ras</i>, he is not liable even if he did not wait at all, but continued eating mustard-seed-sized portion after mustard-seed-sized portion.
Similar [laws apply when] a person who drinks a <i>revi'it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The standard liquid measure that applies with regard to the Torah's mitzvot and prohibitions.</i> of ordinary gentile wine little by little, swallows liquefied leaven on Pesach or fat little by little, or drinks blood<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries have noted that in Chapter 6, Halachah 1, the Rambam mentioned that the minimum measure for which one is liable for partaking of blood is an olive-sized portion and question why in this context, a <i>revi'it</i> is mentioned. It is possible to explain that here the subjects are slightly different, for we are not speaking about the minimum amount for which one is liable, but rather the minimum time period. The commentaries, however, do not see this as a significant enough point.</i> little by little. If he waits from the beginning until the end the time it takes to drink a <i>revi'it</i>, [all of the sipping] is combined.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">For if one prolongs his drinking over a longer period, his deed is not considered significant.</i> If not, it is not combined.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> note that other authorities do not accept the concept of "the time it takes to drink a <i>revi'it</i>" and even with regard to prohibitions that involve drinking, speak of <i>k'dei achilat p'ras</i>. Indeed, the Rambam himself mentions that measure with regard to drinking within the context of the laws of ritual purity (<i>Hilchot Sha'ar Avot HaTumah</i>.<br>The Radbaz explains the Rambam's ruling here, stating that with regard to the prohibitions against eating, what is important is that one feel significant satisfaction. If he prolongs his drinking longer than that, he will not feel satisfaction from it. See also the gloss of the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> to <i>Hilchot Shivitat Esor</i> 2:4 which discusses this issue.</i>
One is not liable for partaking of any of the prohibited foods unless one partakes of them in a manner in which one derives satisfaction with the exception of a mixture of meat and milk and mixed species grown in a vineyard. [The rationale is that with regard to these prohibitions, the Torah] does not use the term "eating,"<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">When, however, the Torah uses the term "eating," that implies that one derives satisfaction in the ordinary manner one derives benefit from food (see the gloss of the <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> to Chapter 8, Halachah 16). Only then is one liable. Needless to say, it is forbidden by Rabbinic Law to partake of a forbidden substance even if one does not derive benefit.</i> but instead conveys the prohibition against partaking of them in other terms. [With regard to meat and milk, it uses] the term "cooking" and [with regard to mixed species grown in a vineyard, it uses the term] "become hallowed."<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in Chapter 10, Halachah 6, "becom[ing] hallowed" means being "set apart and forbidden."</i> [This implies] that they are forbidden even when one does not derive satisfaction.
What is implied? When one liquefied fat and swallowed it when it was so hot that his throat was burned from it, he eat raw fat,<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Which does not have a pleasant taste.</i> mixed bitter substances like gall or wormwood into wine<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">This concept also applies with regard to wine used as a libation, for, Deuteronomy 32:38, the prooftext from which this prohibition is derived, also mentions "eating" [<i>Minchat Chinuch</i> (mitzvah 111)].</i> or into a pot [where meat from] a <i>nevelah</i> [is cooking] and he partook of it while they were bitter,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even if the unappetizing element of the food is not dependent on them, but on a foreign substance.</i> or he ate a forbidden food after it became decayed, spoiled, and unfit for human consumption, he is not liable.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the concept referred to as <i>notein taam lifgam</i>, giving an unfavorable taste. Our Rabbis extend this concept further, explaining that any pot which has not been used for non-kosher food for a day no longer causes the pot to be forbidden according to Scriptural Law, because the forbidden food has already lost its flavor [<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 103:5)]. See also Chapter 15, Halachot 28-31.</i> If, by contrast, he mixed a bitter substance into a a pot [where meat and milk are cooking] or into wine from a vineyard where mixed species are growing and partook of it, he is liable.
When a person partakes of one of the forbidden food in a frivolous manner or as one who is acting purposelessly, he is liable. Even though he did not intend to actually partake of the food, since he derived pleasure, it is considered as if he intended to actually partake of the food.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Generally, a person who violates a transgression without intent is not liable (<i>Shabbat</i> 22a). Here, however, an exception is made, because the person is deriving physical benefit.</i> When, [by contrast,] a person is forced to derive [forbidden] pleasure, if he focuses his intent on it, he is liable. If he does not, it is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Even though he derived pleasure, since he did not act voluntarily and did not desire the forbidden pleasure, he is not held liable.</i>
When a person partakes of a forbidden food because of desire or because of hunger, he is liable.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Although either his desire or hunger causes him pain, he is not considered as if he was compelled to partake of the forbidden food.</i> If he was wandering in the desert and he has nothing to eat but a forbidden substance, it is permitted, because of the danger to his life.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Yesodei Torah</i> 5:6, all prohibitions are superceded by danger to life with the exception of idolatry, murder, and forbidden sexual relations.</i>
When a pregnant woman smells a forbidden food [and is overcome by desire for it],<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">A pregnant woman may have severe cravings for food with an attractive aroma. Our Sages feared that if she were not given some of the food she desired, she might miscarry and perhaps even her own life would be endangered. See <i>Yoma</i> 82b.</i> e.g., consecrated meat or ham, she should be given some of the gravy. If her mind becomes settled, that is commendable. If not, we feed her less than the forbidden measure<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., less than an olive-sized portion as stated in Halachah 1. Since one is not liable unless one partakes of an olive-sized portion within <i>k'dei achilat pras</i> (see Halachah 8), if we do not fear the situation is overly dangerous, the woman can be fed this minimal amount in intervals. See <i>Hilchot Sh'vitat Asor</i> 2:9, <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Orach Chayim</i> 617:2).</i> [of the meat itself]. If her mind does not become settled, we feed her until her mind becomes settled.
Similarly, when a sick person smells food that contains vinegar or the like, [i.e.,] substances that arouse a soul's [desire], he is governed by the same laws that apply to a pregnant woman.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Since he is sick, his condition is precarious and we are concerned that his craving may place his life in danger. See <i>Ketubot</i> 61a.</i>
When a person is overcome by severe hunger,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to a state of infirmity that overcomes a person because of lack of nourishment. He becomes dizzy, faint, and unable to focus his eyes.</i> he may be fed forbidden food immediately until his eyesight clears. We do not seek permitted food. Instead, we hurry to feed him what is available.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Out of fear that the delay may be crucial to his life. Similarly, we do not try first giving him the gravy and then smaller portions as in the previous halachot (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>). Needless to say, if kosher food is available, there is no reason to give him non-kosher food.</i><br>We feed him substances bound by more lenient prohibitions first. If his sight clears, that is sufficient. If not, we feed him the substances bound by the more severe prohibitions.
What is implied? If there is <i>tevel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Produce from which <i>terumah</i> and the tithes have not been separated.</i> and a <i>nevelah</i>, we feed him the <i>nevelah</i> first. {The rationale is] that [partaking of] <i>tevel</i> is punishable by death [at the hand of heaven].<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">This applies when the <i>terumot</i> have not been separated from the produce. If the<i> terumot</i> have been separated, but the tithes have not been separated, the prohibition is of the same degree of severity as partaking of a <i>nevelah</i>. See Chapter 10, Halachot 19-20.</i> If [the choice is between] a <i>nevelah</i> and produce that grows on its own during the Sabbatical year, we feed him the produce, for it is forbidden [only] by Rabbinic decree, as will be explained in <i>Hilchot Shemitah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Shemitah ViYovel</i> 4:2.</i><br>If [the choice is between] <i>tevel</i> and produce grown during the Sabbatical year,<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., produce grown in the Sabbatical year that remains after the time when it is supposed to be disposed of (Rashi, <i>Yoma</i> 83a).</i> we feed him the produce grown during the Sabbatical year.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">For the prohibition against eating produce cultivated in the Sabbatical year stems from a positive commandment. This is considered as more lenient than a prohibition stemming from a negative commandment, because there is no punishment involved.</i> If [the choice is between] <i>tevel</i> and <i>terumah</i>, if it is impossible to make the <i>tevel</i> acceptable,<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Some interpret this as referring to a situation where there is no one who knows how to separate the <i>terumot</i> present. Rashi (<i>loc. cit.</i>) interprets this as referring to a situation where the sick person must eat the entire amount of produce available. It is preferable not to separate the<i> terumot</i>. For even though he will be eating less of a forbidden substance, the prohibition will be more severe because <i>terumah</i> is sanctified.</i> we feed him the <i>tevel</i>. [The rationale is] that it is not sanctified as <i>terumah</i> is. Similar laws apply in all analogous situations.
We have already explained<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 17:8-9.</i> that one prohibition does not take effect when another prohibition is in effect unless both of the prohibitions take effect at the same time,<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 5, Halachah 5, gives an example of this concept. When a person rips a limb from a living animal which causes the animal to become <i>trefe</i>, he is considered to have transgressed two prohibitions: the prohibition against eating flesh from a living animal and the prohibition against partaking of an animal that is <i>trefe</i>, for both prohibitions take effect at the same time.</i> the latter prohibition forbids additional entities,<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">This concept is exemplified in this and the following halachah. See also Chapter 7, Halachah 2, and <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i> 17:9-10.</i> or the [latter] prohibition encompasses other entities.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">This concept is exemplified in Chapter 8, Halachah 6: A person who partakes of a <i>gid hanesheh</i>, a sciatic nerve, of an animal which is <i>trefe</i> is liable for two transgressions. Since when the animal became <i>trefe</i>, its entire body became encompassed in the prohibition, that prohibition also encompasses the <i>gid</i> even though it was prohibited beforehand.</i><br>Accordingly, [it is possible] for there to be a person who eats one olive-sized portion of forbidden food and yet, he will be liable for five [sets of] lashes for it, provided he was warned for all five prohibitions that accumulated.<br>What is implied? For example, on Yom Kippur, a person who was ritually impure ate an olive-sized portion of forbidden fat from a consecrated animal that remained after its prescribed time.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Every sacrifice of which we are allowed to partake has a certain time span - a day and a night or two days and a night - in which we are allowed to partake of it. After that time span, it becomes forbidden because of the prohibition referred to as <i>notar</i> (<i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 18:10.</i> He is liable for lashes because he partook of forbidden fat, <i>notar</i>, because he ate on Yom Kippur,<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam also states this concept in <i>Hilchot Shegagot</i> 6:4. There he emphasizes that to be liable for eating on Yom Kippur, one must add another small portion of food. For one is not liable for eating on Yom Kippur unless he consumes a date-sized portion. That additional portion, however, need not involve all these different prohibitions.</i> because he partook of consecrated food while ritually impure, and because he derived benefit from consecrated food, thus [violating the prohibition of] <i>me'ilah</i>.
Why do these prohibitions fall on each other? Because although it was forbidden to partake of the fat of this animal, it was permitted to benefit from it. Once he consecrated it, it became forbidden to benefit from the fat. Since the prohibition to benefit from it was added to it, the prohibition against [benefiting from] consecrated articles became added to it.<br>Although this fat was forbidden to an ordinary person, it was still permitted to be offered to the One on High. When it became <i>notar</i>, since it became forbidden to the One on High, [that] prohibition was added to an ordinary person.<br>This person was permitted to partake of the meat of the animal,<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Before it became <i>notar</i>.</i> although he was forbidden to partake of its fat. When he became impure, since its meat became forbidden an additional prohibition was added to its fat. When Yom Kippur commenced, all food became included [in the prohibition], since this prohibition affects non-consecrated food, it adds a prohibition to this fat. Similar laws apply in all analogous situations.

Chapter 15

When a forbidden substance becomes mixed with a permitted substance of another type, [it causes it to become forbidden] if its flavor can be detected. When [a forbidden substance becomes mixed with a permitted substance of] the same type and it is impossible to detect [the forbidden substance] by its flavor,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Because it tastes the same as the permitted substance.</i> its presence becomes nullified if there is a majority [of the permitted substance].<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">According to Scriptural Law. As stated in Halachot 4-5, the Rabbis enforced more stringent requirements.</i>
What is implied? When the fat of the kidneys<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Which is forbidden (Chapter 7, Halachah 5).</i> falls into beans and becomes dissolved, the beans should be tasted.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">By a gentile (see the notes to Halachah 30 and Chapter 9, Halachah 8) for a discussion of why the gentile's word is accepted.</i> If the taste of fat cannot be detected, they are permitted. If [not only] the taste, [but also] the substance of the fat is present, they are forbidden according to Scriptural Law. If the flavor could be detected, but there is no substance, they are forbidden by Rabbinic Law.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Tosafot</i> (<i>Chullin</i> 98b) which mentions a difference of opinion among the Rabbis if the principle "the flavor of an entity is equivalent to its substance" is of Rabbinic or Scriptural origin.</i>
What is meant by its substance? For example, there was enough forbidden fat for there to be an olive-sized portion [of fat] in each portion the size of three eggs from the mixture. If a person eats a portion of beans the size of three eggs, he is liable for lashes for they contain an olive-sized portion of forbidden fat, for [not only] the flavor, [but also] the substance of [the forbidden fat] is present.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam shares the perspective of <i>Tosafot</i> (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 67b) who maintains that if there is more than an olive-sized portion of fat in a portion of food <i>k'dei achilat p'ras</i> (the size of three eggs), its substance is considered as present even though it is dissolved and not discernable. Rashi differs and maintains that as long as the fat is dissolved, it is considered as if the substance of the forbidden entity is not present.</i> If one eats less than a portion the size of three eggs [of the mixture], one is liable for stripes for rebellious conduct as prescribed by Rabbinic Law.<br>Similarly, if there was less than an olive-sized portion of [forbidden] fat in every portion the size of three eggs, even if the flavor of fat is detectable and he eats the entire pot, he is not liable for lashes<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Even though he may eat an olive-sized portion of forbidden fat, he will not have eaten it in the required time (the time it takes to eat three eggs, as stated in Chapter 14, Halachah 8) for one to be held liable. The concentration of the forbidden fat is too small for that to happen.</i> [as prescribed by Scriptural Law], only stripes for rebellious conduct.
[The following laws apply when] the fat of the kidneys<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Which is forbidden (Chapter 7, Halachah 5).</i> falls into the fat from the fat tail<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Which is permitted.</i> and the entire [mixture] becomes dissolved.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Since both are fat, the mixture is considered as being of the same substance.</i> If there is twice as much fat from the fat tail as fat of the kidneys, the entire mixture is permitted according to Scriptural Law.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">For according to Scriptural Law, as long as the majority is kosher, the mixture may be eaten. Indeed, there is no need for there to be twice as much kosher fat as non-kosher fat. A simple majority is sufficient.</i> Even when a piece of [meat from] a <i>nevelah</i> becomes mixed with two pieces of [meat from] a ritually slaughter animal, everything is permitted according to Scriptural Law.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">With regard to the mixture of fat, there is greater reason for leniency, for there is no longer any non-kosher fat that exists as an independent entity, it is all mixed together with the kosher fat. In this instance, the meat from the <i>nevelah</i> exists as an independent entity, it is just that we have no way of detecting which of the pieces it is (Radbaz).</i> Nevertheless, according to Rabbinic Law, everything<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">In all instances when forbidden substances are mixed with kosher substances. See <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 109:1) which rules leniently and allows one to rely on Scriptural Law in certain situations. See the notes to Halachah 20.</i> is forbidden until the forbidden substance will be nullified because of the tiny proportion [of the entire mixture it represents] to the extent that it is not significant and it is as if it does not exist, as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">In the following halachot.</i>
Into what quantity [of a permitted substance] must a forbidden substance be mixed for it to be considered nullified because of its tiny proportion? [Each forbidden substance according to] the measure the Sages specified for it. There are substances that are nullified in a mixture 60 times its size, others in a mixture 100 times its size, and still others in a mixture 200 times its size.
Thus we learn from this that [the following laws apply] with regard to all of the prohibited substances in the Torah, whether those punishable by lashes or punishable by<i> kerait</i> or substances from which it is forbidden to benefit that become mixed with permitted substances. If the substances are of different types, [the mixture is forbidden] if the flavor is detectable.<br>If the substances are of the same type and thus it is impossible to detect the flavor [of the forbidden substance], we measure [whether there was] 60, 100, or 200 [times the amount of permitted substances]. The only exceptions are wine poured as a libation to a false deity, because of the severity [of the prohibition against] worship of a false deity<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 5:8), the Rambam cites Deuteronomy 13:18: "No trace of the condemned should cling to your hand" as evidence that even the slightest amount is forbidden. See Chapter 16, Halachah 28, for a leniency that is granted with regard to this restriction.</i> and <i>tevel</i>, because it can be corrected.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>terumot</i> and the tithes can be separated from it, causing it to be permitted. See Halachah 10.<br>In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>ibid.</i>), the Rambam cites the rationale given by<i> Avodah Zarah</i> 73b: Just as one kernel of grain can serve as <i>terumah</i> and correct the entire crop; so, too, one kernel of grain from which <i>terumah</i> was not separated can cause an entire crop to be forbidden. The rationale given by the Rambam here has its source in the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Shivi'it</i> 6:3).</i> For that reason, even the slightest mixture of them with a substance of their type is forbidden. If they become mixed with substances of a different type, the matter is dependent on whether their flavor is detectable.
What is implied? When several barrels of wine fell over a drop of wine that was poured as a libation, the entire mixture is forbidden, as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 16, Halachah 28.</i> Similarly, if a cup of wine which is <i>tevel</i> becomes nixed into a barrel [of wine], the entire [barrel] is considered <i>tevel</i> until the amount of <i>terumah</i> and tithes that are appropriate to be separated<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the <i>terumah</i> and tithes that would have been required to have been separated from the <i>tevel</i> originally. In this context, the fact that it became mixed with other wine is not significant.</i> are separated as will be explained in the appropriate place.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Terumah</i>, Chapter 13.</i>
[Related concepts apply with regard to] produce of the Sabbatical year.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam feels it necessary to mention this point, because his source, <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 73b mentions the produce of the Sabbatical year together with the two prohibited substances mentioned above. The Rambam clarifies that the comparison is not entirely correct, because the produce of the Sabbatical year is not forbidden.</i> If [such produce] becomes mixed with [produce of] the same type, the tiniest amount [causes the mixture to be considered bound by the laws of the produce of the Sabbatical year].<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad mentions that this concept applies only until the time it is required to destroy the produce of the Sabbatical year. After that time, that produce is forbidden to be eaten and hence, is considered like other forbidden substances.</i> [If it becomes mixed with produce of] another type, [the ruling depends on whether] its flavor can be detected. [Nevertheless,] it is not considered as one of the substances forbidden by Scriptural Law. For this mixture is not forbidden. Instead, one is obligated to eat the entire mixture in keeping with the holiness of the produce of the Sabbatical year, as will be explained in the appropriate place.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Shemitrah ViYovel</i>, chs. 4-7.</i>
Although <i>chametz</i> on Pesach is forbidden by Scriptural Law, it is not governed by these general principles,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., although even the tiniest amount of <i>chametz</i> causes an entire mixture to be forbidden, <i>chametz</i> was not mentioned by <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 73b together with wine poured as a libation and <i>tevel</i>. The reason is that the prohibition of the mixture of <i>chametz</i> is motivated by a different rationale (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> for this mixture is not forbidden forever. For after Pesach, the entire mixture will be permitted, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Chametz UMatzah</i> 1:5 which states that a mixture of <i>chametz</i> and another substance is permitted after the Pesach holiday.</i> Therefore the slightest amount [of <i>chametz</i>] causes [a mixture] to become forbidden,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the following halachah and notes. See also Halachah 12.</i> whether [it becomes mixed] with a substance of its own type or of another type.
The same law<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz states that the comparison is to <i>tevel</i> and not to <i>chametz</i> on Pesach. For like <i>tevel</i>, if it becomes mixed with a different substance, it is permitted if its flavor cannot be detected. There are special stringencies applied with regard to <i>chametz</i>, as stated in Halachah 12 (Radbaz).</i> applies when new grain becomes mixed with old grain before [the offering of] the <i>omer</i>. Even the tiniest amount causes [the entire mixture] to become forbidden. For there is a factor that will cause the substance to become permitted. For after [the offering of] the <i>omer</i>, the entire mixture is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 10, Halachah 2.</i><br>Similarly, whenever there is a factor that will cause the substance to become permitted, e.g., consecrated entities,<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">For they can be redeemed (Radbaz).</i> the second tithe,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">For they can be eaten in Jerusalem or their holiness can be transferred to money (Radbaz).</i> or the like,<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., <i>Bikkurim</i>; see the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Bkkurim</i> 2:2).</i> our Sages did not mention a measure [in which it could be nullified]. Instead, even if one [of the forbidden substance] becomes mixed with several thousand<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Our Sages (<i>Beitzah</i> 3b) state it is not nullified when mixed with 1000 times the amount of kosher substances. The Rambam's wording clarifies that 1000 is not an upper limit. No matter how many times more of the permitted substance there is, the mixture is forbidden.</i> [times that amount of a permitted substance], it is not nullified. [The rationale is that] there is a way that the prohibition can be released.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, this option should be taken rather than relying on the nullification of the forbidden substance.</i> [This principle applies] even when the prohibition stems from Rabbinic decree, e.g., an article set aside or born on a festival.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <i>Hilchot Sh'vitat Yom Tov</i> 1:19 that when a person sets an object aside before the holiday with the intent that he will not use it on the holiday, he may not change his mind and use it on the holiday. This prohibition is referred to as <i>muktzeh</i>.<br>Halachah 1:20 states that an egg laid on a holiday following the Sabbath was prepared on the Sabbath, as it were. Therefore it may not be used on the holiday. This prohibition is referred to as <i>nolad</i>. Both of these prohibitions are of Rabbinic origin. Halachah 1:21 states that if such an egg becomes mixed with other eggs, they are all forbidden.</i>
With regard to <i>orlah</i>, mixed species grown in a vineyard, fat, blood, and the like, our Sages fixed a measure [that would enable mixtures to be nullified]. Similarly, our Sages fixed a measure with regard to <i>terumot</i>, for there is no way it can be permitted for all people.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a mixture of <i>terumah</i> could be eaten in a permitted manner by a priest. Nevertheless, since there is no way it could be permitted to an ordinary person, our Sages were not stringent (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).<br>Both the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> and the Radbaz ask: It is possible to have to have <i>terumah </i>permitted by making a statement of regret concerning its separation before a wise man. If so, seemingly, it should be considered as an object that could be eaten in a permitted manner. The Radbaz explains that a wise man who can nullify the separation of <i>terumah</i> may not always be found. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> states that since this is not the common practice, a substance may not be considered as an object that could be eaten in a permitted manner for this reason.</i>
It appears to me<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">This term indicates a conclusion deduced by the Rambam without an explicit prior Rabbinic source. There are others, including Rav Yitzchak Alfasi and Rav Moshe HaCohen, who differ and maintain that since the mixture could be eaten in a permitted manner, the above stringencies apply. The Ra'avad, however, states that this concept is explicitly stated in the Mishnah. He does not, however, mention which mishnah. The Radbaz and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> offer different hypotheses as to the Ra'avad's intent and why the Rambam did not accept it.</i> that even there is a factor that will cause a substance to become permitted, if that substance becomes mixed with a substance of a different type and its flavor is not detectable, it is permitted. The fact that there is a factor that will cause the substance to become permitted does not [cause the prohibition to be] more severe than <i>tevel</i>. [For <i>tevel</i>] can be corrected,<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">By separating the appropriate <i>terumot</i> and tithes.</i> and yet when it [becomes mixed with a substance] of a different type, [it is permitted if] its flavor cannot be detected, as explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Halachah 6.</i> One should not raise a question with regard to <i>chametz</i> on Pesach [where such leniency is not granted. A distinction can be made.] For with regard to <i>chametz</i>, the Torah [Exodus 12:20] states: "Do not eat any leavened substance." For this reason, [our Sages] were stringent with regard to it, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Halachah 9.</i>
These are the measures which the Sages established: <i>Terumah, terumat ma'aser<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>terumah</i> which the Levites offer from the tithes they are given.</i> challah</i>, and <i>bikkurim</i> become nullified [when the mixture is] 101 times the [original] amount. [In addition,] one must separate [a portion and give it to a priest].<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Although the prohibition is negated, we are still concerned with the fact that property due the priest is not given to him, as stated in Halachah 15.</i> [All of these sacred foods] are combined one with the other.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., as stated in the conclusion of the halachah, if two of these substances fall into the same accumulation of permitted substances, it is necessary to have 100 times their combined size.</i> Similarly, a slice of the showbread becomes nullified when mixed with slices of ordinary bread [if] the mixture is 101 times the original amount.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> notes that the Rambam's ruling here appears to contradict his ruling in <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 6:24 where he states that the showbread is not nullified. He explains that in <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i>, the Rambam is speaking about pieces of the showbread that are ritually pure. Hence the entire mixture should be eaten by the priests. (Note the Radbaz who questions how the priests could eat the mixture.) Here, by contrast, we are speaking about pieces of the showbread that are impure. If the showbread was a significant part of the mixture, the entire mixture would have to be burnt. Since it is not significant, we considered its existence negated.</i><br>What is implied? When a <i>se'ah</i> of flour from one of the above<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Terumah, terumat ma'aser challah</i>, or <i>bikkurim</i>.</i>- or one <i>se'ah</i> from all of them [combined] - falls into 100<i> se'ah</i> of ordinary [flour] and [the flour] became mixed together,<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, the sacred substances are distinct, they must be separated from the ordinary substances.</i> one should separate one <i>se'ah</i> from the mixture for the <i>se'ah</i> that fell in originally. The remainder is permitted to all people.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">It does not have to eaten with attention to the laws of <i>terumah</i>.</i> If it fell into less than 100 <i>se'ah</i>, the entire mixture is <i>meduma</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">This term refers to a mixture of <i>terumah</i> or other sacred substances with ordinary substances. The mixture must be sold to priests (at the price of <i>terumah</i>) with the exception of the original sacred amount (<i>Hilchot Terumah</i> 13:2).</i>
<i>Orlah</i> and mixed species grown in a vineyard become nullified [when the mixture is] 201 times the [original] amount. The [two prohibitions] are combined one with the other,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Although they are separate and unrelated prohibitions, since it is forbidden to benefit from both of them and we derive the laws pertaining to one from the laws pertaining to the other, we rule that they may be combined (<i>Orlah</i> 2:1).</i> and it is not necessary to separate any thing.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the following halachah, in this instance, one is not causing the priests a loss.</i><br>What is implied? When a <i>revi'it</i> of wine which is <i>orlah</i> or which [came from grapes] grown together with mixed species in a vineyard - or one <i>revi'it</i> was combined from both prohibited substances - falls into 200<i> revi'iot</i> of wine, the entire mixture is permitted. It is not necessary to separate anything. If it falls into less than 200, it is forbidden to benefit from the entire [mixture].<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad questions the Rambam's ruling, objecting to the decision that it is forbidden to benefit from the mixture. (He maintains that although partaking of the mixture is forbidden, one should be able to sell it to a gentile with the exception of the value of the forbidden substance. For, he maintains, it is never forbidden to benefit from a mixture that is not inherently forbidden.) The Radbaz justifies the Rambam's view.</i>
Why is it necessary to separate [a measure of] <i>terumah</i> and not a measure of <i>orlah</i> or mixed species from a vineyard? Because <i>terumah </i>is the property of the priests. Accordingly, any <i>terumah</i> which the priests are not concerned with, e.g., <i>terumah</i> from [low-grade] figs,<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 11:4).</i> carobs, and Edomite barley, need not be separated.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">For the priests will not be concerned about its loss.</i>
Why was the measure doubled for <i>orlah</i> and mixed species grown in a vineyard? Because it is forbidden to benefit from them.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore they were treated more stringently. The Radbaz emphasizes that they are compared to <i>terumah</i> and not to other forbidden substances for the root <i>kodesh</i> is used with regard to them.</i><br>Why did [the Sages] choose the figure of 100 for <i>terumot</i>? For<i> terumat ma'aser</i> is one hundredth of the entire crop,<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">For it is one tenth of a tenth.</i> and yet it causes the entire crop to be "sanctified,"<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., "forbidden."</i> as [Numbers 18:29] states: "its sacred part."<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">As the Rambam continues to explain, the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Orlah</i> 2:1) offers a non-literal interpretation of this phrase, understanding it as meaning "the one who sanctifies it."</i> Our Sages said: "An entity which must be separated from it sanctifies it if it returns to it.
The measure for all of the other prohibitions of the Torah,<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">With this wording, the Rambam also eliminates those prohibitions of Rabbinic origin, which have a smaller measure as stated in the following halachah.</i> e.g., the meat of crawling animals, teeming animals, fat, blood, and the like is sixty times [the original amount].<br>What is implied? When an olive-sized portion of the fat of the kidneys falls into sixty times the size of an olive of the fat from the fat tail, the entire mixture is permitted. If it falls into less than sixty [that amount], the entire mixture is forbidden. Similarly, if a portion of forbidden fat the size of a barley-corn, [the mixture] must contain permitted substances the size of sixty barley-corns.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Although there is not enough of the forbidden substance for a person to be liable for lashes according to Scriptural Law, unless there is 60 times the amount of forbidden fat, the mixture is forbidden according to Scriptural Law (see <i>Chulin</i> 98a).</i> Similar [laws apply] with regard to other prohibitions.<br>Similarly, if the fat of the <i>gid hanesheh</i> falls into a pot of meat,<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibitions are considered of the same type, because the taste of the fat is not distinct from that of the meat (Rabbenu Yitzchak Alfasi to <i>Chulin</i> 98a).</i> we require sixty times its amount. The fat of the <i>gid</i> itself is included in this sum.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">As is the law with regard to Rabbinic prohibitions as stated in the following halachah.</i> Although the fat of the <i>gid</i> is prohibited [only] by Rabbinic Law, as we explained,<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 8, Halachah 1.</i> since the <i>gid hanesheh</i> is considered a creation in its own right,<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">As our Sages ruled [<i>Chulin</i> 100a; Chapter 16, Halachah 6; quoted by <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 100:1)], a forbidden being which is a creation in its own right is never forbidden. Therefore they ruled more stringently. In this instance, the <i>gid hanesheh</i> itself will be removed. Hence the full stringency of our Sages' ruling is not applied, nevertheless, in recognition of the serious of the prohibition involved, this stringency is applied.</i> [our Sages] ruled stringently concerning it as if it was forbidden by Scriptural Law. The <i>gid</i> itself is not measured and it does not cause other substances to be forbidden, because the <i>gid</i> does not impart flavor.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 8, Halachah 6.</i>
When, by contrast, an udder is cooked with meat, we require sixty times its amount and the udder is considered as part of the sum.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Thus instead of requiring 61 times the forbidden substance (60 plus the substance itself) all that is required is 60 (59 and the substance itself).</i> [The rationale is that] since [the prohibition against] the udder is Rabbinic in origin, [our Sages] were lenient in establishing a measure.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 9, Halachot 12-13. As explained in the notes there, the Ra'avad and the Rashba offer a different rationale for this ruling, explaining that since the meat of the udder is acceptable, we include it in the reckoning of 60. Thus in contrast to other instances where 60 times the amount of the forbidden substance is required, here, we require only 59. According to his view, we cannot extrapolate from this ruling to other Rabbinic prohibitions.</i>
[The following laws apply when] an egg in which a chick is found<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Such an egg is forbidden to be eaten (see Chapter 3, Halachah 8).</i> is cooked together with eggs that are permitted. If there are 61 and it,<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a total of 62.</i> they are permitted. If, however, there are only sixty [permitted eggs], the entire mixture is forbidden. [The rationale is that the chick] is a creation in its own right,<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in the notes to the previous halachah, in this instance, the forbidden substance itself will be removed. Hence the full stringency of our Sages' ruling concerning an entity that is a creation in its own right is not applied. Nevertheless, in recognition of the serious of the prohibition involved, this stringency is applied. See the notes to the following halachah where a rationale cited by other authorities is mentioned.</i> [our Sages] made a distinction and added to its [required] measure.
If, however, the egg of an non-kosher fowl was cooked together with the eggs of kosher fowl, it does not cause them to become forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Here we are talking about eggs that are cooked in their shells. When an egg contains a chick, the chick will impart its flavor to the entire pot. When, by contrast, eggs are cooked in their shells, they do not impart flavor (<i>Chulin</i> 97b). The non-kosher egg must be removed from the mixture. This, however, can sometimes be done, because the appearance of non-kosher eggs may differ from that of kosher eggs (Chapter 3, Halachah 18).<br>The Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 86:6) differs and states that if a substantial loss will not be caused, we should be stringent and follow the same ruling with regard to all eggs. If, however, there will be a substantial loss, even he counsels to rely on the more lenient views.</i><br>If [the eggs were opened and] mixed together or the egg of a non-kosher fowl or the egg of a fowl that is <i>trefe</i> become mixed with other eggs,<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">For, at times, non-kosher eggs are not distinguishable from kosher eggs (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> the required measure is 60.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the mixture is judged as an ordinary instance in which kosher food becomes mixed with non-kosher food. According to the Rambam, the non-kosher egg is not a creation that is forbidden in its own right.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 86:5) differs and rules that we require 61 kosher eggs in this instance as well. The Ramban explains that the reason is that not all eggs are the same size and by adding an extra egg, we make certain that we have the necessary amount. (He uses this rationale to explain the law stated in the previous halachah as well.) The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 86:15 offers a different rationale, stating that an egg itself is considered a creation in its own right.<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> notes that there are some who would rule that 60 eggs are not necessary, for there are opinions [see <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 109:1)] that when kosher and non-kosher entities are intermingled in a dry mixture, we rely on Scriptural Law and require only a simple majority of the kosher substances. As obvious from this ruling, the Rambam does not accept this leniency.</i>
What is the source because of which the Sages relied on the measure of 60? For the portion given [to the priest] from the ram brought by a Nazirite,<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">When a Nazirite completes the days of his Nazirite vow, he brings several sacrifices. Among them is a ram brought as a peace offering. The foreleg from this offering is given to the priest and may not be eaten by an ordinary Israelite (<i>Chulin</i> 98a; <i>Hilchot Nazirut</i> 8:1-4).</i>i.e., the foreleg, is one sixtieth of the remainder of the ram. It is cooked together with it and does not cause it to be forbidden,<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., although this portion which is forbidden to an Israelite is cooked together with the entire ram, the Israelite is permitted to partake of the remainder of the ram. Accordingly, our Sages inferred that a similar ratio may be used when other prohibited substances are cooked with permitted substances.</i> as [Numbers 6:19] states: "And the priest shall take the cooked foreleg from the ram."
[The following rules apply when] two substances of the same type, [one permitted and one forbidden,] and a [third] entity become mixed together, e.g., there was a pot with fat from the fat tail and beans and fat from the kidneys fell into it. The entire [mixture] dissolved and became a single entity. We view the fat from the fat tail and the beans as a single entity and we measure the fat from the kidneys against it. If the ratio was one to sixty, it is permitted. For it is impossible to detect the taste.<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Because there is no significant difference between the taste of fat from the fat tail and fat from the kidneys, as indicated by Halachah 4.</i>
The same principle applies when <i>terumot</i> are mixed together [with other substances, some of the same type and some of a different type], their measure is 100. And the measure of mixed species from a vineyard and <i>orlah</i> is 200.
When we calculate the measure of permitted substances with regard to all prohibitions, whether the measure is 60, 100, or 200, we include the soup, the spices, everything that is in the pot, and what the pot has absorbed after the prohibited substance fell according to our estimation.<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., at that time, it absorbed both the permitted substances and the prohibited substance (Radbaz).<br>In his <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> and in his <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 99:4), Rav Yosef Caro follows the view of Rashi and the <i>Tur</i> who maintain that we should measure the prohibited substance and the permitted substances as they are at present, for there is no way of knowing how much the pot absorbed. This stringency applies, however, only with regard to forbidden substances mixed with permitted substances of a different type. (For then the prohibition stems from Scriptural Law.) If they are of the same type (when a simple majority is required according to Scriptural Law), the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> rules more leniently and accepts the Rambam's ruling.</i> For it is impossible to know the exact amount which the pot absorbed.
It is forbidden to nullify a substance<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., after a forbidden substance fell into a mixture, one may not add enough permitted substances that there will 60, 100, or 200 times the amount of the forbidden substance.</i> forbidden by Scriptural<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, a substance forbidden by Rabbinic Law accidentally fell into a mixture, one may add enough permitted substances to nullify the prohibition, as stated in Halachah 26.</i> Law as an initial and preferred measure. If, however, one nullified it, the mixture is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Because in fact the presence of the forbidden substance has been nullified.</i> Nevertheless, our Sages penalized such a person and forbade the entire mixture.<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 99:11 explains the reason for this penalty. If we would permit him to benefit from it, we fear that if, in the future, such a situation would recur, he would instruct his servants to nullify the prohibited substances for him.</i> It appears to me that since this is a penalty, we forbid this mixture only to the person<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Or the person whom he intended to serve after nullifying the forbidden substance. Were this not the case, he would benefit from his undesirable act (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> who transgressed and nullified the prohibited substance.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's wording implies that the penalty was imposed only when he willfully nullified the existence of the forbidden substance. If he did so accidentally or inadvertently, no prohibition applies, for our Sages did not impose penalties in such situations [<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>; <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 99:5)].</i> For others, however, the entire mixture is permitted.
What is implied? If a <i>se'ah</i> of <i>orlah</i> falls into 100 <i>se'ah</i> [of permitted produce], the entire [mixture] is forbidden. One should not bring another 100 <i>se'ah</i> and join [the entire quantity] together so that [the forbidden substance] will be nullified because of the presence of 201 times the original amount. If, however, he transgressed and did so, the entire [mixture] is permitted.<br>With regard to a prohibition forbidden by Rabbinic decree,<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">This point was not accepted by all authorities. The Ashkenazic authorities (as reflected by the ruling of Rabbenu Asher) maintain that even a Rabbinic prohibition should not be nullified as an initial and preferred measure. The Radbaz proposes an intermediate position: that the stringency should be applied only to Rabbinic prohibitions that have a source in Scriptural Law, e.g., milk and fowl, but not those enacted by the Rabbis entirely on their own initiative. This compromise, however, was not accepted. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 99:6) adopts the Rambam's position, while the <i>Tur</i> and the Rama follow that of Rabbenu Asher.</i> we do nullify a prohibition as an initial and preferred measure.
What is implied? If milk fell into a pot that contains fowl and imparted its flavor to the food, one may add other fowl to the pot until the flavor [of the milk] is no longer discernable. Similar laws apply in all analogous situations.
We already explained<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Halachah 1.</i> that if a forbidden substance imparts its flavor to a permitted substance, the entire mixture is not permitted. When does the above apply? [When the flavor imparted] improves [the flavor of the permitted food]. If, however, the forbidden substance detracts from the flavor of the permitted substance and impairs it, it is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Avodah Zarah</i> 66a derives this concept from the statements of Deuteronomy 14:21 concerning the meat of an animal that died without slaughter: "Give it to the stranger in your gate and he will partake of it." Implied is that the prohibition applies only to meat that is fit for a non-Jew to partake of. If it is not fit for the non-Jew to eat, it cannot cause a Jew's food to be forbidden.<br>See also <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 103:2) which states: "The impairment of the food's flavor does not have to be complete to the extent that one would be disgusted to eat it. Instead, even if it slightly detracts [from its flavor], it does not cause the mixture to become forbidden."</i><br>[This applies] provided it detracts from its flavor from the beginning until the end. If, however, it detracted from its flavor at the outset, but ultimately improved it or improved it initially, even though it will ultimately detract from it, [the mixture] is forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Avodah Zarah, loc. cit.</i> states that if initially, the flavor of a substance is improved by the addition of the forbidden substance, it becomes forbidden. The fact that ultimately the addition detracts from the flavor of the permitted substance is not sufficient to cause it to become permitted again. The Rambam draws the conclusion that ultimately if the flavor of the substance will be improved, it is also prohibited (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).<br>Note, however, the <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 103:7 who states that it is permitted to partake of a mixture after the flavor of the permitted substance was impaired, before it improved, even though one knows that ultimately, it will improve. Based on the wording of the following halachah, however, it is questionable if the Rambam would accept this conclusion.</i>
Who will taste the mixture?<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., since there is a question whether the mixture is forbidden or not.</i> If <i>terumot</i> were mixed with ordinary crops, a priest should taste the mixture.<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">For he is permitted to partake of <i>terumot</i>.</i> If the flavor of the <i>terumah</i> is discernible, the entire mixture is considered as <i>miduma</i>. In <i>Hilchot Terumot</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Chs. 13 and 14.</i> the laws pertaining to [produce that is] <i>miduma</i> will be explained.
If [the mixture involved] meat and milk, wine poured as a libation, wine that was <i>orlah</i>, or [made from grapes that grew together with] mixed species in a vineyard that fell into honey, or the meat of crawling animals or teeming animals that were cooked with vegetables and the like, a gentile should taste [the mixture].<br>We rely on his word.<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">There is a difficulty with the Rambam's statements, for generally, we do not rely on the word of a gentile with regard to ritual matters. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 98:1) relies on the opinion of the Rashba quoted by the <i>Tur</i>, that this refers to a situation where the gentile does not know that we are relying on him, but instead makes his statements as a matter of course (<i>masiach lifi tomo</i>).<br>See also the <i>Turei Zahav</i> 98:2 and the <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 98:2 who quote views which state that an ordinary gentile is not sufficient, but instead, the intent is a gentile chef who is an expert on recognizing flavors. According to some, however, this interpretation leads to a leniency. For since he is a professional, he will not risk his professional reputation by lying to mislead a Jew. Hence, according to these views, his statements can be accepted even if they are made in response to direct questions and not as a matter of course. There are, nevertheless, authorities who differ and require even a chef to make his statements as a matter of course. Moreover, there are authorities (among them, the Radbaz and the Rama) who never accept the statements of a gentile with regard to these matters.<br>The Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 98:1) states that in the present age, we do not rely on the statements of a non-Jew who tasted food to determine whether it is kosher or not.</i> If he says: "It does not have the flavor [of the forbidden substance]," or he says: "It [imparted] its flavor, but that flavor is bad and it detracts [from the flavor of the permitted substance," the entire [mixture] is permitted, provided it will not ultimately improve it, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Halachah 28.</i> If there is no gentile to taste it, we rely on the measures of 60,<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">For it is an accepted principle that the taste of a forbidden substance will be nullified in more than 60 times its volume.</i> 100, or 200.<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to mixtures of <i>terumot, orlah</i>, and mixed species in a vineyard. The ruling is, however, problematic. For if we are speaking about a mixture of these substances together with different substances, then 60 times their volume will be sufficient. For the taste of all substances except spices is nullified in 60 times their volume as stated in the previous note. And if we are speaking about a mixture of substances with their own kind, the taste of the forbidden substance will not be detectable.</i>
When a rat falls into beer or vinegar, we require a measure of 60, for we suspect that it imparted its flavor to the beer or the vinegar and it improves it.<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Avodah Zarah</i> 68b leaves unresolved the question whether the rat's flavor detracts from the flavor of beer and vinegar. Hence we rule stringently.</i>When, however, it falls into wine, oil, or honey,<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">These substances are not mentioned by the Talmud, <i>loc. cit.</i>, but it is common knowledge that the rat's flavor will detract from their own, as the Rambam explains (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> it is permitted, even if it imparts its flavor, for the [rat's] flavor detracts [from the flavor of these substances]. For [these substances] must all have a pleasant fragrance and rat meat spoils their aroma and detracts from their flavor.
When a goat is roasted in its fat, it is forbidden to eat from even the tip of its ear. [The rationale is that] the fat permeates through all its limbs, improves [their taste], and imparts flavor. Accordingly, if [a goat] is lean and possessed only a meager amount of fat on its kidneys and digestive organs,<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">These are prohibited by Scriptural Law.</i> i.e., one in sixty-one [of the entire animal], one may cut away [the meat] and eat it<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">In his <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, Rav Yosef Caro states that, according to the Rambam, one may partake of the meat as it is. He need not scrape off or cut away its surface (<i>kelipah</i> or <i>netilah</i>).
