In his <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 105:5), however, he rules according to the perspective of Rabbenu Asher, the Rashba, the Ran, and the <i>Tur</i> who maintain that the outer layer of the meat next to the fat itself must be cut away.<br>See also the ruling of the Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah, loc. cit.</i>) that at present, we are not capable of differentiating which fat is considered succulent and which is considered lean. Hence, we require 60 times the amount of forbidden fat in all instances.</i> until he reaches the fat.<br>Similarly, when the thigh [of an animal] is roasted<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> emphasizes that the leniency mentioned by the Rambam applies only when the animal is roasted with its <i>gid</i> or with the forbidden tissues. If it is cooked, more stringent rules apply.</i> together with the <i>gid hanesheh</i>, one may cut away [the meat] and eat it until he reaches the <i>gid [hanesheh]</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Although the fat of the <i>gid hanesheh</i> is prohibited, there is not enough fat to cause the other limbs of the animal to become prohibited (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>). Nevertheless, one must cut away the outer layer of the meat next to the <i>gid</i>.</i> [This], he should cast away. Similarly, if an animal was roasted whole without removing the forbidden strands of tissue and membranes, one may cut away [the meat] and eat it.<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance as well, there is not a significant enough quantity of fat to cause the meat to become forbidden.</i> When he reaches a forbidden substance, he should cast it away. There is no need to calculate the ratio [of this forbidden tissue to the meat,] for this [forbidden] tissue does not impart flavor.
One should not roast ritually slaughtered meat with the meat of a<i> nevelah</i> or the meat of a non-kosher species in one oven, even though they do not touch each other.<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 108:1) quotes the Rambam's ruling forbidding roasting the two in the same oven, but allows for certain leniencies, e.g., the oven is very large or the substances are covered.</i> If one roasted them together, [the kosher meat] is permitted. [This applies] even if the forbidden meat was very succulent and the permitted meat was lean. For an aroma does not cause a substance to become forbidden; only the flavor of a forbidden substance does.
When the meat of a ritually slaughtered animal<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">It appears that the kosher meat was unsalted. If, however, it were salted, it would not absorb the juices of the non-kosher meat, as indicated by the principle: "One that is involved in discharging its own juices does not absorb from another" (Radbaz).</i> was mixed together<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries note an apparent contradiction to the Rambam's rulings in Chapter 7, Halachot 17-19. The Radbaz explains that there, both the forbidden and the kosher substances were salted, while here the kosher meat was not. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that here the two pieces of meat are mixed together, while there the substances were merely near each other.</i> with the meat of a <i>nevelah</i> that was salted, the [kosher] meat becomes prohibited,<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">In this halachah, the Rambam is communicating the principle stated by <i>Chulin</i> 111b <i>et al</i> that meat which is salted is considered as if it is burning hot. It emits concentrated juices which are absorbed by other meat.</i> for the concentrated [juices] of the <i>nevelah</i> are absorbed in the kosher meat. It is impossible to detect their flavor or to calculate the quantity of the forbidden substance.<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., no matter what the ration of the kosher meat to the non-kosher meat, the mixture is forbidden (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).<br>The <i>Tur</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 70:3-4) follows the more lenient views of Rabbenu Asher, the Rashba, and the Ran who maintain that only the external surface of the kosher meat becomes forbidden. Once it is peeled off, the meat is permitted.</i><br>When the meat of a unsalted species of kosher fish was mixed together with the meat of a species of unkosher fish that was salted, the [kosher] fish becomes prohibited because of the [non-kosher] brine. If, however, it was the kosher fish that was salted and the non-kosher fish was unsalted, the salted fish does not become forbidden. For even though the unsalted [fish] absorbs [the brine] of the salted one, it does not absorb it to the degree that it will cause it to discharge [its own brine].<br>When a non-kosher fish was pickled with a kosher fish, the entire mixture is forbidden unless the ratio of kosher fish to non-kosher is 200:1.<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">For pickling is considered equivalent to cooking. Fish brine is considered as very powerful. Hence it requires a much larger measure than ordinary non-kosher substances. See Rav Kapach's notes to the Rambam's Commentary on the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 10:8) where he writes that the Rambam changed his mind three times on this issue, twice stating more stringent views than the one stated here, before writing this view as his final conclusion.</i>

Chapter 16

All the above measures given by our Sages with regard to a forbidden substance being mixed with a permitted substance of the same type apply when the forbidden substance is not a leavening agent, a spice, or an important entity that is discrete and is not mixed together with or blended with the permitted substance.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it does not become mixed with the forbidden substances into a single blend, nevertheless, it cannot be distinguished from the kosher substances.</i> If, however, [the forbidden substance] is a leavening agent, a spice, or an important entity, even the slightest amount of it causes [the entire mixture] to be forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The presence of the forbidden substance is never nullified no matter how great the ratio between it and the permitted substances. Needless to say, this stringency was instituted by Rabbinic degree. As mentioned above, according to Scriptural Law, a simple majority is sufficient to nullify the presence of an entity.</i>
What is implied? When yeast from wheat that is <i>terumah</i> falls into a dough of ordinary wheat [flour] and it is of sufficient quantity<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, there is not enough to cause the dough to leaven, it is not considered a leavening agent and its presence can be nullified [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Orlah</i> 2:6-7)].</i> to cause the dough to leaven, the entire dough is considered as having been mixed with <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">And must be sold to a priest at the price of <i>terumah</i>.</i> Similarly, if spices that are <i>terumah</i> fall into a pot of ordinary food [containing] the same substance,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, they do not contain the same substance, according to the Rambam (<i>loc. cit.</i>), we see if their flavor can be detected or not. If it cannot be detected, the mixture is permitted.</i> when [the forbidden spices] are of sufficient quantity to season [the dish], the entire [dish] is considered as having been mixed with <i>terumah</i>. This applies even if the ration between the yeast and the spices [to the permitted substances] is 1:1000.<br>Similarly, if yeast from mixed species grown in a vineyard fall into a dough or spices of <i>orlah</i> fall into a pot, it is forbidden to benefit from the entire [mixture].
Even the smallest amount of an important entity [that is forbidden] can cause a mixture of its own type to become forbidden. The seven entities that follow are considered as important: nuts from Perach,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Perach and Baden are names of places. These and the following terms are defined in the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Orlah</i> 3:8).</i> pomegranates from Baden, sealed barrels,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 134:2) states that this applies only when the barrels are large and therefore important. A small barrel is not important and its presence can be nullified by a simple majority.</i> beet shoots, cabbage heads, Greek squash, and loaves baked by a private person.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to those baked by a baker.</i>
What is implied? If one pomegranate from Baden that was <i>orlah</i> became mixed with several thousand other pomegranates, it is forbidden to benefit from the entire mixture.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., all the pomegranates are considered as if they are the pomegranate that is <i>orlah</i>.<br>The Radbaz mentions opinions that state that one may throw away the value of the forbidden pomegranate, but then benefit from the entire mixture. He, however, brings support for the Rambam's position.<br>The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 101:1) appears to support the Rambam's ruling. Note, however, the comments of the <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 110:2 who writes that even though a Jew may not benefit from any one of the forbidden pomegranates himself, he may sell the entire mixture to a gentile, minus the price of the forbidden pomegranate. See also the notes to Halachah 7.</i> Similarly, if a sealed barrel of wine that is<i> orlah</i> or that is a product of mixed species from a vineyard that became mixed with several thousand sealed barrels, it is forbidden to benefit from the entire quantity.
Similarly, when a piece of meat from a <i>nevelah</i> or from a non-kosher species of animal, beast, fowl, or fish become mixed with several thousand other pieces of meat, the entire mixture is forbidden until one separates that piece of meat and makes certain that there is sixty times its measure.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to an instance when the forbidden piece of meat was cooked with the other pieces.</i> For if one does not separate [the forbidden piece of meat], it will continue to be present and it will not have changed.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">If its form changes, different rules apply, as apparent from Halachah 11.</i> And this piece of meat is important to him, for he receives honor [by serving it] to guests.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 109:1) state that if a piece of meat is not of the type that will bring a person respect by serving it to guests, the stringency mentioned in this halachah does not apply.<br><i>Tosafot</i> (<i>Chulin</i> 100a) explains that even though in practice, the piece of meat will not bring honor to the person - because since it is forbidden, he cannot serve it - it is placed in this category, for were it not to be forbidden, it would bring him honor.</i>
The same laws apply with regard to a piece of meat [cooked] with milk<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">There is an important insight associated with this ruling. The stringency relating to a piece of meat from which one receives honor applies only when the meat is inherently forbidden. When, however, a piece of kosher meat falls into a stew of non-kosher meat, it is not considered a piece of meat from which one receives honor, for it is not inherently forbidden. What is forbidden is the flavor of the non-kosher meat and that flavor is not a substance from which one receives honor.<br>This does not apply with regard to milk and meat. Although it is absorbing the milk that causes the meat to be considered forbidden, once it absorbs that milk, it becomes inherently forbidden. For both of the substances are themselves permitted, it is their mixture that is forbidden by the Torah (Radbaz).</i> or an ordinary animal that was slaughtered in the Temple courtyard, for it is forbidden to benefit from [the latter] according to Rabbinic decree,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., this stringency is applied even though we are speaking only about a Rabbinic prohibition (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> as will be explained in <i>Hilchot Shechitah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 2, Halachah 3.</i> Even the slightest amount of them causes [a mixture] to become forbidden until they are removed.<br>Similarly, when a <i>gid hanesheh</i> was cooked with other similar tissue or with meat, when it can be recognized, it should be removed and the remainder is permitted. For <i>giddim</i> do not impart flavor.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Lechem Mishneh</i> states that this is speaking about a situation when the <i>gid hanesheh</i> was cooked without its fat. Otherwise, 60 times its volume is required, for the fat does impart flavor.</i> If one cannot recognize it, the entire mixture is forbidden. For [the <i>gid hanesheh</i> is considered as a created being in its own right.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 100:1) gives the following criteria for a creation in its own right: It must be alive, in contrast to a kernel of wheat. It must be inherently forbidden, in contrast to a kosher fowl that was slaughtered improperly. It must be a complete entity to the extent that were it to be divided it would no longer be referred to with that name in contrast to non-kosher fat. And it must actually be whole, in contrast to a <i>gid hanesheh</i> that was cut in half.</i> Hence, it is significant; no matter how small it is, it causes [a mixture] to become forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">This principle applies with regard to all entities that are creations in their own right. Until they are removed, the mixture is forbidden regardless of the ratio of kosher to nonkosher substances. Once they are removed, the ratio must be 60:1 (<i>ibid.</i>:2).</i>
Similarly, all living animals are significant and they never become nullified. Therefore, if an ox sentenced to be stoned to death<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">An ox sentenced to execution for goring a human being. See Chapter 4, Halachah 22.</i> becomes intermingled with 1000 oxen, a calf whose neck is to be broken<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">This calf is an atonement offering brought by the elders of a city when there is an unresolved murder. See <i>Hilchot Rotzeach</i> , ch. 9.</i> becomes intermingled with 1000 calves, a dove selected for a <i>metzora</i><sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tzara'at</i> refers to a unique affliction of the skin resembling leprosy that afflicted a person because of he spoke <i>lashon hora</i>, unfavorable gossip. When the physical signs of his affliction have disappeared, the person must bring two doves as sacrifices. One is slaughtered and one is sent away, as stated in <i>Hilchot Tumat Tzara'at</i> 11:1. It is forbidden to benefit from the one that is slaughtered.</i> becomes intermingled with 1000 doves, or a firstborn donkey<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">A firstborn donkey must either be either exchanged for a lamb and the lamb given to a priest or the donkey's neck must be broken (Exodus 13:13; <i>Hilchot Bikkurim</i>, ch. 12).</i> becomes intermingled with 1000 donkeys, it is forbidden to benefit from any of them.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam's ruling, stating that all that is necessary is to destroy the benefit one would receive from one of the forbidden entities. The benefit from the remainder is permitted. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> points to Halachah 29 as indication that the Rambam would also accept the ruling stated by the Ra'avad. The <i>Migdal Oz</i> differs and maintains that the Rambam would not accept that ruling. As mentioned above, the <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 101:2 defends the position of the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, stating that the Rambam would allow one to sell the entire mixture to a gentile, minus the price of the forbidden article.</i> With regard to other entities, even though it is customary to [sell] them by number,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur</i> and the Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 110:1) cite the view of other <i>Rishonim </i>who state that whenever an article is always sold by number, not by a package, its presence is never nullified.</i> they can be nullified according to the ordinary measures.
What is implied?<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., what is an example of an article that is sold by number being nullified.</i> When a bundle of vegetables that come from mixed species grown in a vineyard are mixed with 200 bundles or an <i>esrog</i> which is <i>orlah</i> is mixed with 200 <i>esrogim</i>, the entire quantity is permitted. Similar laws apply in all analogous situations.
It appears to me that every article that is significant to the inhabitants of a given place as nuts from Perach and pomegranates from Baden were significant in <i>Eretz Yisrael</i> in [the Talmudic] era causes a mixture to be forbidden if even the slightest amount becomes mixed in because of its importance in that time and in that era. The particular entities [referred to above] were mentioned because the slightest amount of them causes a mixture to be forbidden in every place. The same laws apply to articles similar to them in other places. It is clear that all of these prohibitions stem from Rabbinic decree.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">For according to Scriptural Law, a substance mixed with substances of the same type are forbidden when there is a majority of the permitted substance. When mixed with substances of a different type, they are nullified when the taste can no longer be detected.</i>
If one pomegranate from a mixture [of pomegranates including a forbidden pomegranate from Baden] falls into two other [permitted] pomegranates from Baden and then one of these three pomegranates fell into other pomegranates, the latter mixture is permitted. [The rationale is that the presence of] the pomegranate from the first mixture [which fell into the second mixture] is nullified because of the majority of permitted substances.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., according to Scriptural Law; according to Rabbinic Law, both the first and the second mixtures are forbidden.</i> If, however, [a pomegranate] from the first mixture falls into 1000 pomegranates, they are all forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">For with regard to each of the pomegranates in the second mixture, there is a question if it is forbidden by Scriptural Law or not (Radbaz).<br>The Radbaz notes that the Rambam's ruling here appears to contradict his ruling in <i>Hilchot Avodat Kochavim</i> 7:10 where the Rambam rules that if a goblet used for idol worship becomes mixed with other goblets and then one from the first mixture falls into a second mixture, one may use the goblets of the second mixture. The Radbaz maintains that with this ruling, the Rambam changed his mind and adopted a more stringent position.<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that the Rambam rules more stringently in this instance than with regard to idol worship because the prohibition against idol worship is universally known. The prohibition against benefiting from a significant entity, by contrast, is less recognized. Therefore there is need for greater stringency. Alternatively, here the Rambam is speaking about partaking of the forbidden mixture, while in <i>Hilchot Avodat Kochavim</i>, he is speaking about benefiting from the mixture. Obviously, there is greater reason to prohibited a substance from which one partakes.<br>The position followed by the Rambam in <i>Hilchot Avodat Kochavim</i> is followed by other <i>Rishonim</i> even with regard to a significant entity. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 110:8) quotes the Rambam's view and the Rama adds further stringencies. The <i>Turei Zahav</i> 110:10 and the <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 110:3 mention the more lenient views.</i> [The concept that the presence of the forbidden pomegranate] was nullified because of the majority of permitted substances only when there is a multiple doubt involved:<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Perhaps the pomegranate that fell from the first mixture into the second mixture was not forbidden by Scriptural Law. Even if it was forbidden, perhaps the pomegranate that fell from the second mixture was not forbidden by Scriptural Law. Thus there is a multiple doubt if an entity forbidden by Scriptural Law is present.</i> i.e., that if one of the second mixture will fall into another place, it does not cause [that third mixture] to become forbidden. Similar laws apply in all analogous situations.
If the nuts that were forbidden because of the nut that was <i>orlah </i>intermingled with them were cracked open, the pomegranates were taken apart, the barrels were opened, the squash was cut, or the bread was sliced after they became forbidden, [the presence of the forbidden entity] can be nullified if there is 201 times its volume.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">As required with regard to the prohibitions of <i>orlah</i> and mixed species in a vineyard. I.e., the stringency of a significant article no longer applies, because the entities are no longer whole and in their present form, they are not significant.</i> This law also applies with regard to a piece of forbidden meat<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Which would be forbidden because one derives honor from serving it as stated in Halachah 5. Once it has been minced, the meat is no longer a piece from which one would derive honor [<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i>101:6)].</i> that is minced together with other pieces and they are all [minced] in the same way, [the presence of the forbidden entity] can be nullified if there is 60 times its volume.
It is, however, forbidden to crack the nuts, take apart the pomegranates, open the barrels after they have become forbidden so that [the presence of the forbidden entity] can be nullified if there is 201 times its volume. For, as an initial and preferred measure, we do not nullify the presence of an entity.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries question the Rambam's statement, noting that in Chapter 15, Halachah 26, the Rambam states that we may nullify the presence of a substance forbidden by Rabbinic Law. Since the prohibition against these mixtures is Rabbinic in origin, seemingly, it would be possible to nullify their presence.</i> If one does so, we penalize him and forbid [the entity] to him, as explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 15, Halachah 25.</i>
[The following rule applies when] yeast that comes from mixed species in a vineyard and from<i> terumah</i> falls into dough and there is not enough of either of [the forbidden substances] alone to cause the dough to rise, but when the two are combined, there is enough to cause the dough to rise. This dough is forbidden to an Israelite, but permitted to the priests.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in Halachah 1, if an entity is sufficient to cause a dough to leaven or to spice a pot, its presence can never be nullified. Although neither forbidden entity on its own is large enough to bring about this change, when the two are combined, this result is achieved. Therefore an Israelite is forbidden to partake of the dough or the pot. With regard to a priest, by contrast, since <i>terumah </i>is not forbidden to him, we do not say that an article forbidden to him brought about this change. For the mixed species alone is not of sufficient size. Hence, he is permitted to partake of the bread or the cooked food.</i><br>Similarly, when spices that come from <i>terumah</i> and from mixed species in a vineyard fall into a pot and there is not enough of either of [the forbidden substances] to spice the pot, but together there is enough of both of them to spice the pot, that pot is forbidden to an Israelite - for an entity forbidden to him spice it - and permitted to the priests.
When there are two or three types of the same species of spice or three species of the same type, they can be combined to cause a pot to be forbidden when they spice it or when [a similar type mixture] causes dough to leaven.<br>What is implied? Yeast from wheat and yeast from barley are not considered as being two separate substances. Instead, since the category yeast is the same, they are considered as one substance and they can be combined to measure to see if they are sufficient to cause a dough of wheat to leaven if their combined flavor is that of wheat<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">If the combined flavor is not that of wheat, the dough does not become forbidden, because the yeast is considered as giving a different flavor to the dough. Hence the dough is forbidden only of that flavor is detectable.<br>The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam's ruling, offering a different interpretation of <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 65a, the Rambam's source. Significantly, their disagreement is mirrored by similar difference in interpretation by Rashi and <i>Tosafot</i>. Rashi and the Rambam follow one approach and <i>Tosafot</i> and the Ra'avad, the other.</i> or to cause a dough of barley to leaven if their combined flavor is that of barley.
What is meant by three species of the same type? For example, river parsley, parsley that grows in meadows, and parsley that grows in gardens. Although each of them has a distinct name, since they are of one type, they can be combined to [cause a dish to be forbidden if they] spice [it].
[The following rules apply when] yeast that is <i>terumah</i> or from mixed species from a vineyard falls into dough that is already leavened or spices that are <i>terumah</i> or from mixed species from a vineyard fall into a pot that has already been spiced. If there is enough [of the forbidden] yeast to cause the dough to leaven if it had been unleavened or there is enough of the spices to spice the pot had it been unspiced, the entire mixture is forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Although in actual fact the forbidden yeast or spices did not have an effect, because the dough leavened and the pot was spiced without them. Nevertheless, since they could have had an effect, they cause the dish to become forbidden.<br><i>Avodah Zarah</i> 68a notes that when a dough is already leavened, adding yeast will spoil its flavor. Hence, seemingly, it should not be forbidden. Nevertheless, an exception is made with regard to dough, for when extra yeast is added to dough, that dough is then used to cause other doughs to leaven. Hence, it is not considered to be spoiled.<br>The Ra'avad understands the emphasis of the passage from <i>Avodah Zarah</i> differently and objects to the Rambam's ruling. He note that <i>Avodah Zarah</i> does not mention spices; the Rambam added those on the basis of his logic. And the Ra'avad, argues, that logic can be disputed. For the addition of yeast to the dough has an effect as explained. The addition of the spices, by contrast, have no effect - for the pot was already spiced. Why then do they cause the pot to be forbidden?<br>The <i>Lechem Mishneh</i> answers, that even according to the Ra'avad's understanding, the Rambam's logic can be defended, for the food from the heavily spiced pot could be used to spice other pots.</i> If they are of sufficient size to spice [the pot] or cause [the dough] to leaven, their presence can be nullified according to the required measure: <i>terumah</i> when [the mixture] is 101 times [the size of the forbidden substance] and mixed species in a vineyard when [the mixture] is 201 times [the size of the forbidden substance].
<i>Terumah</i> can [help] cause <i>orlah</i> and mixed species from a vineyard to be nullified.<br>What is implied? When a <i>se'ah</i> of <i>terumah</i> falls into 99 [<i>se'ah</i> of] ordinary produce and afterwards, a half <i>se'ah</i> of<i> orlah</i> or mixed species from a vineyard falls into the entire mixture, the prohibition against <i>orlah</i> or mixed species in a vineyard does not apply.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The mixture is, however, forbidden to Israelites and permitted only to priests, for the presence of the <i>terumah</i> is not nullified.</i> For it is nullified because of the presence of 201 times [the size of the forbidden substance] even though a portion of the 201 is <i>terumah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following halachah represent the Rambam's interpretation of <i>Orlah</i> 2:2 which is based on the Jerusalem Talmud.</i>
Similarly, <i>orlah</i> and mixed species from a vineyard can [help] cause <i>terumah</i> to be nullified.<br>What is implied? When 100 <i>se'ah</i> of <i>orlah</i> or mixed species from a vineyard fall into 20,000 <i>se'ah</i> of ordinary produce, the entire mixture is thus 20,100 <i>se'ah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the presence of the <i>orlah</i> or the mixed species from the vineyard are nullified.</i> Afterwards, a <i>se'ah</i> of <i>terumah </i>fell into every 100 <i>se'ah</i>, the entire [mixture] is permitted and the presence of the <i>terumah</i> is nullified because of the presence of 101 times [the original amount of <i>terumah</i>].<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">This example is the product of the Rambam's own deduction. Although the simple interpretation of <i>Orlah, loc. cit.</i>, would imply that the concept stated in this halachah could also be derived from the situation described in the previous halachah, it does not work out mathematically. Hence, the Rambam had to find a new example.</i> [This applies] even though part of the 100 that nullify its presence are <i>orlah</i> or mixed species from a vineyard.
Similarly, <i>orlah</i> may [help] nullify mixed species from a vineyard and mixed species from a vineyard may [help] nullify <i>orlah</i>. Mixed species from a vineyard may [help] nullify [the presence of other] mixed species from a vineyard and <i>orlah</i> may [help] nullify [the presence of other] <i>orlah</i>.<br>What is implied? 200 <i>se'ah</i> of <i>orlah</i> or mixed species from a vineyard fall into 40,000 <i>se'ah</i> of ordinary produce.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibition is thus nullified because the ratio of permitted to forbidden substances is 200:1.<br>The Ra'avad criticizes the Rambam, questioning why he uses extremely large numbers. The Radbaz explains that the Rambam's figures enable all the calculations to be made without fragments.</i> Afterwards,<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., after it was discovered that the forbidden substance had fallen into the permitted substances. See the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Terumot</i> 5:8).</i> a <i>se'ah</i> of <i>orlah</i> or mixed species from a vineyard fell into each of the 200 <i>se'ah</i> of <i>orlah</i> or mixed species from a vineyard, the entire mixture is permitted. Since the presence of the forbidden substance that fell into [the mixture] originally was nullified, the entire [mixture] is considered as ordinary produce that is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Thus despite the fact that it contains produce that was originally forbidden, the entire quantity may be used to nullify the presence of the second measure of forbidden produce that falls in.<br>The rationale for this leniency is that according to Scriptural Law, the entire measure is permitted when there is a simple majority of forbidden substances.</i>
A garment that was dyed with shells of <i>orlah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Orlah</i> 1:8), the Rambam quotes the <i>Sifra (Parshat Kedoshim)</i> which teaches that since it is forbidden to benefit from <i>Orlah</i>, it is also forbidden to use it as a dye. The Rambam emphasizes that, accordingly, this applies, not only to fruit which is <i>orlah</i>, but also to the shells from which dye is made. In his notes to the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah, Rav Kapach cites a responsa from the Rambam from which it appears that this applies only to shells because they serve a fruit. It is not forbidden to make dye from the wood or the bark of a tree that is <i>orlah</i>. See Halachah 24 and notes.</i> should be burnt.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Since the substance from which the dye comes is forbidden, the entire article becomes forbidden.</i> If it became intermingled with others, [its presence] may be nullified when there are 201 times the original amount.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's wording - and hence, our translation - implies that one may cause the presence of the forbidden garment to be nullified by adding 200 other garments to it. The Radbaz explains that the mixture is forbidden only according to Rabbinic Law. As stated in Chapter 15, Halachah 26, as an initial and preferred option, one may add a sufficient quantity of permitted substances to nullify the presence of a substance forbidden by Rabbinic Law. Note, however, the glosses of the <i>Tosafot Yom Tov</i> and Rav Kapach to <i>Orlah</i> 3:1, that do not accept this interpretation and state that one may not nullify the prohibition as an initial and preferred option.</i> Similarly, when a dish was cooked or a loaf of bread baked with the shells of <i>orlah</i> or mixed species from a vineyard, the dish or the bread must be burnt, for the benefit [from the forbidden substance] is evident.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 22.</i> If it became intermingled with others, [its presence] may be nullified when there are 201 times the original amount.
Similarly, when <i>milo hasit</i><sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">In his notes to the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Orlah</i> 3:2), Rav Kapach elaborates on the definition of this term, concluding that it is equal to two thumbbreadths. This is also reflected in the Rambam's ruling, <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 9:18.</i> of a garment was dyed with [a dye that is] <i>orlah</i>, and [that garment] cannot be identified, [its presence] may be nullified when there are 201 times the original amount.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if the entire garment is not 201 times the size of the portion dyed with the forbidden dye. In his Commentary to the Mishnah, the Rambam explains that this law teaches us that if even a small portion of a garment is dyed with forbidden dye, the entire garment may become forbidden.</i> If powdered dye that is <i>orlah</i> becomes mixed with powdered dye that is permitted, [its presence] may be nullified when there are 201 times the original amount. When liquid dye that is <i>orlah</i> becomes mixed with liquid dye that is permitted, its presence is nullified when there is a majority [of the permitted substance].<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz explains that a more lenient ruling is issued in this instance, because:<br>a) Here there is no substance that is forbidden, it is only the color that comes from the forbidden dye that is problematic. A differentiation can be made between this instance and the previous laws, for in those instances, the forbidden dye has already become permanently associated with a substance.<br>b) In this instance, the majority of the dyeing will result from the permitted dye. The effect of the prohibited dye is secondary.</i>
When an oven has been heated with shells of <i>orlah</i> or mixed species from a vineyard, it must be cooled off [before cooking in it]. [This applies] to both a new and an old [oven]. Afterwards, one should heat [the oven] with permitted wood.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">If so, it is then permitted to use even a new oven. Heating a new oven with the shells of <i>orlah </i>completes the task of fashioning the oven. Thus there is reason to say that since it was completed in a forbidden manner, the oven itself would be forbidden. Nevertheless, when permitted fuel is used even for such an oven, the products are permitted. The rationale is that they are produced by two substances: the oven which is forbidden and the fuel which is permitted. Whenever there are two factors involved, one permitted and one forbidden, the result is permitted to be used (<i>Pesachim</i> 26b). We do not require the oven to be destroyed, for the oven is not inherently forbidden (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).<br>One might ask: If so, why is an oven that is heated with shells that are <i>orlah</i> forbidden to be used. Here also there are two factors involved, the oven which is permitted and the fuel which is forbidden. It is possible to explain that the leniency of allowing to use the yield produced by a forbidden and a permitted substance only after the fact. In this instance, cooling the oven provides an easy alternative (Radbaz).</i> If one cooked in it before it was cooled, whether bread or food, it is forbidden to benefit from it. [The rationale is that] the forbidden wood increased the value of the bread or the food.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">One might ask, since the oven is permitted, even though the fuel is forbidden, there is both a permitted and a forbidden factor producing this result. Why, then, is the food forbidden? It is possible to explain that since the fire which is forbidden is evident and apparent, we rule stringently (Radbaz, <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i><br>If one removed the entire fire<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">As indicated by the Halachah 24, this applies even if there are still glowing coals in the oven.</i> and then cooked or baked with the heat of the oven [that remained], it is permitted, for the forbidden wood is no longer present.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">One might ask: In the case of a new oven, since both factors - the oven and the fuel - are forbidden. Nevertheless, it appears that according to the Rambam, removing the fire is sufficient for the food to be permitted. For the oven itself is not inherently forbidden, it was only completed in a forbidden manner (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i>
It is forbidden to benefit from plates, cups, pots, and bottles that were fired by a potter with shells of <i>orlah</i>. [The rationale is] they are made new by an object from which it is forbidden to benefit.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Since the fuel used to fire the kitchenware is the primary element in completing them, they are forbidden. The <i>Turei Zahav</i> 142:7 explains that here we are speaking about kitchenware on which food is served cold. Since the kitchenware was made in a forbidden manner, it is forbidden to benefit from it. If, however, a pot was fired with forbidden fuel and then used to cook kosher food, that food would be permitted as is the law concerning a new oven. The Meiri (in his gloss to <i>Pesachim</i> 26b), however, explains that if one cooks food with a pot forbidden because of these factors, the food is forbidden.</i>
When bread was baked on coals from wood<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz emphasizes that here, too, we are speaking about shells from fruit that is <i>orlah</i>. The wood of a tree never becomes forbidden as <i>orlah</i>.</i> that is <i>orlah, </i>it is permitted. Once [the wood] becomes coals, the forbidden dimension is no longer present, even though they are still glowing.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">For once the wood is consumed by fire, it is no longer considered forbidden. See <i>Hilchot Pesulei HaMukdashim</i> 19:13 which states that it is permitted to benefit from the ashes of substances that are forbidden and required to be burnt.</i><br>When a pot was cooked with shells from <i>orlah</i> or mixed species from a vineyard together with permitted wood,<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">As evident from the continuation of the Rambam's statements, this applies when the permitted fuel was added after the forbidden fuel. Implied is that were the two fuels mixed together at the outset, the dish would be permitted.</i> the food [cooked in it] is forbidden, even though [it was cooked by two factors, one forbidden and one permitted]. [The rationale is] that at the time it was cooked with the forbidden wood, the permitted wood had not been introduced. Thus part of the cooking process was performed with permitted wood and part with forbidden wood.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> gives two interpretations for this halachah. Our translation follows the first interpretation. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, however, questions that interpretation, stating that seemingly, the fact that the pot in which the food was cooked was permitted would add another permitted factor and thus the food was never cooked in a totally forbidden setting. He therefore offers another interpretation, stating that here the Rambam is speaking about firing the pot in which the food was cooked. First it was fired with forbidden fuel, then it was fired with permitted fuel, and then food was cooked in it with permitted fuel. Since it was originally fired with forbidden fuel, it becomes forbidden and any food cooked in it is likewise prohibited. The <i>Turei Zahav</i> 142:9 favors the first interpretation, explaining that the situation resembles food cooked in an oven with forbidden fuel.</i>
When a plant that is <i>orlah</i> becomes mixed together with other plants or a row of mixed species from a vineyard became mixed with other rows,<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it was known that one plant or row was forbidden, but one was not able to identify the forbidden plant. It is somewhat difficult to conceive how a row of crops could not be recognized as mixed species growing in a vineyard.</i> at the outset, one should gather all [the produce].<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Significantly, in his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Orlah</i> 1:6), the Rambam rules that at the outset, it is forbidden to gather this produce. The leniency stated in this halachah applies only after the fact. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> states that the Rambam changed his mind, because the more lenient opinion is mentioned in the Talmud (<i>Gittin</i> 54b).</i> If [the ratio of] permitted plants to forbidden plants was 200:1 or the ratio of forbidden rows to permitted ones is 200:1, everything that was gathered is permitted. If the ratio was less than this, all that was gathered is forbidden.<br>[One might ask:] Why is one permitted to gather [the produce] at the outset? Seemingly, the law should require that everything be forbidden for him until he undertakes the difficulty of removing the forbidden plant or row.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., perhaps we should ordain a decree, forbidding benefit from the entire field, lest one intentionally mix a forbidden <i>orlah</i> plant into his vineyard.</i> [It can be explained that that] a person will not cause his vineyard to be forbidden because of one plant.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Hence we do not fear that he will introduce a forbidden plant into the vineyard.</i> Were he to be able to identify it, he would remove it.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., we do not fear that he left the <i>orlah</i> plant intentionally.<br>In <i>Hilchot Terumot</i> 13:12, the Rambam rules more stringently with regard to<i> terumah</i>. A distinction between the two instances can be made, for <i>terumah</i> may be eaten by priests, while <i>orlah</i> is forbidden to everyone.</i>
It is forbidden to benefit from cheese that is made to harden using the syrup of <i>orlah</i> fruit that has not ripened,<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Orlah</i> 1:7), the Rambam states that it is common to use the white syrup that drips from underdeveloped figs as a catalyst to cause cheese to harden.</i> the stomach<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., using the renin as a catalyst.</i> of an animal offered as a sacrifice to false divinities, or vinegar made from the wine of a false divinity. Although the forbidden entity is being mixed with a substance of another type and a very small amount is used, [the cheese] is forbidden for [the effect of] the forbidden entity is obvious, for it [caused the milk] to harden into cheese.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 3, Halachah 13. In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 2:5), the Rambam writes that the laws applying to a catalyst used to make cheese are more severe than those applying to spices and yeast (Halachah 1, for the latter can be nullified when they fall into a substance of another type) for the reason explained above. As the Radbaz explains, even without yeast a dough would be able to be baked and a dish could be served without spices, but without a catalyst, milk would never harden into cheese.</i>
The law is that fruit that is <i>orlah</i> or from mixed species from a vineyard should be burnt.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kiddushin</i> 56b derives this from the exegesis of Deuteronomy 22:9, <i>pen tikadeish</i>, "lest it become hallowed," interpreting it as <i>pen tukad eish</i>, "lest it be consigned to fire."</i> Liquids from [that fruit] should be buried, because it is impossible to burn liquids.
When wine that was poured as a libation to idols is mixed with [other] wine, it is forbidden to benefit from the entire mixture regardless of how small [the amount of forbidden wine], as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 15, Halachot 6-7.</i><br>When does the above apply? When the permitted wine is poured onto a drop of wine that had been poured as a libation.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> quotes <i>Rishonim</i> who rule that if a quantity of permitted wine that is 60 times the volume of the forbidden wine falls into the forbidden wine at the same time, the presence of the forbidden wine is nullified. The stringency mentioned by the Rambam applies only when the kosher wine is poured into the forbidden wine little by little. The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> rules that if a severe loss is involved, one may rely on this leniency.</i> If, however, one poured wine that had been poured as a libation from a small bottle<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a bottle from which the wine is poured one drop at a time.</i> into a cistern of wine, its presence is nullified. Even if one poured the entire day, each individual drop becomes nullified, drop after drop.<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz states that this applies even if the majority of the mixture comes from the forbidden wine. Since each drop was nullified, the entire quantity is permitted. The <i>Siftei Cohen</i> 134:4 differs and requires that the permitted wine be 60 times the volume of the forbidden wine.<br>According to the Rambam, it is even permitted to drink the wine of the mixture. Rashi (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 73a) rules that it is permitted to benefit from the wine, drinking it, however, is forbidden. And the Ra'avad rules that it is forbidden even to benefit from it. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 134:1) quotes the Rambam's ruling.</i><br>If one pours from a jug,<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a container with a large opening.</i> the entire quantity is forbidden. [This applies] whether one pours permitted wine into forbidden wine or forbidden wine into permitted wine. [This stringency is enforced,] because the column of wine which descends from the large jug [creates a connection].
When even the smallest amount of ordinary [gentile] wine is mixed with [Jewish] wine, it is forbidden to drink [the entire mixture].<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 134:2) rules leniently, stating that in the present era, since it is no longer customary to pour wine as libations to false divinities, one may be lenient and permit the mixture if there is 60 times more kosher wine than forbidden wine, provided the kosher wine is not poured into the forbidden wine in one column.</i> Instead, it should be sold to a gentile in its entirety. The money [paid] for the forbidden wine should be cast into the Dead Sea.<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., cast into a place where one will not benefit from it.</i> One may, however, benefit from the remainder of the money.<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">One may, however, destroy one jug and then benefit from the others - e.g., to use the wine as a dye - for it is possible that one will be benefiting directly from the forbidden wine. The advice suggested by the Rambam, by contrast, allows the Jew to benefit from the remainder of the wine without any possibility of benefiting directly from the forbidden wine.<br>The advice suggested applies only to jugs, for each jug is a separate entity. It does not apply when wine becomes mixed with wine, as indicated by the previous halachah (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 74a).</i><br>Similarly, if a jug of wine poured as a libation had become intermingled with jugs of [kosher] wine, it is forbidden to drink the entire mixture.<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">See the Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 134:2) who explains that this applies only when the jugs are large and therefore important as mentioned in Halachah 3. Otherwise, the presence of the forbidden jug can be nullified by a simple majority.</i> One may, however, benefit from it, selling the entire mixture to a gentile and casting the money for the [forbidden] jug into the Dead Sea. The same applies with regard to a jug of ordinary [gentile] wine.<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Seemingly, this ruling is obvious. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> states that it was added to emphasize the stringency that one must destroy the value of the forbidden jug.</i>
When water is mixed into wine or wine is mixed into water, [the forbidden entity causes the mixture to be prohibited] if its flavor can be detected, because they are two different types of substances.<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Chapter 15, Halachah 6.</i><br>When does the above apply? When the permitted liquid falls into the forbidden liquid. If, however, the forbidden liquid fell into the permitted liquid, the presence of it is nullified, drop after drop, provided it fell from a from a small bottle.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 28.<br>Rav Moshe HaCohen questions how the presence of the forbidden entity can be nullified, since its flavor can be detected. Indeed, when quoting this law, <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 134:3) states this leniency applies only when the taste of the forbidden substance cannot be detected.</i><br>How is it possible for water to be forbidden? If it was worshipped or if it was offered to a false divinity.
[The following law applies when] a pitcher of water fell into a cistern of wine and afterwards,<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Avodah Zarah</i> 73a emphasizes that the leniency mentioned in this halachah applies only when the water falls into the wine before the forbidden wine. If the forbidden wine falls in first, the permitted wine becomes prohibited.</i> wine that was poured as a libation fell into it. [Initially,] we consider the permitted wine as if it did not exist,<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">And thus it is not automatically forbidden.</i> We measure the water in relation to the wine poured as a libation. If it<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the water alone. The permitted wine is not considered, for any amount of the forbidden wine mixed into it would cause the permitted wine to be prohibited.</i> is [of sufficient volume] to nullify the taste of the wine poured as a libation, the water is more abundant than it and it nullifies [the forbidden wine] and the entire [mixture] is permitted.
When wine poured as a libation falls on grapes, one should wash them. They are permitted to be eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">For the forbidden wine will not have entered them.</i> If the grapes have split open,<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">And thus the forbidden wine could enter.</i> when the wine imparts its flavor to them, it is forbidden to benefit from them.<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">They may, however, be sold to a gentile, minus the increase in their value produced by the forbidden wine [<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 134:8)].</i>If not, it is permitted to partake of them. [This applies] whether the wine is aged or fresh.<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Avodah Zarah</i> 66a emphasizes that even if the fresh wine has the same flavor as the grapes, the grapes are not forbidden. For the wine is considered as a different type of substance.</i>
When [forbidden wine] falls on figs, they are permitted, because wine impairs the flavor of figs.<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Hence even if it imparts its flavor, the figs are not forbidden. Although the version of <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 5:2 which the Rambam relies on differs from the standard published text of the Mishnah, the Rambam's ruling is accepted as halachah by <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i>134:9).</i>
When wine poured as a libation falls on wheat, [the wheat] is forbidden to be eaten, but it is permitted to benefit from it. One should not sell it to a gentile, lest he sell it again to a Jew. What should be done instead? He should grind [the wheat] into flour, make it into bread, and sell it to a gentile outside the presence of a Jew.<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">This is permitted, for the gentile is paying for the bread alone. He is not paying for the wine at all.</i> [In this way,] a Jew will not repurchase it from the gentile,<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, the Jew selling a fellow Jew selling bread to the gentile, he might purchase it from him.</i> for the bread of a gentile is forbidden, as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 17, Halachah 9. A Jew will have no way of knowing that this bread was not baked by the gentile. Hence he will refrain from purchasing it from him. In a place where it is customary to purchase bread from gentiles, there is no way of benefiting from the wheat [<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 134:11)].</i><br>Why do we not check the wheat to see if [the wine imparted] its flavor? Because [the wheat] draws out the wine<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">As <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 65b states, the kernels of wheat are cracked and this causes them to absorb the wine (<i>Lechem Mishneh</i>; <i>Shulchan Aruch, loc. cit.</i>).</i> and it becomes absorbed within it.<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz emphasizes that the taste of the wine becomes blended in with the taste of the wheat to the extent that it cannot be detected.</i>
When wine poured as a libation becomes vinegar<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">The vinegar is forbidden, as the wine was.</i> and falls into vinegar that comes from beer, even the slightest amount causes it to become forbidden. [The rationale is that] it is considered to have become mixed with the same type of substance, because they are both vinegar.<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The fact that originally one was wine and one was beer is not significant.</i><br>When wine becomes mixed with vinegar, we see if [the forbidden entity] imparts its flavor.<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">If it does, the mixture is forbidden. If not, it is permitted. Even if the vinegar originally came from wine, it is considered as a different substance.</i> [This applies] whether [forbidden] vinegar falls into wine<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">It is not automatically permitted, because vinegar impairs the flavor of wine, for there are some who prefer vinegar to wine (Rashba, as quoted by <i>Turei Zahav</i> 134:8).</i> or [forbidden] wine falls into vinegar.

Chapter 17

When the meat of a <i>nevelah</i> or a crawling animal or teeming animal was cooked in an earthenware pot,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">If the pot was made out of metal, it is possible to purge the flavor of the non-kosher food the pot absorbed through <i>hagaalah</i>. This process is not effective with regard to an earthenware pot.</i> one should not cook the meat of a ritually slaughtered animal in that pot on that same day. If he cooked a type of meat [in the pot that day], the dish is forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Since the dish contains meat and the flavor of the forbidden meat was absorbed in the pot, the laws applying to a forbidden substance mixed with its own type apply. Since we do not know how much of the forbidden substance is absorbed in the pot, we assume that the entire pot is forbidden. For this reason, the Rambam does not mention that if there is 60 times the amount of the forbidden food in the kosher food, the kosher food is permitted. For it is very rare that a pot be able to contain sixty times its own volume (Radbaz).</i> If he cooked another substance in it, [it is forbidden if] its flavor can be detected.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Rambam, it should be tasted by a gentile to determine whether the forbidden flavor is detectable or not, as stated in Chapter 15, Halachah 30. As mentioned, the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 98:1) accepts the Rambam's premise, but the Rama states that in the present age, we do not rely on the statements of a non-Jew who tasted food to determine whether it is kosher or not.</i>
The Torah forbade only [the use of] a pot that was [cooked with the forbidden substance] on that day.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning of the Rambam's words is not clear. Rashi (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 75b) interprets the term as meaning "which has not been left overnight." <i>Tosafot</i>, by contrast, states that it means "that has not been left for 24 hours." The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 103:5) follows the latter view.</i> For [in that time,] the flavor of the fat absorbed in the pot had not been impaired.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">After that time, however, the flavor is impaired and thus will not cause a substance cooked in the pot to become forbidden.</i><br>According to Rabbinic Law, one should never cook in it again.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This is a safeguard less cooking in a pot that had not been used for non-kosher food for a day lead to cooking in one that had been used for non-kosher food that day (<i>Avodah Zarah</i>, loc. cit.).</i> For this reason, one should never purchase used earthenware utensils from gentiles to use them for hot foods, e.g., pots and plates. This applies even when they are coated with lead. If one purchased such a utensil and cooked in it from the second day onward, the food is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Our Sages did not enforce their decree after the fact. Nevertheless, at the outset, an earthenware pot that was used for non-kosher food may never be used.</i>
[The following rules apply when] a person purchases metal or glass dinnerware from a gentile. Utensils that [the gentile] did not use at all should be immersed in the waters of a <i>mikveh</i>. Afterwards, it is permitted to eat and drink with them.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 5 regarding the obligation for this immersion.</i><br>Utensils that he used for cold [food and drink], e.g., cups, flasks, and pitchers, he should wash them thoroughly<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Lest any forbidden food be stuck to them.</i> and immerse them. [Afterwards,] they are permitted. Utensils that he used for hot food: large pots, kettles, and pots used to heat food, should be purged through <i>hagaalah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This will purge any forbidden food that was absorbed in them. There should be at least one day between the last time a pot was used for non-kosher food and the time when <i>hagaalah</i> is performed.</i> and immersed in the <i>mikveh</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 121:2 which discusses what must be done if they were immersed in the <i>mikveh</i> before <i>hagaalah</i> was performed.</i> Afterwards, they are permitted. Utensils that he used by exposing them to fire, e.g., spits and grills, should be exposed to fire<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">That the forbidden article and the utensil were in direct contact with fire without a medium of water or any other liquid.</i> until they become white-hot and their outer surface falls off.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Only then will the forbidden flavor that was absorbed be purged.</i> They may then be immersed and become permitted for use.
How is [the purging process of] <i>hagaalah</i> achieved? A small pot is placed into a large pot and they are filled with water until the smaller one is submerged.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">In that way, there will not be any portion of it that is not exposed to the water.</i> Then one must boil it very thoroughly.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., we follow the principle: "As it absorbed a forbidden flavor, so it purges it." Hence boiling it thoroughly will cause any forbidden taste that is absorbed to be purged.</i><br>If a large pot was [forbidden],<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">And thus it would be difficult to submerge it a larger pot.</i> one should place dough or mud along its edge [so that] he could fill it with water so that it will flow over its edge.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">And thus the boiling water will also cover the edge.</i>He [then] boils it.<br>In all instances, if he used them before boiling [water in them for <i>hagaalah</i>], washing them thoroughly, making them white hot, or immersing them, [the food] is kosher. For any fat [absorbed] in them imparts an unpleasant flavor, as explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">In Halachah 2.</i>
The immersion of the dinnerware that is purchased from gentiles to allow it to be used for eating and drinking is not associated with ritual purity and impurity. Instead, it is a Rabbinic decree.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">As the Jerusalem Talmud (<i>Avodah Zarah</i> 5:15) states, this immersion was instituted to mark the article's transition from the impurity of the gentiles.</i><br>There is an allusion<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Most commentaries understand the Rambam as explaining that the requirement for immersion is an <i>asmachta</i>, i.e., an obligation that is essentially Rabbinic in origin. Although our Sages cited a verse that can be seen to allude to it, the intent is not that the obligation is derived from the verse. Instead, the verse is merely a hint which the Rabbis found to allude to their teaching (Rabbenu Nissim).<br>There are, however, others who note that the Rambam occasionally employs the term he employs here - <i>midvrei sofrim</i> - to refer to obligations and laws that are of Scriptural origin. They are not explicitly stated in the verse, but instead derived through the principles of Biblical exegesis. According to this view, the obligation is of Scriptural origin (the Rashba, Vol. III, Responsum 255, 259).</i> to this [in Numbers 31:23 that describes Moses' instructions with regard to the spoils taken from Midian:] "Everything that can be passed through fire, you shall pass through fire and it will become pure." According to the Oral Tradition, we learned that the verse is speaking only about purifying [the utensils] from gentile cooking, not from ritual impurity. For there is no ritually impurity that is dispelled by fire. All those who are impure ascend from their impurity through immersion and the impurity stemming from [contact with] a human corpse is [dispelled] through the sprinkling [of water and the ashes of the red heifer]. There is no concept of fire [employed in this context], rather [it is employed] with regard to purification from gentile cooking. Since the verse states "and it will become pure," our Sages said: "Add to it another dimension of purity after passing it through fire to cause it to be permitted because [of its contact] with gentile cooking."<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., after you have purged it from the taste absorbed because of gentile cooking, add another dimension of purity through immersion.</i>
[Our Sages] obligate this immersion only for metal<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">This requirement also applies to glass dinnerware, as stated in Halachah 3.<br><i>Avodah Zarah</i> 75b explains the association with metal utensils as follows. Our Sages associated this obligation with the purification of the spoil taken in the war against Midian and the verse which mentions those spoils (Numbers 31:22  refers to metal utensils. Glass utensils are also included, because, halachically, they share similarities to metal utensils.</i> dinnerware utensils<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., utensils used to prepare, serve, or partake of food. Even utensils that are used in the preliminary phases of preparation of food, e.g., a knife used to slaughter or skin an animal, are required to be immersed according to certain authorities [<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 120:5)].</i> that were purchased from a gentile. When, however, a person borrows [such utensils] from a gentile or a gentile left him such utensils as security, it is only necessary to wash them thoroughly, boil them, or expose them to fire. He does not have to immerse [them].<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">For even though he has permission to use them, he has not become their owner. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> quotes certain opinions that maintain that utensils taken as security must be immersed, because if the debt is not repaid, they are considered as payment [see <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 120:5).<br>In this context, there are many authorities who question why the utensils that are "purchased" by a gentile before Pesach are not required to be immersed.</i> Similarly, if one purchased wooden or stone utensils, it is only necessary to wash them thoroughly, boil them, or expose them to fire. Similarly, earthenware utensils need not be immersed.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Needless to say, plastic utensils need not be immersed.</i> If, however, they are coated with lead, they are considered as metal utensils and require immersion.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The Rama ((<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 120:1) states that they should be immersed without a blessing.</i>
When a person purchases a knife from a gentile, he must expose it to fire until it become white hot or have it honed in its sharpener.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">By exposing the knife to fire, the person will burn away any non-kosher substances. By honing it, he will grind away its surface and together with it, the taste of the forbidden substance it absorbed.</i> If it was a perfectly [smooth] knife without any blemishes, it is sufficient to insert it in hard earth ten times.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">One must insert it in ten different places in the earth. It is not sufficient to insert it in the same place ten times [<i>Tur</i> and <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 121:7)].</i> [Afterwards,] one may eat cold food with it.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">For sticking it into the earth will remove any traces of forbidden fat on its surface and the taste of forbidden food that is absorbed will not be released when it is used for cold food.</i> If it had blemishes or it was perfectly [smooth], but one desired to use it to eat hot food or to slaughter with it, he should expose it to fire until it becomes white hot or hone it in its entirety.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">These activities may cause any forbidden taste absorbed by the knife to be released. Hence before the knife is used, the traces of the forbidden flavor must be removed as above.<br>The Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 121:7) quotes opinions that maintain that honing the knife is not sufficient to allow it to be used for hot foods. He states that this is accustomed practice. Even so, after the fact, if a person slaughtered an animal with a knife that was honed in a grinder, thre is no prohibition involved (<i>Siftei Cohen</i> 121:20).</i> If he slaughtered [an animal] with such a knife before purifying it, he should wash thoroughly the place of slaughter.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">To remove any traces of forbidden fat that might be present.<br>This is permitted only after the fact. At the outset, it is forbidden to slaughter with such a knife unless measures are taken to remove the absorbed fat (<i>Siftei Cohen</i> 10:8).</i> If he removes the surface [of the meat around the place of slaughter], it is praiseworthy.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">For according to some opinions, through the slaughter of the animal, the forbidden fat on the knife can become absorbed in the surface of the meat where the animal was slaughtered. Hence it is necessary that it be removed. The Rama (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 10:1) rules that it is necessary to take this measure and remove the surface of the meat.</i>
When a knife was used to slaughter an animal that was <i>trefe</i>, one should not slaughter with it [again] until it is washed thoroughly, even with cold water or wiped clean with worn-out clothes.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">To remove any trace of forbidden blood or fat. Nothing more is necessary, we do not say that the blood or fat became absorbed in the knife.<br>The <i>Turei Zahav</i> 10:15 states that unlike a knife used by gentiles mentioned in the previous halachah, it was not used frequently with a non-kosher substance. Hence washing it thoroughly is sufficient.</i>
There are other substances which are forbidden by the Sages. Even though there is not a basis for their prohibition in Scriptural Law, they decreed against their use<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">These decrees were about the eighteen decrees passed when the students of the School of Shammai outnumbered the students of the School of Hillel, as related in <i>Shabbat</i> 1:3 (the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah, <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 2:6).</i> to separate from the gentiles so that Jews will not intermingle with them and intermarry. They are: It is forbidden to drink [alcoholic beverages] with them<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">See the following halachah.</i> even in a place where there was no suspicion that the wine was poured as a libation. And they forbade eating from their bread or cooked dishes<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachot 12-24.</i> even in a place where there is no suspicion that the food was forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., the food was cooked by gentiles on Jewish premises (the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah, <i>loc. cit.</i>).</i>
A person should not drink at a party of gentiles even though boiled wine which is not forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 11, Halachah 9.</i> [is being served] or he is drinking from his own utensils. If the majority of the attendants of the party are Jewish, it is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tur</i> and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> do not mention this restriction or the accompanying leniency. The <i>Beit Yosef</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 112) explains the Rambam's logic as follows: <i>Avodah Zarah </i>30a relates that one of the Sages, Shmuel was sitting with Abalat, a gentile. They were served boiled wine. Abalat withdrew, lest he touch the wine and cause it to become forbidden. Shmuel called him back, telling him there was no prohibition against boiled wine.<br>Rabbenu Asher asks: Since the prohibition against gentile wine was instituted as a protection against intermarriage, what difference does it make whether the wine is boiled or not? He answers that boiled wine is not common. Hence our Sages did not include it in their decree.<br>Rambam maintains that boiled wine is common and hence included in our Sages' decree. For this reason, it is forbidden to drink it together with gentiles. How then could Shmuel drink with Abalat? Because there were a majority of Jews at the gathering and such a situation is not included in our Sages' decree.</i> We may not drink the beer that they make from dates, figs, or the like. [This is forbidden] only in the place where they are sold.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Thus according to the Rambam [and the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 114:1) who quotes his ruling, it is forbidden to drink at a bar frequented primarily by gentiles. The Rama mentions that it is customary in the Ashkenazic community to rule leniently with regard to alcoholic beverages made from honey and grain.</i> If, however, one brought the beer home and drank it there, it is permitted. For the fundamental point of the decree is that one should not feast with [a gentile].
It is permitted to drink wine from apples, pomegranates, and the like in every place. [Our Sages] did not institute a decree in an uncommon situation. Raisen wine is like ordinary wine and is used for libations.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Hence a gentile's touch renders it forbidden.</i>
Although [our Sages] forbade bread [baked] by gentiles, there are places where leniency is shown regarding this matter and bread baked by a gentile baker is purchased in a place where there is no Jewish baker and it is in a field, because this is a pressing situation.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Because bread is a staple of life and there is no Jewish bread available, our Sages allowed for leniency when purchasing bread from a commercial baker. For buying from him will not lead to close personal relationships. Nevertheless, according to the Rambam, this leniency is granted only: where there is no Jewish bakers and in the fields, not in the cities. The <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 112:2) rules more leniently and does not forbid this in a city. The Rama rules even more leniently and allows the purchase of bread from a gentile baker even in places where bread from a Jewish baker is available.</i> There is, by contrast, no one who will rule that leniency may be shown with regard to bread baked by a homeowner.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">There are opinions which maintain when there is no bread from a commercial baker available, one may even use bread baked by a gentile homeowner [<i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 112)]. The Rama states that one may accept this leniency.</i> For the primary reason for [our Sages'] decree was [to prevent] intermarriage. If one will eat the bread of a [gentile] homeowner, [it is likely that] he will feast with him.
[The bread] is permitted [in the following situations]: A gentile lit the oven and a Jew baked within it, a Jew lit the oven and the gentile baked within it, the gentile both lit the oven and baked, but the Jew stirred the fire or reduced it, since he was involved in the baking tasks, [we rule leniently]. Even though he did not do more than throw one piece of wood into the oven, he caused all the bread in it to be permitted. [The rationale is that this requirement] is only to make a distinction that [a gentile's] bread is forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The Radbaz states that this leniency applies only with regard to baking bread. With regard to cooking, a Jew must take a more active role in the cooking process. This ruling is quoted by the <i>Shulchan Aruch</i> (<i>Yoreh De'ah</i> 113:7). The Rama, however, differs and maintains that kindling the oven is sufficient for cooking as well.</i>
When a gentile cooks wine, milk, honey, quince,<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">There is a slight difficulty with the Rambam's statements, because quince are only edible when cooked.</i> or the like, i.e., any entity that is usually eaten raw, it is permitted. [Our Sages] issued their decree only with regard to entities that are not eaten at all raw, e.g., meat, unsalted fish, an egg, and vegetables. If a gentile were to cook them from the beginning to the end without the Jew participating in the cooking at all, they are forbidden because they were cooked by gentiles.
When does the above apply? To [food] that would be served on the table of kings<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Today, when monarchy is a point of history, the phrase "fit to be served on the table of kings" refers to food served at a dinner for the President or dignitaries of similar status.</i> to be eaten together with bread,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Avodah Zarah</i> 38a gives this and the leniency mentioned in the previous halachah as alternate explanations when food cooked by gentiles is permitted.
